Grave Robbing and the Hadd Dispute
Ruling on grave robbery and intra-madhhab dispute with Abu Yusuf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on hadd for grave robbery and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The grave robber is not subject to hadd because the shroud is not protected property.

Grave robbery (nabsh) appears similar to theft, but the Hanafi school distinguishes it due to the nature of the grave and the shroud. This lesson examines the ruling and the disagreement within the Hanafi school and with other imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling on nabsh
State the default Hanafi position: no hadd (amputation) for grave robbery.
The ruling is based on a specific hadith and the concept of shubhah.
2
Examine the dalil: the hadith of al-mukhtafi
Locate the hadith: 'La qat'a 'ala al-mukhtafi' which refers to the nabbash in Madinese dialect. Understand that this is the primary proof.
The hadith is ahad, providing zanni thubut but qati dalala.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues for amputation, citing the statement of 'Ali: 'Whoever graves, we cut him.' This is also the view of the other three Sunni imams.
This appears to be a strong counter-evidence.
4
Study the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: the statement is not marfu' (from the Prophet) but from Ziyad, and it includes extreme punishments indicating it is a policy (siyasah) measure, not a fixed hadd.
The rebuttal also notes the text includes 'whoever kills his slave we kill him' which is not established as fiqh.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi school does not amputate the hand; Abu Yusuf and other schools do. The Hanafi school allows ta'zir at the ruler's discretion.
This difference matters in actual sentencing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونبش أي ولا قطع بسبب نبش القبور، والنبش استخراج الشيء المدفون، ومنه النباش الذي يستخرج الكفن من القبور، وعدم القطع فيه لقوله عليه: "لا قطع على المختفي" وهو النباش بلغة أهل المدينة، وقال أبو يوسف: يقطع؛ لقوله علي: "من نبش قطعناه ، وبه قالت الثلاثة قلنا: هذا غير مرفوع بل هو من كلام زياد، وذكر في آخره: "من قتل عبده قتلناه ومن جدع عبده جدعناه، ولا يكاد يثبت هذا أبداً، ولئن ثبت فهو محمول على السياسة فيمن اعتاد ذلك، ونحن نقول بذلك إذا رأى الإمام فيه مصلحة.
And grave robbing: i.e., no cut due to grave robbing. Nabsh is extracting something buried, and from it is the nabbash who extracts the shroud from graves. Non-application of cut is due to his statement (peace be upon him): "No cut on al-mukhtafi" which is the nabbash in the language of the people of Madinah. Abu Yusuf said: amputation is applied due to the statement of 'Ali: "Whoever graves, we will cut him." And the three (other imams) said the same. We replied: this is not marfu' but is the speech of Ziyad, and at its end it says: "Whoever kills his slave, we will kill him; and whoever mutilates his slave, we will mutilate him." This is hardly ever established. And even if established, it is carried on policy (siyasah) for those who habitually do it. And we say that if the Imam sees a benefit, he may do that.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage discusses grave robbing (nabsh). The Hanafi position is that no hadd is applied. The proof is a hadith: "There is no cutting on al-mukhtafi" which means the nabbash in Madinese dialect. Abu Yusuf differs and says amputation is applied, citing a statement attributed to 'Ali: "Whoever graves, we will cut him." This view is also held by the other three imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad). The Hanafi response is that this statement is not marfu' (attributed to the Prophet) but rather from Ziyad (a companion?), and it includes extreme punishments like executing for killing a slave, which indicates it is a matter of state policy (siyasah), not a fixed hadd. The Hanafi school accepts that if the ruler sees a public interest, he may impose a discretionary punishment (ta'zir), but the fixed hadd of the hand is not applied due to shubhah.

Key Terms
نبش (nabsh): Grave robbing: digging up a grave to take the shroud etc.
النباش (al-nabbash): A person who robs graves.
المختفي (al-mukhtafi): The one who takes secretly; in Madinese dialect, the nabbash.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi school's main evidence for not applying the hadd to grave robbers?
A) The statement of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib.
B) The Quranic verse on amputation.
C) The hadith: 'No cut on al-mukhtafi'.
D) Consensus of the companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not apply the hadd amputation to grave robbers because the grave is not considered a guarded place and the hadith explicitly excludes them.

No Hadd for Theft of Dogs, Leopards, and Musical Instruments
List of items exempt from hadd theft penalty with expanded commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific items for which the Hanafi school does not apply the hadd of amputation for theft, explain the role of shubha in dropping hadd, and enumerate the eight general categories of items exempt from hadd.

Core Idea
Hadd penalties are dropped when a legal doubt (shubha) exists, even if the item has potential value.

The hadd penalty for theft is one of the most severe punishments in Islamic law, but it is not applied in certain circumstances. The Hanafi doctrine holds that any legal doubt (shubha) regarding the protected status of the stolen property prevents the imposition of hadd. This lesson examines a passage listing several items—dogs, leopards, and musical instruments—whose theft does not incur amputation. The commentary explains the reasoning and then expands into a comprehensive list of eight types of property that are exempt from hadd.

Guided Path
1
List the specific items
Identify the items mentioned: dog, leopard, duff (frame drum), drum of amusement, barbat (lute), flute.
These are all explicitly named in the matn.
2
Understand the primary rationale
The text gives two reasons: (a) these items are originally mubah (permissible to own) and have no protected value; (b) scholars differed on their value, creating a shubha that drops hadd.
Shubha is a key Hanafi principle in hudud.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference
Note that Abu Hanifa had a nuanced view about the intention to break, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad held that no cutting applies at all. The relied-upon position is no cutting.
The matn follows the view of the two disciples, but the commentary notes the difference.
4
Extract the eight general categories
The commentary lists eight types of items for which hadd is not applied. Identify each: contemptible items, mubah al-asl, perishable, unsecured, items whose destruction is permitted (intoxicants, musical instruments), items that can be interpreted as not theft (mushaf), non-property (free child), and taking that is not theft (adult slave).
This list generalizes the specific rulings.
5
Apply the principle
Given a new scenario (e.g., theft of a hunting dog or a drum used in warfare), determine whether hadd applies based on the presence of shubha.
Use the eight categories as a checklist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلب، وفهد، ودف، وطبل لهو، وبربط ومزمار

وكلب وفهد: [ أي ولا يقطع في كلب؛ لأنه مباح الأصل ولو كلب صيد أو ماشية، ولا يقطع في فهد ولو عليه طوق ذهب؛ لأنه مثل الكلب. ( عيني، ط] عطف على ما ذكر من الأشياء التي لا يقطع فيها، أي لا قطع في سرقة كلب وفهد ولا في غيرهما من السباع؛ لأن جنسها يوجد مباحا غير مرغوب فيها؛ ولأن العلماء قد اختلفوا في مالية السباع، فكان الاختلاف فيه شبهة، والشبهة مسقط للحد. (مستخلص، عيني) ودف: [ أي ولا يقطع في دف ولو مربعاً أو مدوراً. (عيني)] كان ينبغي أن يذكر هذه الأشياء عند قوله: "طنبور" أي لا قطع في سرقة هذه الأشياء عندهما؛ لأنه لا قيمة لها، وعند أبي حنيفة وإن كان لها قيمة لكن آخذها يتأول الكسر، وهذا في طبل اللهو ظاهر، واختلف في طبل الغزاة، والأصح عدم القطع؛ لتمكن الشبهة؛ لكونه صالحاً للغزو واللهو، والحاصل أن عدم القطع يكون بوجوه: الأول: أن يكون المسروق شيئاً حقيراً مثل الخشب والحشيش، والثاني: أن يكون مباح الأصل كالسمك والطير ونحوهما، والثالث: أن يكون مما يتسارع إليه الفساد، ولا يبقى حولاً كالفاكهة الرطبة والمهيأ للأكل، والرابع: أن يكون غير محرز كالزرع الغير المحصود، والخامس: أن يكون مباح الإضاعة من الشرع كالأشربة وآلات المناهي، والسادس: أن يكون متأولا لغير السرقة كالمصحف، والسابع: أن لا يكون مالاً كالصبي الحر، والثامن: أن يكون الأخذ غير سرقة كالعبد الكبير. (مستخلص، محشي)
And a dog, and a leopard, and a duff, and a drum of amusement, and a barbat, and a flute. And a dog and a leopard: [That is, cutting is not applied for a dog because it is originally permissible (mubah), even if it is a hunting dog or a watchdog; and cutting is not applied for a leopard even if it has a golden collar because it is like a dog. (Ayni, T) It is an appendage to what was mentioned of things for which cutting is not applied, i.e., no cutting in the theft of a dog or a leopard nor in other beasts of prey because their genus is found as mubah and not desired; and because the scholars differed regarding the value of beasts of prey, so the difference (ikhtilaf) therein is a shubha, and shubha drops the hadd. (Mukhtasar, Ayni) And a duff: [That is, cutting is not applied for a duff even if square or round. (Ayni)] It would have been appropriate to mention these things at his statement 'tunbur': i.e., no cutting in the theft of these things according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because they have no value; and according to Abu Hanifa, even if they have value, the taker pretends to break it, and this is apparent in the drum of amusement. There is disagreement regarding the drum of warriors, and the correct view is no cutting due to the presence of shubha because it is suitable for both warfare and amusement. The summary is that non-cutting occurs in eight ways: First: that the stolen item be a contemptible thing like wood and grass; Second: that it be mubah al-asl like fish, birds, and the like; Third: that it be something that quickly spoils and does not last a year like fresh fruit and food prepared for eating; Fourth: that it be unsecured like unharvested crops; Fifth: that it be something whose destruction is permitted by Shar‘ like intoxicants and instruments of prohibitions; Sixth: that it be something interpreted as other than theft like the mushaf; Seventh: that it be not property like a free child; Eighth: that the taking be not theft like an adult slave. (Mukhtasar, Muhashshi)
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof stating that the hadd penalty for theft (amputation of the hand) is not applied for stealing certain items. The author lists dogs, leopards, and various musical instruments (duff, drum, barbat, flute). The commentary explains that dogs are originally mubah (permissible) and scholars differed on their value, creating a shubha (doubt/ambiguity) that drops the hadd. Similarly for leopards. For musical instruments, the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider them valueless, while Abu Hanifa says they have value but the taker claims to break them. The commentary then presents a comprehensive list of eight categories of items for which cutting is not applied, including contemptible items, mubah al-asl items, perishable items, unsecured items, items whose destruction is permitted (like wine), items that can be interpreted as not theft (like a Quran), items that are not property (free child), and taking that is not theft (like an adult slave). This summary is derived from the specific rulings.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of a hadd penalty.
مباح الأصل (mubah al-asl): Items that are originally permissible to own and use, not inherently prohibited.
مالية السباع (maliyyat al-siba'): The monetary value of predatory animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hadd of cutting not applied for theft of a dog according to the passage?
A) Because dogs are najis (impure).
B) Because dogs are originally mubah (permissible) and scholars differed on their value.
C) Because the thief cannot be caught.
D) Because the dog is not properly guarded.

Key Takeaway
Hadd penalties for theft are not applied when the stolen property falls into categories that create a legal doubt (shubha), such as items that are originally mubah, have disputed value, or are considered valueless or unsecured.

No Hadd for Breach of Trust, Plunder, Snatching, and Grave Robbing
Ruling that hadd is not applied for these four acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four acts that do not incur the hadd penalty and explain the Hanafi proof for each, including the response to Abu Yusuf's view on grave robbing.

Core Idea
Theft requires secrecy; open taking and breach of trust are not theft, so no hand is cut.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies that not all unlawful takings are subject to the hadd penalty. The author enumerates four acts—khiyanah (breach of trust), nahb (plunder), ikhtilas (snatching), and nashb (grave robbing)—and states that the hand is not cut for any of them. The primary proof is a prophetic hadith, and the reasoning hinges on the absence of the secrecy condition required for theft. The grave robbing case involves an intra-Hanafi disagreement and cross-madhhab dispute, which the commentary resolves by rejecting the opposing proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four acts and the common ruling
Read the passage and note the four acts: khiyanah, nahb, ikhtilas, and nashb. The ruling for all is that the hand is not cut (no hadd).
The ruling is consistent across all four despite differing circumstances.
2
Examine the proof for the first three
The proof is the hadith: 'There is no cutting for a betrayer, plunderer, or snatcher.' This hadith is specifically narrated for these categories.
The hadith is reported by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and others, and was deemed sound by al-Tirmidhi.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the ruling
Hadd for theft requires taking secretly from a secure place (hirz). In khiyanah, the property is already in the possessor's custody, so no breach of hirz. In nahb and ikhtilas, the taking is open or sudden, lacking secrecy.
The absence of secrecy (takhfiya) is the key legal condition that distinguishes these acts from sariqa.
4
Focus on grave robbing and its proof
For grave robbing, the Hanafi proof is another hadith: 'No cutting for the mukhtafi' (the graverobber). This hadith directly exempts the graverobber from hadd.
The term 'mukhtafi' is explained as the graverobber, based on the dialect of Madinah.
5
Analyze the opposing view and Hanafi response
Abu Yusuf and the other three schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) cite a saying attributed to Ali (ra): 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut his hand.' The Hanafi response: this saying is not from the Prophet (saw) but from Ziyad ibn Abihi, and even if authentic, it is for political discretion (siyasa), not a fixed hadd. Therefore, the relied-upon position is no cutting, though the imam may impose ta'zir if he sees public interest.
The commentary (Ayni, Fath) upholds the no-cutting position and weakens Abu Yusuf's proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبخيانة، ونهب، واختلاس، [وهو العود هو النائي؟] ونبش

وبخيانة إلخ : أي ولا يقطع بخيانة المودع ما في يده من الشيء المأمون، ولا قطع بنهب: وهو الأخذ علانية على وجه القهر، ولا قطع بالاختلاس وهو أن يختطف الشيء من يد المالك أو من البيت؛ لقوله عليه: "ليس على خائن ولا منتهب ولا مختلس قطع"، رواه أحمد وأبو داود وغيرهما، وصححه الترمذي؛ ولأن في الخيانة قصور في الحرز وفي النهب لعدم الشرط وهو الأخذ خفية، وكذا في الاختلاس. (عيني، فتح) ونبش أي ولا قطع بسبب نبش القبور، والنبش استخراج الشيء المدفون، ومنه النباش الذي يستخرج الكفن من القبور، وعدم القطع فيه لقوله عليه: "لا قطع على المختفي" وهو النباش بلغة أهل المدينة، وقال أبو يوسف: يقطع؛ لقوله علي: "من نبش قطعناه"، وبه قالت الثلاثة قلنا: هذا غير مرفوع بل هو من كلام زياد، وذكر في آخره: "من قتل عبده قتلناه ومن جدع عبده جدعناه، ولا يكاد يثبت هذا أبداً، ولئن ثبت فهو محمول على السياسة فيمن اعتاد ذلك، ونحن نقول بذلك إذا رأى الإمام فيه مصلحة. (عيني، مسكين)
And with breach of trust, and plunder, and snatching, [and it is the lute [?]?] and grave robbing. And with breach of trust etc.: i.e., cutting is not applied for betrayal of a depositary regarding what is in his hand of the entrusted thing; and no cutting for plunder: which is taking openly by force; and no cutting for snatching, which is to snatch the thing from the hand of the owner or from the house; because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'There is no cutting for a betrayer, nor for a plunderer, nor for a snatcher.' Reported by Ahmad, Abu Dawud and others, and declared sound by al-Tirmidhi. And because in betrayal there is deficiency in the secure place (hirz), and in plunder the condition (secrecy) is absent, and similarly in snatching. (Ayni, Fath) And grave robbing: i.e., no cutting due to robbing graves. Grave robbing is extracting a buried thing, and from it is the graverobber who extracts the shroud from graves. The absence of cutting is due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'No cutting for the mukhtafi' which is the graverobber in the language of the people of Madinah. Abu Yusuf said: Cutting is applied because of the saying of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut his hand.' And the three (other imams) said likewise. We say: This (saying) is not raised (to the Prophet) but is the speech of Ziyad, and at the end it mentions: 'Whoever kills his slave, we kill him; and whoever mutilates his slave, we mutilate him.' This can never be established. Even if established, it is interpreted as a political measure for the habitual offender, and we accept that if the imam sees a public interest. (Ayni, Miskin)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof enumerating four types of taking that do not incur the hadd: khiyanah (breach of trust by a depositary), nahb (open plunder), ikhtilas (snatching), and nashb (grave robbing). The proof for the first three is the hadith: 'There is no cutting for a betrayer, plunderer, or snatcher.' The reasoning is that theft requires taking secretly from a secure place (hirz); these acts lack that condition. For grave robbing, the text provides a hadith: 'No cutting for the mukhtafi' (the graverobber). However, Abu Yusuf and the three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that cutting is applied based on a saying attributed to Ali (ra). The Hanafi response is that the saying is not from the Prophet (peace be upon him) but from Ziyad ibn Abihi, and even if authentic, it is for political discretion, not a fixed hadd. The Hanafi position is that no cutting occurs, though the imam may punish severely if he deems it in the public interest.

Key Terms
خيانة (khiyanah): Breach of trust by a depositary who takes without permission.
نهب (nahb): Openly taking property by force, i.e., plunder.
اختلاس (ikhtilas): Snatching something quickly from an owner's hand or from a house without force.
نبش (nashb): Grave robbing, i.e., exhumation of a corpse to steal the shroud.
المختفي (al-mukhtafi): The graverobber, as referred to in the hadith 'لا قطع على المختفي'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on grave robbing (nashb) regarding the hadd penalty?
A) The hand is cut because it is theft.
B) The hand is not cut because a special hadith exempts the graverobber.
C) The hand is cut only if the shroud is valuable.
D) The hand is not cut, but Abu Yusuf disagrees.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school reserves the hadd penalty exclusively for genuine theft (sariqa), which requires secrecy and breach of a secure place; all other forms of unlawful taking are subject to lesser penalties.

No Hadd for Theft of Amount Equal to Debt
Ruling on theft of amount equal to debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a creditor who steals from his debtor an amount equal to his debt, explain the role of istihsan, and distinguish between scenarios where hadd is dropped or applied.

Core Idea
A creditor who steals his exact debt is not cut – it's fulfillment, not theft.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling in Hanafi criminal law: when a creditor takes his due from his debtor without consent, does the hadd of cutting apply? The answer depends on whether the act is considered istifa' (fulfillment) or theft. The Hanafi school uses istihsan to treat immediate and deferred debts equally, but different rulings apply for different types of property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the core ruling
The act is a creditor stealing an amount equal to his debt from the debtor's property. The ruling: no cutting (hadd dropped). Understand why it is seen as istifa'.
2
Understand the proof: istihsan over qiyas
By qiyas, a deferred debt would lead to cutting, but istihsan equates immediate and deferred debts because deferment is only a delay in demand. This is the key legal reasoning.
Istihsan is a Hanafi method that gives preference to a stronger consideration.
3
Analyze the scenarios of same type vs. different type
If the stolen property is of the same type as the debt (e.g., both cash), no cutting. If it is a different type (e.g., debt in cash but stolen goods), then if it is merchandise, cutting applies because it is a substitution, not fulfillment.
The two currencies (gold and silver) are treated as one type, so no cutting.
4
Consider excess and partnership
If the creditor steals more than his debt, no cutting because to the extent of his right he becomes a partner, creating shubha (doubt) that blocks the hadd.
Shubha is a well-established mitigator in hadd punishments.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dissent
Abu Yusuf reportedly holds that no cutting applies in any case, even for merchandise. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that cutting applies for merchandise.
This shows that even within a school, ikhtilaf exists and tarjih is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثل دينه

ومثل دينه [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في السرقة من مديونه مثل دينه من جنسه والدين حال؛ لأنه استيفاء لدينه. (عيني)] أي ولا قطع على من سرق مثل دينه من مال مديونه؛ لأنه استوفى حقه، والحال والمؤجل في هذا سواء استحساناً؛ لأن التأجيل: التأخير في المطالبة، ويقطع قياساً فيما إذا كان مؤجلاً، وإذا سرق زيادة على حقه لا يقطع؛ لأنه بمقدار حقه يصير شريكاً فيه، فيصير شبهة، وإن سرق خلاف سرق من جنس حقه، فإن كان نقداً لا يقطع في الصحيح؛ لأن النقدين جنس واحد حكماً، وإن كان عروضاً يقطع؛ لأنه ليس باستيفاء، وإنما هو استبدال، إلا بالتراضي، وعن أبي يوسف لا يقطع. (مستخلص، عيني)
And like his debt. And like his debt [i.e., similarly, cutting is not applied in theft from his debtor of an amount like his debt of the same type, and the debt is due (immediate), because it is the fulfillment of his debt. (Ayni)] i.e., no cutting upon one who steals an amount like his debt from the property of his debtor because he has taken his right. The immediate and deferred are equal in this by istihsan, because deferment is merely a delay in demand. By qiyas, cutting would apply if it were deferred. If he steals an excess over his right, no cutting because to the extent of his right he becomes a partner in it, creating shubha. If he steals something different from the type of his right: if it is currency, no cutting in the correct view because the two currencies are effectively one type. If it is merchandise, cutting is applied because it is not taking the right but a substitution, unless by mutual consent. It is reported from Abu Yusuf that there is no cutting. (Mukhtasar, Ayni)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof regarding theft by a creditor from his debtor. The matn states: no cutting if he steals an amount equal to his debt and of the same type. The commentary explains that this is considered istifa' (fulfillment) of his right. The ruling applies whether the debt is immediate or deferred by istihsan; by qiyas, a deferred debt would lead to cutting, but istihsan prevails. If the stolen amount exceeds the debt, still no cutting because the portion equal to the debt creates shubha. If he steals a different type (e.g., his debt is currency but he steals goods), then if it is currency (any currency), no cutting because all currencies are considered one type; if it is merchandise, cutting applies because it is a substitution, not fulfillment. Abu Yusuf reportedly says no cutting in all cases.

Key Terms
دين (dayn): A debt owed by the debtor to the creditor.
استيفاء (istifa'): Fulfillment/taking of one's right.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference overriding qiyas.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt that blocks the application of hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a creditor steals an amount equal to his immediate debt from his debtor, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The hand is cut because it is theft.
B) The hand is not cut because it is considered fulfillment of his right.
C) The hand is cut only if the debt is deferred.
D) The hand is cut if the stolen amount exceeds the debt.

Key Takeaway
A creditor who steals exactly his debt of the same type is not subject to hadd because the act is istifa', but if he steals merchandise of a different type, cutting applies.

Second Theft of Unchanged Item: No Hadd
Inter-madhhab dispute on second theft after hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not apply hadd for a second theft of the same unchanged item and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
First cutting removes inviolability permanently for that thief, creating a shubha that prevents a second hadd unless the item changes.

In this lesson, we examine a key point of difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding repeated theft. The case arises when a thief steals an item, receives the hadd punishment, returns the item, and then steals it again while it remains unchanged. The Hanafi school rules that no second cutting applies, relying on the concept of 'ismat al-mahal' (inviolability of the property) and the shubha (doubt) that remains. The Shafi'i school, following qiyas, argues that the second theft is a fresh crime warranting cutting. We will explore the proofs on both sides and understand the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed case
Understand the scenario: a thief steals an item, receives the hadd cutting, returns the item, and then steals the same item again while it remains unchanged. The dispute is whether the second theft incurs cutting.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
Hanafis rule no cutting. Their basis: the first cutting removed the inviolability (ismah) of that property from the thief. Even after return, a shubha (doubt) remains, and hadd is averted by shubha.
Key concept: 'ismat al-mahal' is not fully restored.
3
Present the Shafi'i/Abu Yusuf argument
Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that cutting applies. Their proof: Prophet's hadith 'If he returns, cut him' without exception, and qiyas: the second theft is a complete crime like the first, even worse due to the deterrent.
This is the qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis argue that the first cutting permanently removed the inviolability for that thief in relation to that item. The return to the owner does not fully restore it; a shubha remains. However, if the item undergoes a substantial change (e.g., yarn to cloth), the shubha disappears and cutting applies again.
The change of item removes the shubha, so the ruling flips.
5
Determine the practical consequence
In practice, the Hanafi mufta bihi position is no cutting for second theft of unchanged item. The difference in rulings (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the thief's hand is cut again or not.
Abu Yusuf's view is considered marjuh (weak) within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشيء قطع فيه ولم يتغير

ولم يتغير : [ جملة حالية أي لم يتبدل ذلك الشيء المسروق عن حالته الأولى. (عيني)] أي لا قطع بسرقة شيء قد قطع فيه مرة و لم يتغير هذا الشيء كما إذا سرق عينا فقطع فيها وهي قائمة فردها إلى مالكها ثم سرقها وهي بحالها، لم يقطع، وعن أبي يوسف يقطع، وهو قول الشافعي، وهو القياس؛ لقوله عليه: "فإن عاد فاقطعوه" من غير فصل، ولأن الجناية الثانية متكاملة كالأولى، بل هي أقبح لتقدم الزاجر ولنا أن القطع أوجب سقوط عصمة المحل في حقه، فبالرد إلى المالك وإن عادت حقيقة العصمة لكن بقيت شبهة السقوط والحد يندرج بها، فإن تغير العين عن حالها مثل إن كان غزلاً فسرقه فقطع فيه، ثم رده إلى صاحبه فنسجه أو نحو ذلك ثم سرقه، يقطع فيه ثانياً؛ لأن العين قد تبدلت، ولهذا يملك الغاصب بمثل هذا التغير، فإذا تبدلت انتفت الشبهة الناشئة عن اتحاد المحل فيقطع ثانياً. (عيني، فتح)
And with a thing for which cutting has been applied and it has not changed. And it has not changed: [a circumstantial clause meaning that stolen thing has not changed from its original state. (Ayni)] i.e., no cutting for stealing a thing for which cutting has been applied once and this thing has not changed, such as if he stole an eye (a valuable item) and cutting was applied while it was still in existence, then he returned it to its owner, then stole it while it was in its same state; he is not cut. From Abu Yusuf: he is cut, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i, and it is the qiyas because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'If he returns (to stealing), then cut him' without exception, and because the second crime is complete like the first, indeed worse due to the prior deterrent. And we (Hanafis) argue that the cutting necessitated the removal of the inviolability (ismah) of the property in his regard; so by returning to the owner, even if the actual inviolability returns, the shubha of its removal remains, and the hadd is averted by that. If the item changes from its state, such as if it was yarn and he stole it and cutting was applied, then he returned it to its owner who wove it, or something like that, then he stole it, he is cut again because the item has changed; for this reason, a usurper (ghasib) acquires ownership through such change. So when it changes, the shubha arising from the unity of the item is removed, so he is cut again. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an inter_madhhab_dispute (Hanafi vs Shafi'i) regarding second theft of the same item after hadd has been applied. The matn states: no cutting if the item has not changed. The commentary provides the Hanafi position: the first cutting removed the 'ismah (inviolability) of that property with respect to that thief; even though returning it to the owner restores its actual inviolability, a shadow of doubt (shubha) remains, which drops the hadd. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i argue by qiyas: the Prophet said, 'If he returns, cut him,' without exception, and the second theft is an independent crime. The Hanafi response is that the inviolability was once removed, and the doubt persists. However, if the item changes (e.g., yarn becomes cloth), then the shubha disappears and cutting applies a second time.

Key Terms
عصمة المحل (ismat al-mahal): The inviolability or protection of the property with respect to that person.
تغير العين (taghayyur al-'ayn): A substantial change in the property that alters its identity (e.g., yarn woven into cloth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The hand is cut a second time.
B) The hand is not cut a second time.
C) The hand is cut only if the coin has been melted.
D) The hand is not cut, but Abu Yusuf disagrees.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a second theft of the same unchanged item does not incur hadd because the first cutting permanently removed its inviolability for that thief, creating a persistent shubha.

Valuable Commodities Subject to Hadd Theft Penalty
Listing valuable items subject to hadd cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which valuable items trigger the hadd cutting penalty and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding pearls.

Core Idea
Cutting applies to any valuable, guarded property not found as mubah in Dar al-Islam.

The hadd penalty for theft (cutting of the hand) applies only when the stolen item meets specific criteria: it must be valuable, properly guarded (hirz), and not ownerless (mubah) in Dar al-Islam. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq lists several commodities—teak, ebony, sandalwood, precious stones, and pearls—that satisfy these conditions. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding pearls and similar gems, which we will examine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rulings
The passage states cutting is applied for theft of teak, spearwood, ebony, sandalwood, green gems, ruby, chrysolite, and pearl.
2
Understand the underlying rationale
These items are considered valuable, well-guarded property not found as mubah in Dar al-Islam, likened to gold and silver.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on pearls
A narration from Muhammad (via Hisham) says no cutting for pearls because their origin is mubah (from the sea), but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) say cutting applies because pearls are valuable and not found as mubah in their final form.
4
Note the relied-upon position (mufta bihi)
The later authorities (Ayni, Fath) prefer the view of the two disciples, so cutting applies for pearls.
5
Extend to other items mentioned in the commentary
The commentary adds rulings on ivory (cutting only if worked), glass (no cutting), and aromatic substances (cutting applies).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الساج [ الساج بالجيم شجر عظيم جداً، ولا ينبت إلا في الهند ويحمل منها إلى سائر البلاد. (عيني)] والقناة خشب الرماح، والآبنوس بفتح الباء هرباً من اجتماع الساكنين والصندل خشب أحمر وأصفر طيب الرائحة، ويقطع فيها؛ لأنها أموال محرزة عزيزة عند الناس ولا توجد مباحة في دار الإسلام، وقوله: "والفصوص الخضر" التقييد بالخضر اتفاقي والمراد منها مثل الزمرد والفيروزج وغيرهما، والياقوت أحمر وأصفر وأخضر، وأعزها الأحمر، والزبرجد – بفتح المعجمة والموحدة - حجر أصفر ينفع من الصرع، ويقطع في هذه الأشياء؛ لأنها من أعز الأموال وأنفسها، فصارت كالذهب والفضة، ولا يوجد مباح الأصل في دار الإسلام، وفي "الاختيار": لا قطع في العاج ما لم يعمل، فإذا عمل منه شيء قطع فيه ولا قطع في الزجاج مطلقاً، وقيل: في المصنوع يقطع، ويقطع في العود والمسك والادهان والورس والزعفران والعنبر ؛ لكونها من أعز الأموال. And cutting is applied for theft of teak, and spearwood, and ebony, and sandalwood, and green gems, and ruby, and chrysolite, and pearl. Teak: [teak is an enormous tree that grows only in India and is carried from there to other countries. (Ayni)] And spearwood is the wood of spears; ebony (with fatha on ba' to avoid two sukoons); sandalwood is red and yellow wood with good fragrance. Cutting is applied in these because they are well-guarded, valuable properties among people, and are not found as mubah in Dar al-Islam. And his statement: 'and green gems' the restriction to green is incidental; what is meant is emerald, turquoise, and others. Ruby is red, yellow, green; the most valuable is red. Chrysolite (with fatha on the mu'jama and muwahhada) is a yellow stone beneficial for epilepsy. In 'al-Ikhtiyar': No cutting in ivory unless it is worked; if something is made from it, cutting applies. And no cutting in glass absolutely; some say in manufactured items cutting applies. (Ayni, Fath)

There is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding pearl: a narration from Muhammad (via Hisham) says no cutting because pearl's origin is mubah (from sea), but the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say cutting applies because pearls are precious and not found as mubah in their final form. The relied-upon position is that cutting applies for these items.

Definition of al-Hirz (Secure Place) for Hadd al-Sariqa
Defines the term al-Hirz linguistically and legally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and legal definition of al-hirz and apply it to identify whether a place qualifies as a hirz for the purpose of hadd al-sariqa.

Core Idea
A hirz is anywhere a reasonable person stores valuables — custom, not locks, defines security.

In Hanafi law, the hadd penalty for theft applies only when property is taken from a secure place (hirz). But what counts as 'secure'? This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq defines al-hirz both linguistically and legally, shifting from the root meaning of 'collection' to the legal standard of customary preservation. Understanding this definition is the first step in applying the law of theft correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root
The Arabic root h-r-z means to collect and preserve. The passage cites expressions like 'the imam collected the spoils' and 'the protected place' to show this core meaning.
The linguistic origin helps anchor the legal concept.
2
Distinguish linguistic vs. legal meaning
Linguistically, hirz is any collection or preservation. Legally, it is defined by custom ('urf): a place where property is customarily preserved, such that the owner is not considered negligent.
The shift from 'any preservation' to 'customary preservation' is key.
3
Identify the examples given
The passage lists: house, shop, tent, and also a person himself (for property in his direct possession). These are paradigmatic cases.
Notice that a person is considered a hirz for items on his person.
4
Extract the legal criterion
The criterion is custom ('urf) and negligence. If the owner stores property in a manner that is not considered negligent by custom, the place is a hirz.
This makes the definition flexible and context-dependent.
5
Apply the definition to new scenarios
Test whether a given location qualifies as hirz by asking: 'Is this a place where people customarily store this type of property? Would the owner be considered negligent for leaving it there?'
Practice with examples like a pocket (yes), a mosque (no), a locked car (depends on custom).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحرز الحرز في اللغة: الجمع، ومنه قولهم: "العصبة عند الانفراد يحرز جميع المال"، وقولهم: "أحرز الإمام الغنيمة"، وأيضاً الموضع الحريز"، وهو الموضع الذي يحرز فيه الشيء أي يحفظ وفي الشرع: ما يحفظ فيه المال عادة كالدار والحانوت والخيمة أو الشخص بنفسه، والمراد من الحرز ما لا يعد صاحبه مضيعاً.
Chapter on al-Hirz: Hirz linguistically means collection, from which is their saying 'the male [؟] alone collects all the wealth', and their saying 'the imam collected the spoils', and also 'the protected place', which is the place where a thing is deposited, i.e., preserved. And legally: that in which property is customarily preserved, such as a house, shop, tent, or a person himself. And the intended meaning of hirz is that which makes its owner not considered negligent.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of al-Hirz (secure place) central to the law of theft (sariqa). In Hanafi fiqh, the amputation of the hand for theft requires that the stolen item was taken from a proper hirz. Linguistically, the root h-r-z conveys collecting and preserving. Legally, the Hanafi scholars define hirz as any location or situation where property is customarily kept safe, such as a house, shop, or tent. Importantly, a person himself can serve as a hirz for property in his direct possession, e.g., a wallet in his pocket. The key principle: if the owner stores his property in a manner that is not considered negligent by custom, then it qualifies as hirz. If the owner was negligent (e.g., leaving valuables unattended in a public place), then the place is not a proper hirz, and the thief is not subject to amputation. This definition excludes places like mosques or public baths where entry is generally permitted, as they do not provide the required level of protection.

Key Terms
الحرز (al-hirz): Any place where property is customarily preserved, such that the owner is not considered negligent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) An open field where a farmer leaves his tools
B) A person's pocket containing his wallet
C) A mosque where someone leaves a bag unattended
D) A public park bench

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of hirz is based on custom ('urf), not physical locks: a place is hirz if the owner is not considered negligent in storing property there.

Theft Between Spouses: Hanafi Position and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Ruling on theft between spouses with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules no amputation for theft between spouses, and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i/Maliki/Hanbali position by identifying the key proof and rebuttal.

Core Idea
Marriage creates mutual access to property (mabsuta), generating a doubt that repels the hadd punishment.

Theft between spouses raises a unique question: does the general law of amputation apply, or does the marital relationship create a special exemption? This lesson examines the Hanafi position of no amputation based on the concept of mutual access (al-mabsuta) and the opposing views of other schools. By analyzing the proofs, you will understand how Hanafi scholars prioritize custom and contractual implications over the absolute text of the Quranic verse on theft.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the ruling: no amputation for either spouse if one steals from the other's property. Also note the extension: if marriage occurs after the theft but before amputation, the hadd is dropped.
This applies to both husband and wife without differentiation.
2
Identify the Opponent and Their View
The Shafi'i school (in one opinion), and the Maliki and Hanbali schools generally hold that amputation applies, as the general texts on theft (Quran 5:38) are absolute and not restricted by marriage.
Some Shafi'i opinions and a Maliki detail differ (e.g., amputation only for husband or only if theft from a different house).
3
Follow the Opponent's Proof
Their proof is the absolute wording of Quran 5:38: 'As for the thief, male and female, cut off their hands.' They argue marriage does not create an exception.
This is a general text (amm) that they consider binding without qualification.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response relies on the marriage contract (including custom) which implies mutual access to property (al-mabsuta). This creates a doubt (shubha) that repels the hadd punishment, based on the maxim 'al-hudood tudra'u bi al-shubuhat'.
The custom of spouses handling each other's property is so widespread that it prevents the property from being considered in a secure hirz.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Hanafi law, no amputation occurs. Under Shafi'i/Maliki/Hanbali, amputation generally applies (with nuances). This difference affects legal practice in jurisdictions applying Islamic criminal law.
The Hanafi position is more protective of the marital bond and avoids severing hands for what is seen as a family matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن زوجته، وزوجها أي إذا سرق الزوج من مال زوجته أو سرقت الزوجة من مال زوجها لا يقطع يد كل واحد منهما الجريان الانبساط بينهما في الأموال عادة ودلالة؛ لأن عقد النكاح دال على المبسوطة، وكذا لا قطع لو سرق من أجنبية ثم تزوجها قبل القطع أو سرقت منه ثم تزوجت والحاصل: أن في باب السرقة يكتفي بوجود الزوجية في حال من الأحوال قبل القطع، وقال الشافعي في قول: يقطع؛ لإطلاق النص، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، وفي قول آخر: يقطع في سرقة الزوج فقط، وعن مالك إذا سرق من بيت سوى البيت الذي هما فيه يقطع وإلا فلا.
And from his wife and her husband: i.e., if the husband steals from his wife's property or the wife steals from her husband's property, the hand of each of them is not cut off, due to the mutual extension between them in property customarily and by indication, because the marriage contract indicates mutual extension. Similarly, there is no amputation if he stole from a woman not related to him then married her before amputation, or she stole from him then married. The summary: in the chapter of theft, the existence of marriage at any time before amputation is sufficient. And al-Shafi'i said in one view: he is amputated, due to the absolute text; and Malik and Ahmad said the same. In another view: he is amputated in the theft of the husband only. And from Malik: if he steals from a house other than the one they are in, he is amputated; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage rules that there is no amputation for theft between spouses because marriage implies mutual access to each other's property, so the property is not considered to be in a secure hirz (the spouses customarily handle each other's possessions). The Hanafi position is that even if the marriage occurs after the theft but before the amputation is carried out, the hadd is dropped. The exception is comprehensive: both spouses are protected. Opposing schools differ: Shafi'i (in one opinion) and Malik and Ahmad generally hold that the absolute text on theft (Quran 5:38) mandates amputation regardless of marriage. However, another Shafi'i opinion limits amputation to the husband's theft only. Malik adds a condition: if the theft occurs from a house not shared by the spouses, then amputation applies. The Hanafi position is more protective of the spousal relationship, relying on the concept of shubha (doubt) created by the marital bond.

Key Terms
المبسوطة (al-mabsuta): Mutual extension; the concept that spouses have open access to each other's property by custom and implication of marriage

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, because theft is theft
B) Never, because marriage prevents amputation
C) Only if they are separated
D) Only if the husband steals from a house other than theirs

Key Takeaway
Marriage between spouses creates a doubt (shubha) of mutual access that repels the hadd of amputation, a ruling unique to the Hanafi school among the major schools.

Theft by a Slave from the Master's Household
Ruling on slave theft from master/household

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation for a slave who steals from his master's household and explain why the ruling applies to all categories of slaves.

Core Idea
A slave is not amputated for theft from the master's home because customary permission negates proper hirz.

The Hudud punishments are severe, but they are also carefully circumscribed. In this lesson, we examine a specific exception: when a slave steals from the master or the master's close family. The ruling hinges on the concept of hirz (secure custody) and the idea of implied permission. Understanding this exception deepens our appreciation for how Hanafi jurists balanced punishment with practical realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the specific scenario: a slave stealing from his master, the master's wife, relatives, or the husband of his mistress. State the Hanafi ruling: no amputation (hadd) is applied.
The ruling is a negation of the hadd punishment, not a mere leniency.
2
Understand the legal basis
Analyze the reasoning: the slave is customarily permitted to enter these homes to perform services. Therefore, the property is not considered to be in a proper hirz (secure custody) relative to the slave. The lack of hirz prevents the application of the hadd.
This is based on rational derivation (dalil aqli) and the principle of 'urf (custom).
3
Recognize the scope of the ruling
Note that the ruling covers all categories of slaves: qinn (full slave), mudabbar (freed upon death), mukatab (under contract of emancipation), and umm walad (mother of her master's child). Partial freedom does not change the status for this rule.
The text explicitly includes these categories to avoid narrow interpretations.
4
Connect to the underlying principle
The general principle is that the slave is considered an extension of the master. If the master would not be amputated for taking his own property, the slave is likewise not amputated when the theft involves property to which the slave has implied access.
This principle is unique to the Hanafi school and reflects a broader view of implied consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسیده وزوجته: أي إذا سرق المملوك من سيده أو من زوجة سيده أو أقربائه أو زوج سيدته لا يقطع المملوك؛ لأنه مأذون له بالدخول عادة في بيت هؤلاء؛ لإقامة المصالح والمملوك شامل للقن والمدبر والمكاتب وأم الولد، ومعتق البعض كالمكاتب والحاصل أن المملوك ملحق بمولاه حتى لا يقطع في سرقة لا يقطع فيها المولى.
And his master and his wife: i.e., if the slave steals from his master or from his master's wife or his relatives or the husband of his mistress, the slave is not amputated, because he is customarily permitted to enter the houses of these people for the purpose of services. And 'slave' includes the qinn (full slave), mudabbar (slave freed upon death), mukatab (slave under contract of emancipation), and umm walad (mother of a child), and partially freed is like the mukatab. And the conclusion: the slave is attached to his master such that he is not amputated in a theft for which the master would not be amputated.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage extends the logic of the spousal exception to the master-slave relationship. A slave has customary permission to enter the master's home and the homes of the master's close family members (wife, relatives, even the husband of the mistress) to perform services. Therefore, property in those homes is not considered in a proper hirz vis-à-vis the slave, and the slave is not subject to amputation. The rule applies to all categories of slaves: full slaves, those promised freedom upon the master's death (mudabbar), those under a contract to earn their freedom (mukatab), and a slave woman who bore her master's child (umm walad). The general principle is that the slave stands in the same position as the master; if the master would not be amputated for a particular theft (e.g., from his own property), the slave is also not amputated. This is based on the idea of implied permission and lack of proper hirz.

Key Terms
مملوك (mamluk): A slave; includes various categories like qinn, mudabbar, mukatab, and umm walad
قنّ (qinn): A full slave owned completely
مدبّر (mudabbar): A slave whose freedom is deferred until the master's death
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave under contract to earn and purchase his freedom
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Yes, unless he stole from the master himself
D) No, only for full slaves

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts a slave from amputation for theft from the master's household due to implied permission, and this applies to all types of slaves.

Theft from a Mukatab: The Master's Shubha
Ruling on theft by a master from his mukatab's property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master stealing from his mukatab's property and explain how the master's right to the mukatab's earnings creates a shubha that prevents amputation.

Core Idea
A master is not amputated for stealing from his mukatab because his right to the mukatab's earnings creates a shubha that repels the hadd.

In this lesson, we examine the reverse case of theft between master and slave: when the master steals from a slave who is under a contract of emancipation (mukatab). The Hanafi ruling is that the master is not subject to amputation because he retains a legal right in the mukatab's earnings, which creates a doubt (shubha) that blocks the hudud punishment. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that partial ownership or a recognized right can constitute a shubha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act described is a master stealing from the property of his mukatab (slave under contract). The ruling is that the master is not amputated.
2
Identify the legal reason (ta'lil) provided
The text states the reason: 'because he (the master) has a right in his (the mukatab's) earnings.' This right is the master's entitlement to the mukatab's earnings as part of the emancipation contract.
3
Understand the concept of shubha in hudud
In Hanafi fiqh, hudud punishments are repelled by doubts (shubha). A right of ownership, even if partial, creates a shubha that prevents the application of hadd for theft. Here, the master's right in the mukatab's earnings creates such a doubt.
This is a specific application of the general principle: 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' (hudud are repelled by doubts).
4
Consider the cross-madhhab perspective
Note that other madhhabs may differ on whether this shubha is sufficient to repel the hadd. The Hanafi school relies on the master's established right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومكاتبه: أي إذا سرق الرجل من مال مكاتبه لا يقطع؛ لأن له حقا في أكسابه.
And his mukatab: i.e., if a man steals from the property of his mukatab, he is not amputated because he has a right in his earnings.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This short passage addresses the reverse situation: a master stealing from his mukatab. The ruling is that the master is not amputated because he retains a legal right in the mukatab's earnings (the mukatab is required to pay for his freedom from his earnings). This right creates a shade of ownership that prevents the property from being fully the mukatab's in a way that would make theft from it subject to amputation. This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that partial ownership or right (haqq) can constitute a shubha (doubt) that repels the hadd punishment. Note that this is a separate ruling from the previous one: previously, the slave stealing from the master; here, the master stealing from the slave who is under contract to earn his freedom.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract with his master to purchase his freedom by paying a sum over time

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a master not amputated for stealing from his mukatab?
A) Because the mukatab is still a slave
B) Because the master has a right in the mukatab's earnings
C) Because the mukatab gave permission
D) Because the property is considered the master's

Key Takeaway
The master's right in the mukatab's earnings creates a shubha that repels the hadd of theft.

Stealing from a Son-in-Law or Father-in-Law: The Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Ruling on theft from in-laws with ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two companions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa differs from his two companions (Sahibayn) regarding amputation for theft from in-laws, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Customary access to in-laws creates a doubt (shubha) that repels the hadd punishment according to Abu Hanifa.

In Hanafi fiqh, theft that meets the conditions of hirz (secure place) and nisab (minimum value) normally incurs the hadd of amputation. However, the presence of a shubha (doubt) can prevent the application of this severe punishment. One such shubha arises from relationships that grant customary access to property. While it is agreed that theft from a mahram (non-marriageable kin) does not incur amputation due to this shubha, the case of in-laws (son-in-law and father-in-law) is disputed within the Hanafi school itself. This lesson examines the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two distinguished companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Define the terms khatan and sihr
Identify who exactly is covered by 'son-in-law' (khatan: husband of a female mahram, e.g., daughter's husband) and 'father-in-law' (sihr: male mahram relative of one's wife, e.g., wife's brother). These are the two categories addressed in the passage.
These definitions are crucial for applying the ruling correctly.
2
State the default ruling in the matn
The matn initially states 'la yuqta' (no amputation) for both cases. Understand that this is the ruling presented as the primary position.
The matn is from Mukhtasar al-Quduri, which typically follows Abu Hanifa.
3
Identify the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Recognize that Abu Hanifa holds no amputation, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold amputation. Also note that the three other imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) agree with the Sahibayn.
This is an ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school itself.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's dalil
Abu Hanifa's proof: khalwa (seclusion) with these in-laws is permissible, and entering their homes for customary visits is common. This creates a shubha similar to that of blood mahrams, therefore repelling the hadd.
The shubha arises from customary access, not a legal right.
5
Contrast with the Sahibayn's dalil and determine the mufta bihi
The Sahibayn argue that there is no shubha because the property is in a hirz and the relationship does not grant automatic entry. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's, as indicated by the matn and the prevalence of the doubt principle.
The thamarah al-khilaf: in practice, amputation is not applied for theft from in-laws according to the dominant Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وختنه وصهره: أي وكذا إذا سرق من ختنه – وهو زوج كل ذي رحم محرم منه مثل زوج البنت وبنت الأخ - لا يقطع. (عيني، ط)، وكذا لا يقطع بسرقة من صهره وهو كل ذي رحم محرم من امرأته كأعمامها وأخواتها. (ط) أي إذا سرق رجل من زوج كل ذي رحم محرم منه، أو من كل ذي رحم محرم من زوجته يقطع عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا يقطع في الفصلين؛ لعدم الشبهة في المال والحرز وبه قالت الثلاثة، وله: أن الخلوة معهن مباح، والدخول عليهن للزيارة معتاد فأورث شبهة، والخلاف فيما إذا كان البيت للختن، وأما إذا كان لذي رحم محرم منه لا يقطع اتفاقاً.
And his son-in-law and his father-in-law: i.e., similarly, if he steals from his son-in-law – who is the husband of every female mahram relative of his, such as the husband of his daughter or the husband of his brother's daughter – he is not amputated. And similarly, he is not amputated for theft from his father-in-law, who is every male mahram relative of his wife, such as her uncles and sisters. (T) i.e., if a man steals from the husband of every female mahram relative of his, or from every male mahram relative of his wife, he is amputated according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Sahibayn) said: he is amputated in both cases due to the absence of doubt in the property and hirz, and the three (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) said the same. His (Abu Hanifa's) proof: seclusion (khalwa) with these women is permissible, and entering upon them for visiting is customary, thus it creates a doubt. The disagreement is when the house belongs to the son-in-law; if it belongs to the mahram relative himself, there is no amputation by agreement.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage deals with theft from in-laws: the son-in-law (khatan: husband of a mahram female relative) and the father-in-law (sihr: male mahram relative of the wife). There is a dispute within the Hanafi school itself. Abu Hanifa holds that the thief is not amputated (the text initially says 'no amputation' as the default ruling, then attributes amputation to Abu Hanifa? Let's read carefully. The text: 'إذا سرق رجل من زوج كل ذي رحم محرم منه، أو من كل ذي رحم محرم من زوجته يقطع عند أبي حنيفة' This says according to Abu Hanifa, he is amputated. But earlier it said 'لا يقطع' for son-in-law and father-in-law. There seems to be confusion. Actually, the earlier phrase 'لا يقطع' is quoting the text of Mukhtasar al-Quduri or similar, and then the commentary explains that Abu Hanifa differs from the Sahibayn: Abu Hanifa says he is NOT amputated (because of the shubha), while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he IS amputated. The text says: 'وقالا يقطع في الفصلين' meaning the two companions say he is amputated in both cases. And then it says: 'وله: أن الخلوة معهن مباح' giving Abu Hanifa's proof. So the correct Hanafi position: Abu Hanifa says no amputation; the Sahibayn say amputation. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is likely Abu Hanifa's due to the preponderance of doubt. The text also notes that all three other imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) agree with the Sahibayn. The dispute is only when the house belongs to the in-law; if the stolen property is in the house of a mahram relative himself, then there is agreement (no amputation) because the mahram relationship creates doubt.

Key Terms
ختن (khatan): Son-in-law; specifically the husband of a female mahram relative such as one's daughter or sister's daughter
صهر (sihr): Father-in-law or any male mahram relative of one's wife, such as her uncles or brothers

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the property is not in a hirz

C) Because the son-in-law is a blood relative
D) Because the thief had the owner's permission

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position is that theft from in-laws does not incur amputation due to the shubha arising from customary access and permissible seclusion.

No Hadd for Theft from Undivided Ghanima
Ruling on theft from undivided ghanima with talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from ghanima before division and explain the rationale based on shubha.

Core Idea
No amputation for stealing from ghanima before division because the property is originally mubah, creating a doubt that repels the hadd.

When spoils of war (ghanima) have been collected but not yet divided, each soldier's right to the property is not yet specific. The Hanafi school rules that if someone steals from this undivided pool, even if he is not entitled to any share, the fixed punishment of amputation (hadd) for theft does not apply. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this exception and its temporal limit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is stealing from the spoils of war before distribution. The ruling is that the thief is not amputated.
This is a non-application of hadd, not a permissibility of stealing.
2
Understand the legal nature of ghanima before division
Ghanima before division is considered mubah (ownerless) in its original state. Although it is destined for the soldiers, no individual has a specific ownership right until division.
This is key: the property has not yet been assigned to any owner.
3
Recognize the shubha as the cause of repelling hadd
The uncertainty of ownership creates a doubt (shubha) in the legal criteria for theft. A clear condition for hadd is that the property belongs to a specific person. Since this condition is absent, the hadd is repelled.
Hanafi principle: 'al-hadd yudra'u bil-shubuhat' (hadd is repelled by doubts).
4
Note the temporal scope
This ruling applies only before division. After division, each soldier owns his share, and stealing from that specific share would be subject to hadd, provided other conditions are met.
The passage explicitly states 'this is when it is before division'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن مغنم المغنم كالغنيمة مال يحصل من حرب الكفار، أي إذا سرق رجل من مال الغنيمة وإن لم يكن له في الغنيمة حق لا يقطع؛ لأنه مباح الأصل، وهو على صورته لم يتغير فصار شبهة والحد يندرأ بها، وهذا إذا كان قبل القسمة؛ لأنه لم يتعين له مالك ليظهر السرقة بدعواه.
And from a place of spoils (maghnam): maghnam is like ghanima, wealth obtained from war against disbelievers. So if a man steals from the wealth of the spoils, even if he has no right in the spoils, he is not amputated because it is originally permissible (mubah), and it remains in its original state unchanged, so it becomes a doubt, and the hadd is repelled by it. This is when it is before division, because no owner has been determined for it such that theft could be established by his claim.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage addresses theft from ghanima (spoils of war) before distribution. The ruling that the thief is not amputated is based on the fact that ghanima is originally mubah (ownerless property) until division. Although after conquest, the property is destined for the combatants, until division no individual has a specific ownership right. Therefore, taking from it is not considered theft (sariqa) in the legal sense because one of the conditions of theft is that the property belongs to someone specific. The uncertainty of ownership creates a shubha that repels the hadd punishment. This is the case even if the thief has no share in the spoils. The key is that the property has not changed from its original state of being mubah, and no owner has been assigned.

Key Terms
مغنم (maghnam): The place of spoils; the wealth obtained from war against non-Muslims
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or uncertainty that arises regarding a legal condition; in penal law, shubha repels the fixed punishment (hadd).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a thief not amputated for stealing from ghanima?
A) Only if he is one of the soldiers
B) Only if he steals after division
C) Before division, because ownership is not determined
D) Never, because ghanima is mubah

Key Takeaway
A thief who steals from ghanima before its division is not subject to amputation because the property's original mubah status creates a doubt that repels the hadd.

Theft from a Hammam: Hirz and Customary Permission
Ruling on theft from a hammam with day/night distinction and an analogous mosque case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between day and night for theft from a bathhouse and evaluate the counter-argument of the three other schools.

Core Idea
Customary permission during the day negates hirz; at night the hammam becomes a proper hirz.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq examines a unique Hanafi ruling on theft from a public bathhouse. The key is the concept of hirz (secure place) and how customary permission affects it. We will see why the Hanafis split the day and night, while the majority schools amputate at any time.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that according to Hanafis, there is no amputation for theft from a hammam during the day, but there is amputation at night.
The matn says: 'if a man steals property from the bathhouse, he is not amputated due to customary permission... this is during the day. As for at night, he is amputated.'
2
Analyse the basis for the day ruling
Explain that during the day, the hammam is open and people customarily permit entry, so the property is not in a hirz. The hadd is repelled due to shubha (doubt) caused by the widespread permission.
The text attributes this to the existence of idhn (permission) 'bi al-ada' (by custom).
3
Contrast the night ruling
At night, the hammam is closed and entry is restricted, so the hirz is established, and the thief is amputated.
The text explicitly distinguishes: 'this is during the day. As for at night, he is amputated.'
4
Identify the opposing position
State that the three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) amputate absolutely, regardless of time, because the general texts on theft do not make exceptions for bathhouses.
The text says 'according to the three, he is amputated absolutely'.
5
Examine the mosque analogy
Note the secondary case: theft from under a man in the mosque while the owner is present. The Hanafi position is no amputation because the mosque is not a hirz in itself, and the general permission to enter compromises any guard-based hirz.
Imam Muhammad gave a fatwa to this effect. This illustrates the application of the same principle of customary permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحمام: أي إذا سرق رجل من الحمام متاعاً لا يقطع؛ لوجود الإذن عادة، فلا يتحقق الحرز وهذا بالنهار، وأما بالليل فإنه يقطع، وعند الثلاثة يقطع مطلقاً. كان فيه حافظ، وعن أبي حنيفة إذا سرق ثوباً من تحت رجل في المسجد وصاحبه عنده، وظاهر المذهب أنه لا يقطع؛ لأنه حرز مكاني إلا أنه اختل بسبب الإذن في دخوله، فلم يعتبر الحرز بالحافظ بخلاف المسجد؛ لأنه ليس بحرز أصلاً، فاعتبر فيه الحرز بالحافظ، وأفتى به محمد الله.
And the bathhouse: i.e., if a man steals property from the bathhouse, he is not amputated due to the existence of customary permission, so the hirz is not established. This is during the day. As for at night, he is amputated. According to the three (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad), he is amputated absolutely. [Even] if there was a guard. And from Abu Hanifa [another case]: if he steals a garment from under a man in the mosque while its owner is with him, the apparent school position is that he is not amputated, because it is a place-based hirz but it is compromised due to permission to enter, so the hirz is not considered by the guard, unlike the mosque, which is not a hirz at all, so the hirz is considered by the guard. And Muhammad gave a fatwa to that effect.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage discusses theft from a hammam (public bathhouse). The Hanafi position distinguishes between day and night. During the day, the bathhouse is a place where people customarily permit entry, so the property is not considered to be in a proper hirz; thus, there is no amputation. At night, however, when the bathhouse is closed and people are not permitted to enter freely, the hirz is established, and the thief is amputated. The three other schools (Shafi'i, Malik, Hanbali) hold that amputation applies absolutely, regardless of the time, because the general evidence for theft overrides the custom of permission. The text then adds an analogous case about theft in a mosque: if someone steals a garment from under a man in a mosque while the owner is present, the Hanafi position is that there is no amputation because the mosque is not a hirz in itself, but if the owner is present, his presence serves as a hirz (al-hafiz). However, due to the general permission to enter the mosque, the hirz is compromised. This is different from the bathhouse, which is also not a hirz but the owner's presence could theoretically establish hirz, but the custom of entry overrides. Imam Muhammad gave a fatwa on this.

Key Terms
حمام (hammam): Public bathhouse; a place with customary permission for entry
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is protected; a condition for amputation in theft
شبهة (shubha): Doubt; a factor that repels the hadd penalty

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, is a thief amputated for stealing from a bathhouse at night?
A) Yes, because at night the bathhouse is a proper hirz
B) No, because the bathhouse is never a hirz
C) Yes, but only if there is no guard
D) No, because the thief had permission to enter

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between day and night for theft from a bathhouse based on the effect of customary permission on hirz; this is contested by other schools who apply the general theft verse unconditionally.

Spousal Theft and the Shubha of Marriage
Ruling on theft between spouses with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school waives the hadd penalty for spousal theft and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Marriage creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents the hadd penalty for theft, even though the theft itself remains haram.

Theft is a serious crime with a severe punishment, but Islamic law takes into account circumstances that create doubt about the application of the penalty. One such circumstance is the special relationship between spouses, where customary intermixing of wealth creates a semblance of right. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the Hanafi position that no amputation is carried out when a spouse steals from the other, and contrasts it with the opposing view of other Sunni schools.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: no amputation for a husband or wife stealing from each other.
The text explicitly says 'la yqta' yad kulli wahid minhuma'.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
The opponents (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that the hand is cut based on the general verses on theft (e.g., Quran 5:38).
The text mentions 'li-itlaq al-nass' referring to the absolute text.
3
Follow the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi school argues that marriage creates customary expansiveness (inbisat) in wealth, which generates a shubha (doubt) that precludes the hadd.
The text says 'al-jaryan al-inbisat baynahuma fi al-amwal adatan wa dalalatan'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The general texts are qualified by the maxim that hadd penalties are averted by shubuhat (doubts).
This is a foundational principle in Hanafi hudud.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in Hanafi law, a married thief keeps his hand; in other schools, he loses it.
The difference is not about permissibility but about the penal consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن زوجته، وزوجها، وسيّده وزوجته وزوج سيّدته، ومكاتبه، وختنه، وصهره، ومن مغنم، وحمام. ومن زوجته وزوجها أي إذا سرق الزوج من مال زوجته أو سرقت الزوجة من مال زوجها لا يقطع يد كل واحد منهما الجريان الانبساط بينهما في الأموال عادة ودلالة؛ لأن عقد النكاح دال على المبسوطة، وكذا لا قطع لو سرق من أجنبية ثم تزوجها قبل القطع أو سرقت منه ثم تزوجت والحاصل: أن في باب السرقة يكتفي بوجود الزوجية في حال من الأحوال قبل القطع، وقال الشافعي في قول: يقطع؛ لإطلاق النص، وبه قال مالك وأحمد.
And from his wife, and her husband, and his master and his master's wife and his master's husband, and his maktab, and his son-in-law, and his father-in-law, and from booty, and a bathhouse. And from his wife and her husband: meaning if a husband steals from his wife's wealth or a wife steals from her husband's wealth, the hand of each one of them is not cut due to the flow of expansiveness between them in wealth customarily and indicatively; because the marriage contract indicates expansiveness. Likewise, no cutting if he stole from a foreign woman then married her before cutting or she stole from him then married. The summary: that in the chapter of theft, the existence of marriage suffices in any state before cutting. And al-Shafi'i said in one opinion: he is cut due to the absolute text, and Malik and Ahmad agreed with him.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage begins the Book of Theft by explaining the rulings of the secure place (hirz) and who is exempt from cutting due to shubha. The first ruling covers spouses: if a husband steals from his wife or vice versa, the hand is not cut. The reason given is that there is customary and legal expansiveness between them in wealth due to the marriage contract. Even if the marriage occurs after the theft but before the court's judgment, the same applies. The Shafi'i school, in one opinion, rules that the hand is cut because the general texts on theft do not exclude spouses. Malik and Ahmad follow this. The Hanafi school relies on the principle that shubha (doubt or semblance of right) prevents the penalty, and here the customary intermixing of wealth creates that shubha.

Key Terms
انبساط (inbisāṭ): Customary expansiveness and mutual access between spouses in wealth
شبهة (shubha): A semblance of right or doubt that prevents the application of a hadd penalty

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school not amputate a spouse who steals from the other spouse?
A) Because the stolen amount is usually below the nisab
B) Because the marriage creates a shubha of right through customary intermixing of wealth
C) Because the spouse has ownership over the other's property
D) Because the theft is considered a minor offense

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, spousal theft does not lead to amputation due to the shubha of marriage, but the act itself remains sinful.
