Theft by a Slave and by a Master from Maktab
Ruling on theft by a slave from master's family and from maktab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on theft in these two scenarios and explain the shubha that prevents the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
A slave is not cut for stealing from his master; a master is not cut for stealing from his maktab.

This lesson examines two specific theft cases where the hadd punishment of amputation is waived due to shubha (doubt). The first involves a slave taking from his master's household; the second involves a master taking from his maktab's property. Understanding these exceptions helps clarify the conditions required for hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Locate the two distinct scenarios in the text: (1) a slave stealing from his master or master's family, (2) a free man stealing from his maktab slave's property.
2
State the ruling for each
For both cases, the ruling is that the hadd (amputation) is not applied. Memorise this conclusion.
The text explicitly says 'la yuqta' (he is not cut) for the slave and 'la yuqta' for the master.
3
Identify the legal proof (dalil)
The proof is rational (dalil 'aqli): customary permission for the slave to enter those houses; the master's right in the maktab's earnings.
This is based on 'urf (custom) and haqq (right), not explicit nass.
4
Explain the shubha
The shubha arises because the slave is customarily allowed into the premises, so the place is not a secure place (hirz) relative to him. For the master, his partial ownership right in the maktab's earnings creates a doubt that prevents hadd.
Shubha is a key concept in Hanafi hudud: any doubt about the fulfilment of conditions nullifies the punishment.
5
Recognise the scope and categories
The term 'mamluk' includes all types of slaves: qinn, mudabbar, maktab, umm walad, and partially free. The ruling applies to theft from master, his wife, relatives, and husband of master's wife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسیده وزوجته: أي إذا سرق المملوك من سيده أو من زوجة سيده أو أقربائه أو زوج سيدته لا يقطع المملوك؛ لأنه مأذون له بالدخول عادة في بيت هؤلاء؛ لإقامة المصالح. والمملوك شامل للقن والمدبر والمكاتب وأم الولد، ومعتق البعض كالمكاتب. والحاصل أن المملوك ملحق بمولاه حتى لا يقطع في سرقة لا يقطع فيها المولى. ومكاتبه: أي إذا سرق الرجل من مال مكاتبه لا يقطع؛ لأن له حقا في أكسابه.
And his master and his master's wife: meaning if a slave steals from his master or from his master's wife or his relatives or his master's husband, the slave is not cut because he is customarily permitted to enter the houses of these people for serving interests. And the slave includes a qinn, mudabbar, maktab, and umm walad, and a partial free person is like a maktab. The conclusion is that the slave is attached to his master so he is not cut in a theft where the master would not be cut. And his maktab: meaning if a man steals from the wealth of his maktab slave, he is not cut because he has a right in his earnings.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This section covers two categories. First, a slave (whether fully owned, partially free, or a maktab) who steals from his master or the master's family is not punished with amputation. The reason is that the slave is customarily permitted to enter those houses to perform services, so the place is not a hirz (secure place) in relation to him. This applies to all types of slaves: qinn (full slave), mudabbar (promised freedom upon master's death), maktab (purchasing freedom), umm walad (mother of master's child), and mu'ataq ba'd (partially freed). The slave is legally attached to the master such that wherever the master would not be cut, the slave is also not cut. Second, if a free man steals from his maktab slave's property, the master is not cut because he has a right in the slave's earnings (since the maktab must work to pay off his contract). This creates a shubha that prevents the hadd.

Key Terms
مملوك (mamlūk): Any type of slave (qinn, mudabbar, maktab, umm walad)
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has a contract to purchase freedom by paying installments
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a slave not punished with amputation if he steals from his master?
A) Because the slave owns the master's property partially
B) Because the slave is customarily permitted to enter the master's house, so it is not a hirz for him
C) Because the master's property is considered mubah for the slave
D) Because the slave is not legally accountable

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the hadd punishment for theft is not applied when there is a shubha (doubt) arising from customary permission or partial ownership rights.

Hadd Penalty: Theft from Booty and Bathhouse
Ruling on theft from booty and bathhouse with inter-madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not amputate for theft from booty before distribution and from a bathhouse during the day, and distinguish the Shafi'i/Maliki/Hanbali position on the bathhouse.

Core Idea
Shubha in ownership or security of place blocks the hadd penalty.

This lesson examines two scenarios where the hadd penalty for theft (amputation) is not applied according to the Hanafi school: stealing from war booty before it is distributed, and stealing from a bathhouse during the day. Both cases involve a shubha (ambiguity) that prevents the full punishment. We will also see how the three other major imams differ on the bathhouse ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main rulings
Read the passage and note the two distinct cases: (1) theft from booty before distribution, (2) theft from a bathhouse. For each, the Hanafi ruling is 'no amputation' under certain conditions.
2
Understand the shubha in booty
Booty (ghanimah) is originally mubah (permissible to take) for the Muslim army. Before division, it retains that original permissibility. This creates a shubha (ambiguity) that prevents the hadd. The thief may have a claim or right, even if he doesn't actually have one in the portion stolen.
Shubha repels hadd.
3
Analyze the bathhouse case: day vs night
By day, people customarily enter and leave the bathhouse freely. This customary permission means the place is not a hirz (secure place). Hence no amputation. At night, the bathhouse is closed and thus becomes a hirz, so the hadd applies.
The three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that the bathhouse is always a hirz, so the hand is cut regardless of time.
4
Examine the additional point about mosque theft
The passage mentions a scenario: stealing a garment from under a sleeping man in a mosque while the owner is present. According to Abu Hanifa, the apparent madhhab is no cut because the mosque itself is not a hirz (spatial hirz is compromised by general entry permission). However, the presence of the owner creates a 'hirz by guardian'. The fatwa given by Muhammad (likely al-Shaybani) favors cutting when the owner is present.
This illustrates the interplay between spatial hirz and guardian-based hirz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن مغنم المغنم كالغنيمة مال يحصل من حرب الكفار، أي إذا سرق رجل من مال الغنيمة وإن لم يكن له في الغنيمة حق لا يقطع؛ لأنه مباح الأصل، وهو على صورته لم يتغير فصار شبهة والحد يندرأ بها، وهذا إذا كان قبل القسمة؛ لأنه لم يتعين له مالك ليظهر السرقة بدعواه. وحمام: أي إذا سرق رجل من الحمام متاعاً لا يقطع؛ لوجود الإذن عادة، فلا يتحقق الحرز وهذا بالنهار، وأما بالليل فإنه يقطع، وعند الثلاثة يقطع مطلقاً. كان فيه حافظ، وعن أبي حنيفة إذا سرق ثوباً من تحت رجل في المسجد وصاحبه عنده، وظاهر المذهب أنه لا يقطع؛ لأنه حرز مكاني إلا أنه اختل بسبب الإذن في دخوله، فلم يعتبر الحرز بالحافظ بخلاف المسجد؛ لأنه ليس بحرز أصلاً، فاعتبر فيه الحرز بالحافظ، وأفتى به محمد الله.
And from booty: al-mughnam is like ghanimah, wealth obtained from war against disbelievers. So if a man steals from the wealth of the booty even if he has no right in the booty, he is not cut because it is originally mubah (permissible), and it remains in its original form unchanged, so it becomes a shubha and the hadd is repelled by it. This is before distribution because no specific owner is determined for the claim of theft. And bathhouse: if a man steals goods from the bathhouse, he is not cut due to the existence of customary permission, so the hirz is not realized. This is during the day; at night he is cut. According to the three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali), he is cut absolutely. And from Abu Hanifa: if he stole a garment from under a man in the mosque while its owner was with him, the apparent madhhab is that he is not cut because it is a spatial hirz but it became compromised due to permission to enter, so the hirz by the guardian is not considered unlike the mosque because it is not a hirz at all, so hirz by guardian is considered, and this is the fatwa of Muhammad Allah.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This section covers two distinct rulings. First, theft from booty (ghanimah) before distribution: no amputation because the property is originally mubah (free for anyone) and has not changed to private possession; therefore, a shubha exists. Since no specific owner is identified, the hadd is waived. Second, theft from a bathhouse (hammam): during the day, people customarily enter and leave, so the place is not a hirz; the hand is not cut. At night, when the bathhouse is closed, it becomes a hirz and the hand is cut. The three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) rule for cutting absolutely, regardless of time. The passage then digresses to a ruling about theft in a mosque where the owner is present: according to Abu Hanifa, if someone steals a garment from under a sleeping man in the mosque, the apparent Hanafi position is that the hand is not cut because the mosque is a spatial hirz but compromised by general permission; however, the mosque itself is not a hirz at all, so the guardian (the owner's presence) makes it a hirz. The phrase 'wa aftā bihi Muḥammad Allāh' indicates that the latter opinion (cutting when owner is present) is given as fatwa.

Key Terms
مباح الأصل (mubāḥ al-aṣl): Property that is originally free for anyone to take, like booty before distribution
حرز مكاني (ḥirz makānī): A place that is, by its nature, secure for storing property (e.g., a closed house)
حرز بالحافظ (ḥirz bi-l-ḥāfiẓ): Security achieved by the presence of a guardian or owner

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because booty is not yet owned by anyone
B) Because the booty is originally mubah (permissible) and remains so, creating shubha
C) Because the thief must be a participant in the war
D) Because the amount is usually below nisab

Key Takeaway
Shubha of ownership or security of place blocks the hadd penalty; customary permission can negate hirz.

Theft from Houses with General Permission: Day vs Night
Ruling on theft from houses with general permission (day vs night)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when general permission negates hirz and apply the day/night rule for theft from public-access places.

Core Idea
Permission negates hirz by day but not by night; theft at night from a shop cuts the hand.

This lesson examines how general permission to enter a place affects the requirement of hirz for hadd al-sariqa. The Hanafi school distinguishes between daytime, when entry is expected, and nighttime, when the place is closed and goods are secured. We will also see how specific permission vs. general permission plays a role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is theft from a house that people are generally permitted to enter, like shops and inns. The ruling: no hadd (amputation) if theft occurs during the day; hadd applies if theft occurs at night.
The ruling hinges on the presence of actual permission during the day.
2
Understand the basis: hirz and permission
Hadd requires that the property be taken from a hirz (secure place). General permission to enter negates the character of hirz during the day because anyone can enter openly. At night, the place is closed and becomes a hirz.
The text says: 'the houses are built for securing wealth, and permission is only for daytime.'
3
Apply the day/night distinction
If theft occurs during the day, no cut because permission exists. If at night, cut because the place is then used for securing wealth.
Consider a shop: open during day (no cut), closed at night (cut).
4
Consider specific permission vs. general
If a group is given specific permission and someone else enters with them without permission and steals, that person is cut because he had no permission at all.
This shows that only those with actual permission are exempt during the day.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيت أذن في دخوله أي إذا سرق من بيت قد أذن للناس بالدخول فيه كحوانيت التجار والخانات لا يقطع؛ لوجود الإذن حقيقة، وهذا إذا كانت السرقة نهاراً، فلو سرق منها ليلاً يقطع؛ لأنها بنيت لإحراز الأموال، وإنما خص الإذن بالنهار، ولو أذن الجماعة مخصوصين فدخل معهم من لم يؤذن له وسرق ينبغي أن يقطع. ومن المسجد إلخ والحكم غير قاصر على المسجد بل كذلك كل مكان لم يوضع للإحراز، ومنه الطريق والصحراء أي إذا سرق رجل من المسجد متاعاً والحافظ حاضر عند ماله يقطع؛ لأن المسجد ما بني لإحراز المال فلم يكن المال محرزاً بالمكان، فيعتبر الحرز بالحافظ، والحرز بالحافظ لا اعتبار له عند وجود الحرز بالمكان، والمراد بالمسجد مسجد الجماعة، فلو سرق من مسجد البيت قطع مطلقاً. وأشار بقوله: 'وربه عنده' إلى أن الثياب ليست عليه، فلو سرق من رجل ثوباً أو رداء أو قلنسوة، أو سرق من امرأة نائمة حلياً عليها لم يقطع؛ لأنه مع اللبس لا يكون قاصداً للحرز. والمعنى يقطع السارق من المسجد إذا كان مالك المسروق عند المال نائماً كان أو يقظان في الأصح؛ لأنه عليها قطع سارق رداء صفوان من تحت رأسه وهو نائم في المسجد؛ ولأن المعتبر الإحراز، وقد حصل به، فإن الناس يعدون النائم عند متاعه حافظاً له لا مضيعاً، ويقطع وإن لم يخرج المسروق عن المسجد، بخلاف المحرز بالمكان، فإن إخراجه شرط لوجوب القطع؛ لقيام يد المالك قبل الإخراج، وفي الحافظ يكتفي بمجرد الأخذ؛ لزوال يد المالك به.
And a house that is permitted to enter: meaning if he steals from a house that people are permitted to enter, like shops of traders and inns, he is not cut due to the existence of actual permission. This if the theft is during the day; if he steals from them at night, he is cut because they are built for securing wealth, and permission is only for daytime. And if a group is given specific permission and someone who is not permitted enters with them and steals, it should be that he is cut. And from the mosque etc. The ruling is not limited to the mosque but applies to any place not made for securing wealth, including roads and deserts. So if a man steals from the mosque goods while the guardian is present with his property, he is cut because the mosque was not built to secure wealth, so the property is not secured by the place, so security by the guardian is considered. And security by the guardian has no weight when security by the place exists. And by 'mosque' is meant the congregational mosque; if he steals from a mosque in a house, he is cut absolutely. And he indicated by his statement 'and its owner with him' that the clothes are not on him; so if he steals from a man a garment or a cloak or a cap, or steals from a sleeping woman jewelry on her, he is not cut because with wearing, he is not intending security. And the meaning: the thief from the mosque is cut if the owner of the stolen item is near the property, whether sleeping or awake according to the most correct view, because the hadd was applied to the thief of the cloak of Safwan from under his head while he was sleeping in the mosque, and because the consideration is the securing, and it has occurred with him, for people consider the sleeper near his property as a guardian, not a negligent one. And he is cut even if he did not remove the stolen item from the mosque, unlike the case of security by place, where removal is a condition for the obligation of cutting because the owner's hand remains before removal. In the case of guardian, mere taking suffices because the owner's hand ceases with it.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct but related rulings. First, houses that are publicly accessible, such as shops and inns: during daytime, people have permission to enter, so no amputation for theft; at night, when they are closed, the hand is cut because the place is meant for securing goods. If a group has specific permission and an intruder enters with them, he should be cut because he had no permission. Second, theft from a mosque: the mosque is not built to store wealth, so it is not a spatial hirz. Therefore, security depends on the presence of the owner or a guardian. If the owner is present (even sleeping), the hand is cut because the property is considered secured by him. Reference is made to the incident of Safwan's cloak stolen from under his head in the mosque, and the Prophet ordered amputation. The thief is cut even if he does not remove the item from the mosque, because the owner's hand is gone once the thief takes it. This differs from a spatial hirz (like a locked house) where removal from the place is required. Additionally, if a person steals clothing that is being worn (e.g., a garment on a sleeping person), the hand is not cut because the wearer is not intending to secure it in a hirz; the item is on his body. This distinction between hirz by place and hirz by guardian is a key Hanafi principle.

Key Terms
حرز بالحافظ (ḥirz bi-l-ḥāfiẓ): Security provided by the presence of the owner or a guard, even if the place is not inherently secure
إذن عام (idhn ʿāmm): General permission given to the public

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hand cut for theft from a shop at night but not during the day in the Hanafi school?
A) Because the shop is a hirz only at night
B) Because the shop owner is present at night
C) Because the thief has no permission at night
D) Because the shop is locked at night

Key Takeaway
General permission to enter a place negates hirz during the time of permission; theft at other times incurs hadd.

Theft from a Mosque: Hirz by Guardian
Ruling on theft from a mosque and hirz by guardian

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a mosque is not a spatial hirz, apply the rule that the owner's presence creates a hirz by guardian, and identify exceptions like worn clothing.

Core Idea
A mosque is not a spatial hirz; the owner's presence (even sleeping) makes it a hirz by guardian, so the hand is cut upon taking, without removal.

This lesson explores theft from a mosque, which is not built for storing goods. The Hanafi school applies the concept of hirz by guardian (ḥirz bi-l-ḥāfiẓ). The incident of Ṣafwān's cloak provides the prophetic precedent. We also examine the exception for clothing being worn, where no hadd applies because the wearer does not intend hirz.

Guided Path
1
Understand why a mosque is not a spatial hirz
The mosque is built for prayer, not for securing wealth. Therefore, it does not qualify as a ḥirz bi-l-makān (spatial security). The text states: 'the mosque was not built to secure wealth.'
This is the foundation for applying guardian-based hirz.
2
Define hirz by guardian (ḥirz bi-l-ḥāfiẓ)
When the place itself is not a hirz, the presence of the owner or a guardian makes the property secure. The guardian's care substitutes for the place.
The Prophet cut the hand of the thief who stole Ṣafwān's cloak from under his head while he slept in the mosque.
3
Apply the rule: owner present = hadd
If the owner is present near his property in the mosque (whether awake or asleep), the property is considered secure. The thief is cut even if the item is not removed from the mosque.
Contrast with spatial hirz where removal is required.
4
Distinguish from spatial hirz (removal condition)
In a spatial hirz (like a locked house), the thief is not cut until the item is taken out of the place. In guardian-based hirz, mere taking suffices because the owner's hand is severed.
The text says: 'in the case of guardian, mere taking suffices because the owner's hand ceases with it.'
5
Apply the exception for worn clothing
If a person steals clothing that is being worn (e.g., a garment on a sleeping person), the hand is not cut because the wearer does not intend to secure it in a hirz. The item is on the body, not placed for safekeeping.
The text says: 'with wearing, he is not intending security.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيت أذن في دخوله أي إذا سرق من بيت قد أذن للناس بالدخول فيه كحوانيت التجار والخانات لا يقطع؛ لوجود الإذن حقيقة، وهذا إذا كانت السرقة نهاراً، فلو سرق منها ليلاً يقطع؛ لأنها بنيت لإحراز الأموال، وإنما خص الإذن بالنهار، ولو أذن الجماعة مخصوصين فدخل معهم من لم يؤذن له وسرق ينبغي أن يقطع. ومن المسجد إلخ والحكم غير قاصر على المسجد بل كذلك كل مكان لم يوضع للإحراز، ومنه الطريق والصحراء أي إذا سرق رجل من المسجد متاعاً والحافظ حاضر عند ماله يقطع؛ لأن المسجد ما بني لإحراز المال فلم يكن المال محرزاً بالمكان، فيعتبر الحرز بالحافظ، والحرز بالحافظ لا اعتبار له عند وجود الحرز بالمكان، والمراد بالمسجد مسجد الجماعة، فلو سرق من مسجد البيت قطع مطلقاً. وأشار بقوله: 'وربه عنده' إلى أن الثياب ليست عليه، فلو سرق من رجل ثوباً أو رداء أو قلنسوة، أو سرق من امرأة نائمة حلياً عليها لم يقطع؛ لأنه مع اللبس لا يكون قاصداً للحرز. والمعنى يقطع السارق من المسجد إذا كان مالك المسروق عند المال نائماً كان أو يقظان في الأصح؛ لأنه عليها قطع سارق رداء صفوان من تحت رأسه وهو نائم في المسجد؛ ولأن المعتبر الإحراز، وقد حصل به، فإن الناس يعدون النائم عند متاعه حافظاً له لا مضيعاً، ويقطع وإن لم يخرج المسروق عن المسجد، بخلاف المحرز بالمكان، فإن إخراجه شرط لوجوب القطع؛ لقيام يد المالك قبل الإخراج، وفي الحافظ يكتفي بمجرد الأخذ؛ لزوال يد المالك به.
And a house that is permitted to enter: meaning if he steals from a house that people are permitted to enter, like shops of traders and inns, he is not cut due to the existence of actual permission. This if the theft is during the day; if he steals from them at night, he is cut because they are built for securing wealth, and permission is only for daytime. And if a group is given specific permission and someone who is not permitted enters with them and steals, it should be that he is cut. And from the mosque etc. The ruling is not limited to the mosque but applies to any place not made for securing wealth, including roads and deserts. So if a man steals from the mosque goods while the guardian is present with his property, he is cut because the mosque was not built to secure wealth, so the property is not secured by the place, so security by the guardian is considered. And security by the guardian has no weight when security by the place exists. And by 'mosque' is meant the congregational mosque; if he steals from a mosque in a house, he is cut absolutely. And he indicated by his statement 'and its owner with him' that the clothes are not on him; so if he steals from a man a garment or a cloak or a cap, or steals from a sleeping woman jewelry on her, he is not cut because with wearing, he is not intending security. And the meaning: the thief from the mosque is cut if the owner of the stolen item is near the property, whether sleeping or awake according to the most correct view, because the hadd was applied to the thief of the cloak of Safwan from under his head while he was sleeping in the mosque, and because the consideration is the securing, and it has occurred with him, for people consider the sleeper near his property as a guardian, not a negligent one. And he is cut even if he did not remove the stolen item from the mosque, unlike the case of security by place, where removal is a condition for the obligation of cutting because the owner's hand remains before removal. In the case of guardian, mere taking suffices because the owner's hand ceases with it.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct but related rulings. First, houses that are publicly accessible, such as shops and inns: during daytime, people have permission to enter, so no amputation for theft; at night, when they are closed, the hand is cut because the place is meant for securing goods. If a group has specific permission and an intruder enters with them, he should be cut because he had no permission. Second, theft from a mosque: the mosque is not built to store wealth, so it is not a spatial hirz. Therefore, security depends on the presence of the owner or a guardian. If the owner is present (even sleeping), the hand is cut because the property is considered secured by him. Reference is made to the incident of Safwan's cloak stolen from under his head in the mosque, and the Prophet ordered amputation. The thief is cut even if he does not remove the item from the mosque, because the owner's hand is gone once the thief takes it. This differs from a spatial hirz (like a locked house) where removal from the place is required. Additionally, if a person steals clothing that is being worn (e.g., a garment on a sleeping person), the hand is not cut because the wearer is not intending to secure it in a hirz; the item is on his body. This distinction between hirz by place and hirz by guardian is a key Hanafi principle.

Key Terms
حرز بالحافظ (ḥirz bi-l-ḥāfiẓ): Security provided by the presence of the owner or a guard, even if the place is not inherently secure
مسجد الجماعة (masjid al-jamāʿa): A congregational mosque open to the public

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Hanafi ruling that the hand is cut for theft from a mosque when the owner is present?
A) The mosque is a sacred place
B) The owner's presence creates a guardian-based hirz
C) The mosque is a spatial hirz
D) The Prophet specifically ruled it

Key Takeaway
In places without spatial hirz, the owner's presence creates a hirz by guardian, and the hand is cut upon taking without need for removal; worn items are excepted.

Guest Theft and Theft Without Removal
Ruling on guest theft and theft within the house without exiting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guest theft and theft without removal, and explain why permission eliminates hirz.

Core Idea
Permission to enter removes hirz; theft without removal from the property does not complete the hadd requirement.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq addresses two situations where the hadd punishment for theft (amputation) is not applied. The first involves a guest who steals from his host, and the second involves a thief who steals but does not remove the item from the house. Understanding these exceptions requires careful analysis of the concept of hirz (secure place) and how permission creates legal doubt (shubha).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct cases: (1) a guest stealing from the host, and (2) a theft where the stolen item is not removed from the house.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For the guest, no amputation under any circumstance. For the theft without removal, no amputation unless the house is large and the item is moved to the courtyard.
The guest ruling is unconditional; the removal ruling has a condition based on house size.
3
Explain the rationale for the guest rule
The guest has permission to enter the house, which negates the hirz in his regard. Even locked rooms remain non-hirz because the permission extends to the entire house. This creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents the hadd.
Permission is the key factor, not the physical security of the item.
4
Explain the rationale for the removal rule
The entire house is one hirz. Theft from a hirz requires taking the item out of that hirz. If the thief does not exit the house, the taking is incomplete. In a small house, the courtyard is part of the living space, so removal to the courtyard does not count as exiting the hirz. In a large house, the courtyard is considered a separate hirz, so moving the item there completes the taking.
The distinction depends on whether the courtyard is used as a daily living area.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Consider a guest stealing from a locked chest in the host's bedroom: no amputation. Consider a servant stealing a vase and placing it in the garden of a large estate: amputation applies because the garden is a separate hirz.
The concept of hirz is nuanced by usage and permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضيف إلخ : أي إذا سرق ضيف من مال من أضاف ذلك الشخص لا يقطع، سواء سرق من البيت الذي أضافه تلك الدار التي أذن له في دخولها وهو مقفل، أو من صندوق مقفل؛ لأن البيت لم يبق حرزاً في حقه؛ لكونه مأذونا في دخوله وكونه بمنزلة أهل الدار. وقوله: 'أو سرق شيئاً ولم يخرجه' أي إذا سرق سرقة ولم يخرج المسروق من الدار لا يقطع؛ لأن الدار كلها حرز واحد فلم يتحقق الأخذ من كل وجه، فلا بد من الإخراج منها، وهذا إذا كانت الدار صغيرة لا يستغني أهل البيت عن الانتفاع بصحنها، وإن كانت كبيرة وأخرجه أهل صحنها يقطع، وإن لم يخرجه منها.
Guest etc.: meaning if a guest steals from the wealth of the person who hosted him, he is not cut, whether he steals from the house in which he was hosted, that house that he was permitted to enter even if it is locked, or from a locked chest, because the house is no longer a hirz in his regard due to his being permitted to enter and his being like the inhabitants of the house. And his statement: 'or he stole something and did not remove it' meaning if he committed a theft but did not remove the stolen item from the house, he is not cut because the whole house is one hirz, so the taking is not fully realized; thus removal from it is necessary. This if the house is small such that the household cannot dispense with using its courtyard. If it is large and he removed it to the courtyard, he is cut even if he did not remove it from the house.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage covers two situations. First, a guest who steals from his host: no amputation because the guest has permission to enter the house, and the host's permission effectively makes the guest like a household member in terms of the hirz. This applies even if the guest steals from a locked room or a locked chest, as the permission covers entry into the house generally, creating shubha. Second, a theft where the stolen item is not removed from the house: no amputation because the entire house is considered one hirz. The thief must physically remove the item from the premises. However, if the house is large (e.g., with a courtyard and separate rooms), and the thief moves the item from a room to the courtyard, that is considered removal from a subsidiary hirz to a larger one, so he is cut even if the item remains within the main property. This distinction hinges on whether the courtyard is used by the family for daily needs (small house) or is a separate secure area (large house).

Key Terms
حرز واحد (ḥirz wāḥid): A single secure unit; the entire house is considered one hirz such that removal from that unit is required for the hadd to apply.
شبهة (shubha): Legal doubt that prevents the application of the hadd punishment, such as the guest's permission to enter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a guest not punished with amputation for stealing from his host's house, even if the item is taken from a locked box?
A) Because the guest has a partial right to the host's property
B) Because the guest's permission to enter negates the hirz in his regard
C) Because the guest is not considered a thief
D) Because the host is expected to be negligent

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, hadd for theft is dropped when there is shubha from permission or incomplete removal from the hirz.

Large House with Separate Compartments: Ruling on Theft
Ruling on theft in a large house with multiple private rooms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft in a multi-unit dwelling and explain why each unit is considered a separate hirz.

Core Idea
In a multi-unit dwelling, each private room is a separate hirz; moving stolen goods to the shared courtyard counts as removal from the hirz.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq on the application of the hadd penalty for theft in a large house with separate compartments. The key is understanding the concept of hirz (secure place) and how it applies to shared spaces. By the end, you will see how Hanafi law treats each occupant's private area as a distinct protected zone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the text: a large house with compartments, rooms, and dwellings (dar kabira fiha maqasir wa hujar wa manazil). Note the multi-unit nature.
Each compartment is a separate living space.
2
Identify the act and location
The thief steals from a specific room (hujra) and removes the stolen item to the courtyard (sahn al-dar).
The courtyard is a shared area.
3
State the ruling
The text says: 'yutta' – he is cut (amputated). This is the hadd penalty for theft.
Amputation is wajib upon satisfaction of conditions.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Each room is its own hirz because of its occupant (bi i'tibar sakiniha). Removing to the courtyard is like removing to the street (al-sahn ka al-sikka).
Hirz is determined by occupancy, not physical boundaries alone.
5
Apply the principle
Generalize: in any shared dwelling (e.g., apartment building), each unit is a separate hirz. Moving stolen goods from a unit to a common area completes the removal element for hadd.
This prevents loopholes where thieves would claim they never left the property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أخرجه إلخ: هذا الشرط يشتمل أربع مسائل، وجزاء الكل قوله: قطع المسألة الأولى: دار كبيرة فيها مقاصير وحجر ومنازل فسرق رجل من حجرة وأخرج المسروق إلى صحن الدار يقطع؛ لأنه يكون إخراجه إلى الصحن كإخراجه إلى السكة؛ إذ كل حجرة باعتبار ساكنها حرز على حدة، ألا ترى أن على باب كل مقصورة -
And if he removed it etc.: this condition includes four matters, and the result of all is his statement: 'cut'. The first matter: a large house containing compartments, rooms, and dwellings; so if a man steals from a room and removes the stolen item to the courtyard of the house, he is cut because his removing it to the courtyard is like removing it to the street, since each room, in consideration of its occupant, is a hirz by itself. Do you not see that on the door of each compartment -
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage details a specific scenario: a large house with separate rooms or apartments. If a thief steals from one room and moves the item to the shared courtyard, he is subject to amputation. The reasoning is that each room is an independent hirz for its occupant. Moving the item out of that room and into the courtyard is equivalent to removing it from the overall property, because the courtyard is not part of the private hirz of that room. The text begins to enumerate four matters (masa'il) but is cut off. The key principle is that in multi-unit dwellings, each unit is a distinct hirz.

Key Terms
حرز على حدة (ḥirz ʿalā ḥida): A separate secure place; each room in a large house is treated as its own hirz
مقاصير (maqāṣīr): Compartments or partitioned rooms in a large house
صحن (ṣaḥn): Courtyard; open area within the house but outside private rooms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Hiraz and the Condition of Breaking the Secure Place
Explaining conditions of theft from hiraz with scenarios and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for theft from a secure place (hiraz) when the thief enters or does not enter the hiraz, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the money-bundle protruding from a sleeve.

Core Idea
Theft from a secure place requires breaking into the hiraz; taking from outside does not incur the hadd.

In Hanafi fiqh, the hadd penalty for theft is only applied when the stolen property is taken from a secure place (hiraz) and the thief has broken into it. This lesson examines the meaning of breaking the hiraz through various scenarios, including the famous dispute over a money-bundle protruding from a sleeve. Understanding the precise condition of entry is essential for correctly applying the hadd ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The passage discusses when the hand is cut for theft. The general rule is that cutting applies only if the property is taken from a secure place (hiraz) and the thief has broken into it.
The hiraz can be a house, sleeve, chest, etc.
2
Analyze the four cases of the money-bundle (surah)
The surah (cloth tied with dirhams) can be inside or outside the sleeve. The theft can be by cutting or untying. In two cases there is agreement on cutting, in two there is a dispute.
The two agreed cases: when the bundle is cut inside the sleeve, or the outer tie is manipulated to take from inside.
3
Follow the reasoning of the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
They require complete breaking of the hiraz. Pulling a bundle from outside the sleeve without entering does not break the hiraz, so no cutting. Untying the knot defeats the purpose because the tie is part of the hiraz.
The effective cause (illa) is the breaking of the hiraz.
4
Understand Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf cuts in all four cases, arguing that taking from the bundle itself constitutes theft from the hiraz regardless of entry.
He considers the act of taking the dirhams as the operative factor.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah) and the mufta bihi
In practice, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the majority view: no cutting for theft from outside the hiraz. This is more cautious regarding the hadd punishment.
The text also covers other scenarios like caravan, load, and sack, which reinforce the hiraz condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة
بيان أحكام الحرز

من خارج، أو أدخل يده في بيتٍ وأخذ أو طرّ صرّةً خارجة من كم، أو سرق من المتاع أي قطع وشق قطار بعيراً أو حملاً لا، وإن شق الحمل فأخذ منه، أو سرق جوالقاً فيه متاعٌ .
أي لا يقطع من الحمل متاعا الجوالق = من حرز وهو المقصود، فلا يشترط الدخول فيه كما لو أدخله في صندوق الصيرفي لهما: أن هتك الحرز يشرط فيه الكمال تحرزاً عن شبهة العدم والكمال في الدخول وقد أمكن اعتباره والدخول هو المعتاد، بخلاف الصندوق؛ لأن الممكن فيه إدخال اليد دون الدخول، والثالثة: شق صرة خارجة من كم الغير وأخذ ما فيها، لا يقطع؛ لأن الرباط من خارج فبالطر يتحقق الأخذ من الظاهر ، فلم يوجد هتك الحرز، ولو كان مكان الشق حل الرباط، ينعكس الحكم لانعكاس العلة، وعن أبي يوسف يقطع في الأحوال كلها. وهذه المسألة رباعية؛ لأن الصرة إما أن تكون بباطن الكم أو ظاهره وعلى كل إما أن تكون السرقة بالشق أو الحل، ففي ثنتين يقطع اتفاقاً وهما إذا شق الصرة وهي داخل الكم، أو جعل رباطها الخارج، فتناول الدراهم من الداخل، وفي ثنتين لا يقطع عندهما، ويقطع عند أبي يوسف والصرة هي الخرقة التي تشد الدراهم، والمراد ههنا هو الكم المشدود فيه الدراهم، يقال: صررت الدراهم أصرها صراً إذا شددتها، والمسألة الرابعة: سرق من قطار بعيراً أو حملا لا يقطع؛ لأنه ليس بحرز مقصوداً، فتمكن شبهة عدم الحرز، وهذا لأن السائق والراكب والقائد إنما يقصدون قطع المسافة، ونقل الأمتعة دون الحفظ، حتى لو كان مع الجمال من يتبعها للحفظ يقطع؛ لأن في هذه الصورة المسروق محفوظ مقصوداً. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني)
منه شيئاً يقطع مطلقاً من خارج أي خارج البيت بعد النقب والدخول فيه. (عيني) قطار : بفتح القاف وهو الإبل إذا كانت على نسق واحد، والجمع قطر. (فتح) أو حملاً أي أو سرق حملاً - بكسر الحاء - ما يحمل على ظهر أو رأس من ظهر دابة. (فتح) لا : أي لا يقطع في جميع الصور المذكورة؛ لعدم الحرز أو لعدم هتكه. (فتح) وإن شق الحمل: هذا الشرط يتضمن ثلاث مسائل، وجزاء الكل قوله : "قطع"، الأولى: رجل شق الحمل فأخذ وإن لم يكن معه من يحفظه لتحقق الأخذ من الحرز؛ لأن الحمل يقصد بوضع الأمتعة فيه الصيانة. والثانية: سرق جوالقاً فيه متاع، وصاحبه عنده يحفظه، أو نائم عليه، أو بقرب منه بحيث يكون حافظاً ويعد حفظا عرفاً يقطع؛ لأن الجوالق غير محرز فاعتبر الحافظ، والمال الذي فيه محرز به، ففي شقه وأخذ ما فيه يقطع، وهو المسألة السابقة سواء كان معه حافظ أو لا، وفي أخذه بتمامه لا يقطع إلا أن يكون وهذا إذا كان الجوالق في غير حرز كالطريق. والثالثة: أدخل يده في صندوق الصيرفي أو في جيب غيره أو كمه، وأخذ المال يقطع؛ لأنه لا يمكن هتك الحرز بأكثر هذا. والجوالق بالضم اسم أعجمي معرب جوال، وهو الحمل. (مستخلص، فتح، محشي)
وربه يحفظه أو نائم عليه، أو أدخل يده في صندوق أو في جيب غيره أو كمه فأخذ والحال أن صاحبه المال قطع. الجوالق أو بقربه أي صندوق صيرفي ونحوه
The Book of Theft. Explanation of the rulings of the secure place (hiraz). From outside, or he inserted his hand into a house and took, or he pulled a money-bundle protruding from a sleeve, or he stole from the luggage by cutting and tearing a camel caravan or a load — no [cutting], unless he cut the load and took from it, or stole a sack containing goods. That is, he is not cut for taking goods from the load; the sack is from a secure place, which is the intended [place]. So entry into it is not required, just as if he inserted it into the chest of the money-changer. For the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) claim that breaking the hiraz requires completeness to avoid the doubt of non-existence, and completeness is in entry, which is possible to consider, and entry is the norm, unlike the chest, because inserting the hand is possible without entry. The third case: he cut a bundle protruding from someone else's sleeve and took what was in it — he is not cut, because the tie is from outside, so by pulling, the taking is from the apparent, so breaking the hiraz did not occur. If instead of cutting he untied the knot, the ruling is reversed due to the reversal of the effective cause. From Abu Yusuf: he is cut in all cases. This issue is four-fold, because the bundle is either inside the sleeve or on its outside, and in each case the theft is either by cutting or untying. So in two cases there is agreement on cutting: when he cuts the bundle while it is inside the sleeve, or he makes its outer tie and takes the dirhams from inside. In two cases, there is no cutting according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), but Abu Yusuf cuts. The bundle is the cloth that ties the dirhams, and here it means the sleeve tied with dirhams. The fourth case: he stole a camel from a caravan or a load — no cutting, because it is not an intended secure place, so the doubt of non-hiraz exists. This is because the driver, rider, and leader only intend to cover distance and transport goods, not preservation. However, if there is a guard with the camels following for preservation, he is cut, because in this scenario the stolen property is preserved intentionally. (From Mustakhlas, Fath, 'Ayni) From him, something is cut absolutely from outside, i.e., outside the house after breaching and entering it. (Ayni) Caravan: with fatha on qaf, it is camels in a single line, plural qitar. (Fath) Or load: i.e., he stole a load — with kasr on ha — what is carried on the back or head from the back of an animal. (Fath) No: i.e., he is not cut in all the mentioned scenarios, due to lack of hiraz or lack of breaking it. (Fath) And if he cut the load: this condition includes three issues, and the result of all is his statement: "cut". First: a man cut the load and took, even if there was no one with him to guard it, because the taking is from the hiraz; since the load is intended for preserving goods by placing items in it. Second: he stole a sack containing goods while its owner is present guarding it, or sleeping on it, or near it such that he is a guard and is considered a guard customarily — he is cut, because the sack is not a secure place, so the guard is considered, and the goods in it are secure by him. So in cutting it and taking what is in it, he is cut, and this is the same issue as before whether there is a guard or not. In taking it entirely, he is not cut unless it is otherwise, and this is when the sack is in a non-secure place like the road. Third: he inserted his hand into the chest of a money-changer or into someone else's pocket or sleeve and took the money — he is cut, because breaking the hiraz cannot be done more than this. The sack (jawalq) with damm is a foreign proper noun, arabicized from jwal, meaning the load. (Mustakhlas, Fath, Muhashshi) And its owner is guarding it or sleeping on it, or he inserted his hand into a chest or into someone else's pocket or sleeve and took the money while the owner was guarding it, he is cut. The sack or near it, i.e., a money-changer's chest and the like.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the concept of hiraz (secure place) in theft cases. The author first lists scenarios where the hand is cut or not, then provides reasoning from the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and a dissenting view from Abu Yusuf alone. The key principle is that breaking the hiraz requires a complete act of entry; theft from outside (like pulling a bundle from a sleeve without entering the sleeve) does not constitute breaking the hiraz. The passage distinguishes four cases regarding a money-bundle (surah) based on its location (inside/outside the sleeve) and method (cutting/untying). It also discusses theft from a camel caravan or load, where the hand is not cut unless a guard is present. The commentary emphasizes that the load or sack itself can be a hiraz if it is intended for preservation. The intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Abu Yusuf would cut in all cases, but the relied-upon position is with the Sahibayn. The passage concludes with examples of theft from a chest or pocket, where cutting is applied because the hand enters the hiraz.

Key Terms
الحرز (al-hirz): A secure place where property is kept, such as a house, chest, or sleeve; theft from it triggers the hadd punishment.
الصرة (al-surrah): A money-bundle, i.e., a cloth tied with dirhams; in this context, it refers to the sleeve in which the bundle is tied.
القطار (al-qitar): A camel caravan, with camels in a single line; theft of a camel from the caravan does not usually incur cutting.
الحمل (al-himl): A load carried on an animal's back; if cut, the hand is cut because the load is a hiraz.
الجوالق (al-jawalq): A sack or container; not inherently a hiraz, but the goods inside become hiraz through the presence of a guard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), why is the hand not cut when a thief cuts a money-bundle protruding from a sleeve?
A) Because the bundle is not property.
B) Because the hiraz was not broken; the thief did not enter the sleeve.
C) Because the sleeve is not considered a secure place.
D) Because the bundle was tied from the outside.

Key Takeaway
The hadd for theft requires complete breaking of the hiraz; taking from outside the hiraz does not incur the punishment.

The Manner of Cutting the Thief's Hand: Procedure and Proof
Explaining the procedure for amputation of the thief's hand and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure for cutting the thief's hand and foot, identify the conditions for implementation, and explain the evidence for the obligation of cauterization.

Core Idea
The thief's right hand is cut from the wrist, cauterized, and the left foot from the ankle for a second theft.

This passage details the physical implementation of the hadd punishment for theft, grounding each element in scriptural and prophetic proof. It specifies the exact sites of amputation, the obligatory cauterization, and the conditions under which the punishment is suspended—all while refuting alternate views from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the hadd punishment for theft: amputation of the right hand from the wrist. The ruling is that it is obligatory (wajib) upon the imam to execute it when conditions are met.
This is not a private act but a state-administered punishment.
2
Locate the proofs
The primary proof is the Qur'anic verse (5:38) in the reading of Ibn Mas'ud: 'So cut off their right hands.' This restricts the absolute command to the right hand and is supported by the Prophet's practice of cutting from the wrist.
The verse's imperative form indicates obligation.
3
Examine the specifics of the procedure
The hand is cut from the wrist joint (zand). The wound is then cauterized (hasm) to stop bleeding. For a second theft, the left foot is cut from the ankle (ka'b).
Cauterization is wajib based on the Prophetic command: 'Cut him, then cauterize him.'
4
Note conditions and exceptions
The cutting is not performed in extreme heat or cold, nor during severe illness. Additionally, the victim (the one from whom the theft occurred) must be present to claim the theft, regardless of witness availability.
These conditions ensure the punishment is not applied in harmful circumstances.
5
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute on cauterization
Al-Shafi'i considers cauterization recommended (mandub) not obligatory, interpreting the command as permissive. The Hanafi response relies on the default principle that a command indicates obligation (al-amr yufidu al-wujub) and that omission could lead to death.
Khawarij cut from the shoulder; Abu Thawr cuts from half the foot—both rejected by Hanafis based on prophetic practice.

دهن
Chapter on the manner of cutting and its proof. Explanation of it. And he cuts the right hand of the thief from the wrist (zand) and cauterizes it, and his left foot if he repeats [a second theft], and if he steals a third time, then cut from the ankle (ka'b). That is, or he inserted his hand into the pocket of another, which is a slit on the side of the garment to keep money. (Ayni, T) And its proof: i.e., proof of the cutting. Since cutting is the ruling for theft from a secure place, it is mentioned after the secure place, because the ruling of a thing follows it. (Fath, Ayni) Right hand of the thief: due to the reading of Ibn Mas'ud: "So cut off their right hands" which is famous, so it restricts the absolute text; this is restriction of the absolute (taqyid al-mutlaq) not specification of the general (tafsil al-mujmal), because the correct view is that there is no ambiguity in the verse. His statement: "from the wrist" because he (the Prophet) cut the hand of the thief from the wrist, and this is evidence against those who say to cut only the fingers, and against the Khawarij who say to cut from the shoulder. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 408/1] And he is not cut in severe heat or cold or severe illness, and presence of the stolen-from is required for cutting, whether the witnesses are present or not, if they are absent or dead, and so in every hadd and right. (Fath) From the wrist: with fatha on za and sukun on nun, the joint at the end of the forearm in the palm, which is the wrist. (Ayni, Fath) And cauterize: with the important ha, i.e., cauterize it obligatorily. (Ayni, T) because he (the Prophet) said regarding the thief: "Cut him then cauterize him" and its implication is obligation (wujub) of cauterization, since the basic principle in a command is obligation. It is reported from al-Shafi'i that he deems it recommended (nadb), so if he does not do it, he does not sin according to him, and he recommends hanging the hand from his neck because the Prophet ordered it. According to us, it is not recommended, but the imam may do it if he sees fit, and it has not been established that he (the Prophet) did it in every cutting to be a sunnah. Then the wage of cauterization and the price of oil are on the thief, because the cause is from him. The form of cauterization is two types: one is that his hand is cauterized with a heated iron so that his blood does not flow; the second is that after cutting, his hand is placed in boiling oil to stop the blood. Cauterization is necessary because if not done, it could lead to death. (Mustakhlas, Fath) If he repeats: i.e., if he steals a second time, he cuts his left foot, because he (the Prophet) said: "If he repeats, cut him" and there is consensus on this. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 409/1] And since cutting the hand and foot is similar to the ruling of major theft (qat' al-tariq) which is like two thefts, the second theft is attached to it in ruling. Then cutting the foot is from the ankle according to most scholars, and it is narrated from 'Umar. Abu Thawr and the Rawafid say to cut from half of the foot from the sandal strap, because 'Ali used to do that, leaving him a heel to walk on. (Mustakhlas, Fath) Oil.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This chapter explains the procedure for cutting the hand of a thief as a hadd punishment. The right hand is cut from the wrist (zand), and the wound is cauterized to prevent bleeding. If the thief steals a second time, the left foot is cut from the ankle (ka'b). For a third theft, there is no further cutting; the text implies imprisonment or other punishment. The proof for cutting the right hand is Ibn Mas'ud's reading of the Qur'anic verse 'faqta'u aymanahum' and the Prophetic practice of cutting from the wrist. The hand is not cut in extreme weather or illness, and the victim must be present to claim the theft. Cauterization is obligatory (wajib) according to the Hanafi school, based on the command in the hadith 'Iqt'uhu thumma ihsumuhu'. If the imam fails to cauterize, the thief does not die, but it is a sin of omission. The expense of cauterization is on the thief. This ruling differs from al-Shafi'i, who deems cauterization recommended (mandub) not obligatory. The passage also refutes the Khawarij who cut from the shoulder, and Abu Thawr who cuts from half the foot, by citing the practice of the Prophet and 'Umar. The Hanafi position is that the foot is cut from the ankle joint.

Key Terms
الزند (al-zand): The wrist joint, the point where the forearm meets the hand; the hand is cut from this point.
الحسم (al-hasm): Cauterization of the wound after cutting to stop bleeding; performed with a hot iron or by dipping in boiling oil.
الكعب (al-ka'b): The ankle bone; the foot is cut from this point for a second theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prescribes cutting the right hand from the wrist and cauterizing it, with the foot from the ankle for a second theft, based on the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Ruling on the Third Theft and Effect of Missing Thumb
Explanation of not cutting for third theft and analogous cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a third theft and explain the condition of prior limb loss that prevents hadd cutting.

Core Idea
After two cuts, the hadd lapses; third theft merits imprisonment, not amputation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the punishment for a thief who steals for a third time. The Hanafi school holds that after the second cut, the hadd is exhausted and the thief is imprisoned until repentance. The text also clarifies analogous cases where a missing or paralyzed thumb, two fingers, or foot prevent further cutting. Let's examine the proof and the inter-madhhab disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a third theft after two prior cuts. According to the Hanafi school, the ruling is not to cut but to imprison and discipline.
The text states: 'يحبس إلى أن يتوب ولا يقطع في المرة الثالثة عندنا'
2
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi proof is the consensus (ijma) of the Companions, specifically the statement of 'Ali to his collectors: 'I am ashamed before Allah to leave him a hand to grasp with and a foot to walk with.'
Ijma is a qati' source of law.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
'Ali's statement implies that after two cuts, the thief still has minimal function; further cutting would deprive him of all grasping and walking ability, which contradicts the purpose of the hadd (deterrence without destruction).
The reasoning is based on preservation of basic human function.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i held that the hand should still be cut in the third theft, citing the general command from the Prophet: 'Whoever steals, cut him...'
The Shafi'i view relies on a general hadith.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is preferred because the Companion consensus is stronger than the general hadith, which al-Tahawi found to be unauthentic or abrogated.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is imprisonment and ta'zir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة س حتى يتوب ولم يقطع، كمن سرق وإلهامه اليسرى مقطوعة أو شلاء، أو لا يقطع ولكنه يده اليسرى عندنا إصبعان منها سواها أو رجله اليمنى مقطوعة. الإمام والحال أن إيهامه باطلة غير منتفع بها حتى يتوب: أي وإن سرق ثالثاً يحبس إلى أن يتوب ولا يقطع في المرة الثالثة عندنا، لكنه يعزر بالضرب مع الحبس؛ لأنه لما سقط القطع لم يبق إلا الزجر بالضرب والحبس، وقال الشافعي: تقطع يده اليسرى، وفي الرابعة رجله اليمنى؛ لقوله : "من سرق فافقطعوه فإن عاد فاقطعوه فإن عاد فاقطعوه"، ولنا: إجماع الصحابة الم حين حجهم علي الله بقوله: "إني لأستحيي من الله أن لا أدع له يداً يبطش بها ورجلاً يمشي بها"، ولم يحتج أحد بالحديث بالمرفوع، فدل على عدمه، وقال الطحاوي: تتبعنا هذه الآثار فلم نجد لشيء منها أصلاً، ولهذا لم يقتل في الخامسة وإن ذكر القتل فيما رواه الشافعي، ولئن صح فهو محمول على السياسة أو على النسخ. رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٨/١] منهم أما حمله على السياسة فبدليل أنه علي قال في الخامسة: فإن عاد فاقتلوه"، وهو محمول على ما إذا سرق في الخامسة. وفي "السراجية": إذا سرق ثالثاً ورابعاً للإمام أن يقتله سياسة؛ لسعيه في الأرض بالفساد؛ ولأن قطع اليدين والرجلين هلاك معنى والحد زاجر ؛ لأنه نادر الوجود، والزجر فيما يغلب وجوده.(فتح) كمن سرق: شبه عدم القطع في الثالثة بقوله : "كمن سرق أي كما لا يقطع يمين من سرق وإلهامه اليسرى مقطوعة أو شلاء بل يحبس حتى يتوب، وكذا لو كان إصبعان من اليسرى سوى الإبهام مقطوعة أو شلاء لا يقطع؛ لأن في القطع تفويت جنس المنفعة، وهو البطش أو المشي، وكذلك في الإبهام؛ لأن قوام البطش بها، وقيد بالإصبعين؛ لأنه لو كانت إصبع واحدة سوى الإبهام مقطوعة أو شلاء . يقطع؛ لأن فوت الواحدة لا يوجب خللاً في البطش بخلاف الإصبعين؛ لأنهما بمنزلة الإبهام في نقصان البطش رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٩/١] (مستخلص) أو رجله اليمنى وكذا إذا كانت شلاء لا تقطع يمينه في السرقة الأولى؛ لأن فيه تفويت جنس المنفعة. (فتح)] أي من سرق والحال أن رجله اليمنى مقطوعة، فلا تقطع يده اليمنى؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن له يد ورجل في طرف واحد، فهو لا يقدر على المشي أصلاً، وأما من طرفين فيضع العصا تحت الإبط، فيكون قائماً مقام الرجل الفائتة، وإنما لم يذكر حكم السارق الأشل اليد اليسرى أو الأقطع؛ لأنه لما ذكر الحكم في الإبهام المقطوعة والشلاء علم منه أنه لا يقطع فيه بالطريق الأولى، ولو كانت يده اليمنى شلاء أو ناقصة الأصابع تقطع في ظاهر الرواية؛ لأن المستحق بالنص قطع اليمنى، واستيفاء الناقص عند تعذر الكامل جائز. (مستخلص، فتح)
The book of theft: ...until he repents and is not cut, like one who steals while his left thumb is cut or paralyzed, or two fingers of his left hand other than the thumb, or his right foot is cut. The imam, while his thumb is useless and unbeneficial, until he repents: meaning even if he steals a third time, he is imprisoned until he repents and is not cut in the third time according to us, but he is disciplined with beating and imprisonment. Al-Shafi'i said: his left hand is cut, and in the fourth his right foot, due to his saying: 'Whoever steals, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut him; if he returns, cut him.' Our proof is the consensus of the Companions, when 'Ali said to his collectors: 'I am ashamed before Allah to leave him a hand to grasp with and a foot to walk with' and no one argued with the marfu' hadith, indicating its absence. Al-Tahawi said: we traced these reports and found no basis for them. Hence he is not killed in the fifth, even if killing is mentioned in what al-Shafi'i narrated; if authentic, it is interpreted as policy or abrogation. ...
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the punishment for repeated theft. The text establishes that after two cuts, the third theft leads to imprisonment, not amputation, in the Hanafi school. This is contrasted with al-Shafi'i's view that cutting continues. The Hanafi proof is the consensus of the Companions, as shown by 'Ali's statement. The passage also extends the ruling to analogous cases where the thief lacks a thumb or two fingers on the left hand, or the right foot, because cutting would destroy the entire benefit of the limb. The commentary explains the reasoning: preservation of basic function takes precedence over repeated cutting. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as this is the established Hanafi position.

Key Terms
إيهام (ibham): The thumb of the hand
شلاء (shalla'): Paralyzed, unusable limb
إصبعان (isba'an): Two fingers (other than thumb) on the left hand

A) His left hand is cut
B) His right foot is cut
C) He is imprisoned until he repents and disciplined
D) He is put to death

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school limits hadd cutting to two times; further theft is punished with imprisonment and ta'zir, based on Companion consensus.

Liability for Cutting the Wrong Hand in Hadd Punishment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on executioner's liability for cutting wrong hand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the executioner's liability for cutting the left hand instead of the right, explain the reasoning for each position, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: no liability because thief gains a better limb; Sahibayn disagree in deliberate; Zufar says liable even by mistake.

When a judge orders the executioner to cut a thief's right hand but the executioner cuts the left instead, is he liable for damages? This question exposes a sharp intra-Hanafi dispute grounded in competing views on the nature of liability and the purpose of punishment. By tracing each imam's reasoning, you will learn how nuanced istinbat can lead to different rulings from the same root principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is the executioner cutting the left hand after being ordered to cut the right. Determine whether the act is deliberate or mistaken, and whether the order was specific or general.
The context is hadd punishment for theft, not a private act.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and rationale
Abu Hanifa rules no liability in either case. His reasoning: the left hand is less beneficial than the right, so the thief effectively receives a superior limb, hence no destruction or loss.
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position and generally the mufta bihi.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and rationale
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule liability only when the executioner deliberately disobeys the order. They argue that disobedience makes the act wrongful, triggering compensation.
Imam al-Shafi'i and Malik concur with them.
4
State Zufar's position and his qiyas
Zufar extends liability even to mistakes. He analogizes to any case where a protected limb is cut: mistake does not remove liability for the rights of people.
This is presented as the pure qiyas (analogy) in the text.
5
Identify the exceptions and consensus areas
If the judge said 'cut his hand' without specifying, or if the thief voluntarily extended his left and claimed it was right, there is no liability by consensus.
Also: if the cut occurs before the judge's verdict, it's a private wrong subject to qisas or diya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكيفية القطع وإثباته ولا يضمن بقطع اليسرى من أمر بخلافه. فاعل يضمن وهو قطع اليمني وطلب المسروق منه شرط القطع ولو مودعاً أو غاصباً أو صاحب الربا، يفتح الدال أي أو كان ولا يضمن أي إذا قال الحاكم للجلاد: اقطع يمين هذا السارق فقطع يساره عمداً أو خطأ فلا ضمان على الجلاد عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه أتلف اليسرى وأخلف من جنسه ما هو خير منه فلا يعد إتلافاً، وقالا: يضمن في العمد؛ لأنه خالف الأمر، وبه قال الشافعي ومالك، وقال زفر: "يضمن في الخطأ أيضاً، وهو القياس"؛ لأنه قطع يداً معصومة، والخطأ في حق العباد غير موضوع فيضمنها، هذا إذا عين له الإمام أو الحاكم اليمني بأن قال له: اقطع يمين هذا". وأما إذا أطلق بأن قال له: اقطع يده، ولم يعين لا يضمن القاطع بالاتفاق، وكذا لو أخرج السارق يساره، وقال هذه يميني لا يضمن؛ لأنه قطعه بأمره، وهذا إذا كان بأمر الإمام، وأما إذا قطعه أحد قبل أن يقضي القاضي ولم يؤمر به يجب القصاص في العمد والدية في الخطأ اتفاقاً، ويسقط القطع عن السارق؛ لأنه مقطوع اليد، ويجب عليه ضمان ما سرق؛ لعدم القطع رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٩/١] (مستخلص)
The modality of cutting and its proof. And he is not liable for cutting the left when ordered to the contrary. The subject of 'liability' is cutting of the right. And the demand of the victim of theft is a condition for cutting, even if the victim is a depositary, usurper, or usurer. 'He is not liable' means when the judge said to the executioner: 'Cut this thief's right hand' and he cut his left, deliberately or by mistake, there is no liability on the executioner according to Abu Hanifa, because he destroyed the left and replaced it with something better of its kind, so it is not considered destruction. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is liable in deliberate cases because he disobeyed the order, and al-Shafi'i and Malik also said this. Zufar said: 'He is liable in mistake as well, and this is the analogy', because he cut a protected limb, and mistake in the rights of people is not overlooked, so he is liable. This is when the imam or judge specified the right hand by saying 'cut this one's right'; if he said 'cut his hand' without specifying, the executioner is not liable by consensus. Similarly, if the thief extended his left hand and said 'this is my right', the executioner is not liable because he cut by his order. And all this is when it is by the imam's order. If someone cut the thief before the judge's verdict and without being ordered, retaliation is obligatory in deliberate cases and blood money in mistake by consensus, and the cutting is dropped from the thief because his hand is already cut, and he must guarantee what he stole because cutting did not occur.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the executioner's liability when he accidentally or deliberately cuts the left hand instead of the right. Abu Hanifa holds no liability because replacing with a better limb (right is generally more useful) is not considered destruction. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold liability in deliberate disobedience, as it violates the order. Zufar extends liability to mistake by qiyas. The passage also specifies exceptions: if the order was ambiguous or the thief misled the executioner, no liability. The authority for Abu Hanifa's view is Zahir al-Riwayah, though the Sahibayn's view is also considered. The commentary follows the text's order, explaining each position and its rationale.

Key Terms
جلاد (jallad): The executioner appointed to carry out the hadd of cutting
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay compensation or damages

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, under which of the following conditions is there consensus that the executioner is not liable?
A) When the judge specifically orders cutting the right hand and the executioner deliberately cuts the left.
B) When the judge says 'cut his hand' without specifying which hand, and the executioner cuts the left.
C) When the executioner cuts the left hand by mistake after being ordered to cut the right.
D) When the executioner cuts the right hand but the thief claims it was the left.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon position is that the executioner is not liable for cutting the left hand instead of the right, because the thief gains a more beneficial limb, negating any compensable loss.

Condition of Victim's Claim for Cutting the Thief
Explains condition of victim's claim for cutting, including extended right of custodians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the condition of the victim's claim for cutting the thief, and explain why any custodian with a custodial hand can initiate the claim, distinguishing the Hanafi view from that of al-Shafi'i and Zufar.

Core Idea
The victim's claim is a condition for hadd cutting, and any person with a custodial hand can make that claim.

In the Hanafi school, the application of the hadd penalty for theft requires not only the proven act but also a claim (da'wa) from the victim. This lesson explores why the school insists on this condition and extends the right to claim beyond the owner to any person with a custodial hand over the stolen property. You will learn the Hanafi proof for this extension and how it differs from the views of al-Shafi'i and Zufar.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school requires the victim's claim (da'wa) as a condition for cutting the thief's hand. This applies even if the thief confesses, because the victim may know facts that invalidate the theft (e.g., inheritance or mahram relationship).
This is a condition (shart), not a pillar of the hadd.
2
Identify the opposing views
Al-Shafi'i holds that the victim's presence is not required if the thief confesses (only for testimony). Zufar and Shafi'i both restrict the right to claim to the owner only. Ibn Abi Layla says presence is not required at all.
The main opposition is from Shafi'i and Zufar.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
They argue that when the stolen property is in the custody of a non-owner, there is a suspicion (shubhah) that the owner may have permitted the taking, which prevents the hadd.
This is a shubhah in the right of the owner.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the theft is established by a complete proof (testimony of two men) and a valid litigation. The custodian's right of usufruct and hand is akin to ownership, overriding any mere suspicion.
The complete proof (hujja kāmila) removes the doubt.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Hanafi, a depositary, lessee, or any custodian can initiate the claim leading to cutting. Under Shafi'i, only the owner can do so, so if a depositary refuses or is absent, cutting may not occur.
This expands the application of hadd in many cases.

فالدعوى شرط لظهور السرقة وقطع اليد، وإن كان من حقوق الله تعالى؛ لأنه لا شك أن المسروق منه أعرف بحقيقة الحال من الشاهد، وكذا من السارق المقر؛ إذ يمكن أن يكون المسروق ملكاً للسارق بطريق الإرث، أو ملكاً And the demand of the victim of theft is a condition for cutting, even if the victim is a depositary, usurper, or usurer... (rest as above for liability part) ... and the demand of the victim: i.e., the thief is not cut except in the presence of the victim, because litigation is a condition for the revelation of theft, since the crime against another's property is only revealed through litigation. So the claim is a condition for revealing the theft and cutting of the hand, even though it is from the rights of Allah, because the victim is more knowledgeable of the true state than the witness or the confessing thief; it is possible that the stolen property is owned by the thief through inheritance or by a mahram relative unknown to him, so the absence of the victim's claim is an indication that cutting is not obligatory. There is no difference in the claim between testimony and confession. Al-Shafi'i said: there is no need for the victim's presence in confession without evidence. Ibn Abi Layla said: his presence is not required at all. Then this claim is not exclusive to the owner, but rather anyone who has a custodial hand is cut by his claim, as indicated by his saying 'even if the victim is a depositary' (with fatha on the dal) or a usurper or a usurer (i.e., the taker of riba). So the claim is a condition even if the claimant is one of these. Similarly, cutting is done by the claim of the borrower, lessee, mudarib, recipient of goods for sale, holder on the condition of purchase, pledgee, father, executor, and mutawalli of waqf, because they have a custodial hand. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: cutting is not done except by the litigation of the owner, because the suspicion of permission to take or ownership remains with the owner, so cutting is not obligatory in the presence of doubt. Our proof is that this is a theft that appears through a complete proof, which is the testimony of two men with a valid litigation, due to the establishment of their right of usufruct and hand, like the owner.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about whether the victim's claim is a condition for the hadd of cutting. The Hanafi position requires the victim's presence and claim (litigation) even for confession, because the victim may know facts that invalidate theft. Shafi'i excludes confession cases, and Ibn Abi Layla excludes all. The Hanafi school extends the right to claim to any person with a custodial hand (depositary, etc.), while Zufar and Shafi'i restrict to the owner. The Hanafi proof is that the theft is established by testimony and valid claim, and the custodian's right is akin to ownership in this context. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah, and the passage includes responses to opposing views.

Key Terms
يد حافظة (yad hafizah): A hand that holds the property in trust or custody, with the right to protect it
دعوى (da'wa): The legal claim or litigation by the victim to establish the theft
شبهة (shubhah): A legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd

In Hanafi fiqh, the claim of any person with a custodial hand over the stolen property is a valid condition for the hadd of cutting, not just the owner.
