Executioner Liability for Cutting the Wrong Hand
Intra-Hanafi dispute on executioner liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on executioner liability for cutting the left hand when ordered to cut the right, and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa versus the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The executioner is not liable because the inferior left hand is replaced by a superior right hand—no tort.

In Hanafi criminal law, when a judge orders the amputation of a thief's right hand, what happens if the executioner cuts the left instead? Is he liable? The answer depends on whether the act is considered a tort (itlaf). This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, along with the position of Zufar, using the passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The executioner cutting the left hand when ordered to cut the right. The matn states 'la yadman' (he is not liable).
This is the default position in the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that severing the left hand does not constitute itlaf (destruction) because the left is inferior to the right. The executioner, by leaving the right intact, has in effect replaced a lesser limb with a better one.
This is an istihsan argument overriding qiyas.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's objection
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that intentional violation of the judge's order makes the executioner liable because he deliberately disobeyed. The left hand is still a protected limb.
They agree with Abu Hanifa on mistake, but differ on intent.
4
Consider Zufar's qiyas argument
Zufar extends liability even to mistake by qiyas: cutting any protected limb without right constitutes a tort, and mistake does not cancel liability in huquq al-ibad.
This is the strict qiyas position, but the Hanafi school adopts istihsan.
5
Apply the rule to different scenarios
The passage also covers cases where the judge does not specify (no liability by consensus) and where the executioner acts without prior order (qisas or diyah applies).
These boundary cases clarify the scope of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يضمن بقطع اليسرى من أُمِرَ بخلافه. فاعل يضمن وهو قطع اليمني
ولا يضمن أي إذا قال الحاكم للجلاد: اقطع يمين هذا السارق فقطع يساره عمداً أو خطأ فلا ضمان على الجلاد عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه أتلف اليسرى وأخلف من جنسه ما هو خير منه فلا يعد إتلافاً، وقالا: يضمن في العمد؛ لأنه خالف الأمر، وبه قال الشافعي ومالك، وقال زفر: "يضمن في الخطأ أيضاً، وهو القياس"؛ لأنه قطع يداً معصومة، والخطأ في حق العباد غير موضوع فيضمنها، هذا إذا عين له الإمام أو الحاكم اليمني بأن قال له: اقطع يمين هذا". وأما إذا أطلق بأن قال له: اقطع يده، ولم يعين لا يضمن القاطع بالاتفاق، وكذا لو أخرج السارق يساره، وقال هذه يميني لا يضمن؛ لأنه قطعه بأمره، وهذا إذا كان بأمر الإمام، وأما إذا قطعه أحد قبل أن يقضي القاضي ولم يؤمر به يجب القصاص في العمد والدية في الخطأ اتفاقاً، ويسقط القطع عن السارق؛ لأنه مقطوع اليد، ويجب عليه ضمان ما سرق؛ لعدم القطع
And he is not liable for cutting the left if ordered contrary to it. So the agent is liable when cutting the right. And the demand of the stolen-from is a condition for amputation, even if it be a depositary, a usurper, or an usurer. It opens the dal, i.e., 'or it was'. And he is not liable: meaning, if the judge said to the executioner: 'Cut the right hand of this thief', and he cut his left intentionally or mistakenly, then there is no liability upon the executioner according to Abu Hanifa; because he destroyed the left and replaced it with something better of its kind, so it is not considered destruction. And they two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is liable in intentional (cutting) because he violated the order; and al-Shafi'i and Malik held the same. And Zufar said: 'He is liable also in mistake, and that is the analogy (qiyas); because he cut a protected hand, and mistake regarding the rights of people is not cancelled, so he is liable for it.' This is if the imam or judge specified the right by saying: 'Cut his right.' But if he spoke absolutely, saying: 'Cut his hand', and did not specify, then the cutter is not liable by agreement. Similarly, if the thief extended his left and said 'This is my right', he is not liable because he cut by his order. This is if it was by order of the imam. But if someone cut it before the judge had passed judgment and was not ordered, retaliation is obligatory in intentional and blood money in mistake by agreement, and the amputation is dropped from the thief because his hand is already cut, and he must guarantee what he stole due to lack of amputation.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute concerning the liability of an executioner who cuts the wrong hand. The author (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) first states the matn: the executioner is not liable if he cuts the left hand when ordered to cut the right. The agent is liable only when cutting the right (the correct hand) for compensation? Actually the matn is ambiguous. The commentary explains: if the judge orders amputation of the right hand and the executioner cuts the left, intentionally or mistakenly, there is no liability according to Imam Abu Hanifa. His reasoning is that the left hand is inferior to the right, so destroying an inferior and replacing it with a superior is not considered a tort (itlaf). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold him liable in the case of intentional violation, as he deliberately disobeyed the judge's order. This is also the view of al-Shafi'i and Malik. Zufar argues for liability even in mistake on the basis of qiyas: the left hand is a protected limb, and mistakes are not excused in property torts. The passage then distinguishes cases: if the judge specifies the right, the executioner is not liable unless he cuts the left intentionally (according to the Sahibayn). If the judge merely says 'cut his hand' without specification, there is no liability by consensus. If the thief himself presents his left and says it is his right, the executioner is not liable. If the executioner acts without a prior judicial order, he is subject to qisas or diyah, and the thief's amputation is aborted.

Key Terms
اليسرى (al-yusrā): The left hand; in this context, the left hand of the thief, which is considered inferior to the right.
اليمنى (al-yumnā): The right hand; the proper hand to be amputated for theft.
إتلاف (itlāf): Destruction of property or a limb; here, Abu Hanifa argues that cutting the left does not constitute itlaf because it is replaced by a better (right) hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is the executioner not liable for cutting the left hand when ordered to cut the right?
A) Because the executioner acted under duress
B) Because the left hand is inferior and its destruction is compensated by a better replacement
C) Because the thief consented to the amputation
D) Because the judge's order was invalid

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, an executioner is not liable for cutting the left hand instead of the right because the loss of an inferior limb compensated by a superior one is not considered a tort; this is the mufta bihi position.

Condition of Victim's Demand for Amputation
Ruling that victim's claim is required for cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that the victim's demand is required for the hadd penalty, identify who qualifies as a claimant (yad hafiza), and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument based on shubha.

Core Idea
The claim of any protective hand suffices for amputation; the owner's presence is not essential.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes a key procedural condition for the hadd penalty for theft: the demand of the person whose property was taken. However, the Hanafi school broadens this requirement to include not only the owner but any custodian with a protective hand (yad hafiza), such as a depositary, lessee, or usurer. The author explains the rationale and addresses objections from Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Find the matn statement: the demand of the stolen-from is a condition for cutting. Note that the commentary expands this to include various custodians.
The condition is procedural, not substantive.
2
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi proof is based on qiyas: the theft is proven by complete evidence (two witnesses) with a valid claim from a custodian whose hand is analogous to the owner's.
The dalil is zanni in transmission and meaning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The evidence shows that the custodian's right of usufruct and protective hand make their claim equivalent to the owner's for establishing theft.
The key link is the hand (yad) and its function of preservation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Al-Shafi'i and Zufar argue that only the owner can bring the claim due to shubha (suspicion of permission). Ibn Abi Layla dispenses with the condition entirely.
This is a genuine disagreement with practical impact.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi) because the suspicion is not strong enough to override clear proof, and the custodian's benefit ultimately belongs to the owner.
The text's statement 'yaru' bi-da'wahum' indicates the established view.

وطلب المسروق منه شرط القطع ولو مودعاً أو غاصباً أو صاحب الربا، يفتح الدال أي أو كان
وطلب المسروق منه إلخ أي لا يقطع السارق إلا بحضرة المسروق منه؛ لأن الخصومة شرط لظهور السرقة؛ إذ الجناية على ملك الغير لا يظهر إلا بالخصومة، فالدعوى شرط لظهور السرقة وقطع اليد، وإن كان من حقوق الله تعالى؛ لأنه لا شك أن المسروق منه أعرف بحقيقة الحال من الشاهد، وكذا من السارق المقر؛ إذ يمكن أن يكون المسروق ملكاً للسارق بطريق الإرث، أو ملكاً لذي رحم محرم وهو غير عالم به ففي ترك المسروق منه الدعوى مظنة عدم وجوب القطع، وكذا في غيبته، ولا فرق في الدعوى بين الشهادة والإقرار، وقال الشافعي: لا حاجة إلى حضور المسروق منه في الإقرار دون البينة. وقال ابن أبي ليلى: لا يشترط حضوره أصلاً. ثم هذا الدعوى لا يختص بالمالك، بل كل من كان له يد حافظة تقطع بدعواه كما أشار إليه بقوله: "ولو مودعاً" بفتح الدال، أو كان غاصباً، أو صاحب الربا أي آخذه يعني الدعوى شرط ولو كان المدعي من هؤلاء وكذا يقطع بدعوى المستعير والمستأجر والمضارب والمستبضع والقابض على سوم الشراء والمرتمن والأب والوصي ومتولي الوقف؛ لأن لهم يداً حافظة وقال زفر والشافعي: لا يقطع إلا بخصومة المالك؛ لأن شبهة الإذن بالأخذ أو التمليك قائمة المالك، فالقطع لا يجب بالشبهة ولنا: أن هذه سرقة ظهرت بحجة كاملة، وهي شهادة من رجلين بخصومة معتبرة لثبوت حقهم في الانتفاع واليد كالمالك
And the demand of the stolen-from is a condition for amputation, even if it be a depositary, a usurper, or an usurer – it opens the dal, i.e., 'or it was'. And 'the demand of the stolen-from, etc.' means: the thief is not cut except in the presence of the stolen-from; because litigation (khusuma) is a condition for the manifestation of theft, since the offense against another's property does not become manifest except through litigation. So the claim (da'wa) is a condition for the manifestation of theft and amputation of the hand, even if it is from the rights of Allah; because there is no doubt that the stolen-from is more knowledgeable of the true state of affairs than the witness, and likewise than the confessing thief; since it is possible that the stolen property is the property of the thief by inheritance, or the property of a mahram relative while he is unaware of it. So the absence of claim by the stolen-from suggests that amputation is not obligatory, and likewise in his absence. And there is no difference regarding the claim between testimony and confession. Al-Shafi'i said: There is no need for the presence of the stolen-from in the case of confession, unlike the case of evidence. And Ibn Abi Layla said: His presence is not a condition at all. Then this claim is not restricted to the owner; rather, everyone who has a protective hand (yad hafiza) – amputation is carried out by his claim, as he indicated by his saying: 'even if it be a depositary' with fatha on the dal, or a usurper, or an usurer, i.e., the taker – meaning the claim is a condition even if the claimant is from among these. Similarly, amputation is carried out by the claim of the borrower, the lessee, the mudarib, the one who takes goods for sale, the one who holds [something] with the option to buy, the pledgee, the father, the executor, and the administrator of a waqf; because they have a protective hand. And Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: Amputation is not carried out except by the litigation of the owner; because the suspicion of permission to take or ownership exists with the owner, so amputation is not obligatory upon the basis of suspicion. Our evidence: this is a theft that has appeared with complete proof, which is the testimony of two men with considered litigation that establishes their right of usufruct, and they have a hand like the owner's.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the condition that the victim (or his representative) must demand the stolen property for the amputation penalty to be applied. The author first states the matn: the demand of the stolen-from is a condition, even if that person is a depositary (wadi'), a usurper (ghasib), or an usurer (sahib al-riba). The commentary explains that litigation (khusuma) is required for the theft to become legally manifest, as the crime against another's property only becomes evident through a claim. The stolen-from is more knowledgeable than witnesses or the thief himself—for example, the property might actually belong to the thief by inheritance, or to a close relative, and the thief may be unaware. Hence, the absence of a claim indicates that the amputation might not be obligatory. Al-Shafi'i considers the presence of the stolen-from unnecessary in case of confession, unlike when evidence is relied upon. Ibn Abi Layla dispenses with it entirely. The passage then extends the right to demand to individuals who have a 'protective hand' over the property: the depositary, usurper, usurer, and others like the borrower, lessee, mudarib, etc. Zufar and al-Shafi'i object, arguing that only the true owner can bring the claim, since a suspicion (shubha) of authorization or ownership remains with the owner, and amputation is not established by suspicion. The Hanafi response is that the theft is proven through full evidence (two witnesses) and the claimants have a recognized usufruct right, making their hand akin to that of the owner.

Key Terms
يد حافظة (yad hāfiẓa): A hand that preserves and protects the property; it includes ownership, custody, and usufruct rights.
خصومة (khuṣūma): Litigation or formal claim; a legal confrontation that establishes the theft.
شبهة (shubha): Suspicion or doubt that prevents the application of the hadd penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the original owner of the stolen property
B) Only the judge or ruler
C) Anyone with a protective hand over the property, such as a depositary or lessee
D) The thief's family members

Key Takeaway
The claim of any person with a protective hand suffices for the amputation penalty; the Hanafi school extends this beyond the owner to all custodians with usufruct rights.

Stealing from a Thief After Amputation: No Hadd for the Second Thief
Ruling with proof, explaining why second theft after amputation does not incur hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the second thief and explain why the property being ghayr mutaqawwam prevents the application of hadd.

Core Idea
After amputation, the stolen property loses its legal value for the first thief, so stealing it again does not trigger amputation.

This lesson examines a specific edge case in the law of hudud: if a thief's hand has already been amputated for theft, and then someone else steals that same property from him, does the second thief also face amputation? The Hanafi school says no, because the property is no longer considered valuable (mutaqawwam) in the hands of the first thief. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the concept of taqawwum and how it conditions the hadd penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act? A second thief (sāriq thānī) steals property that was already the subject of a hadd amputation from the first thief (sāriq awwal). What is the ruling? The second thief's hand is not cut.
The ruling applies after the first thief's hand has been cut, not before.
2
Locate the reason (talīl)
The text provides the reasoning: 'because the property is not valuable (ghayr mutaqawwam) with respect to the first after the amputation.' Identify why this matters for hadd: one condition for amputation is that the stolen property must be valuable (mutaqawwam) in the hands of the thief.
3
Analyse the condition of taqawwum
After amputation, the first thief is not liable for the property (he has no damān if it is destroyed). Since he has no guarantee over it, the property is not considered valuable in his possession. Therefore, the essential element for amputation is missing.
Compare with theft before amputation: then the property is still valuable, and both the first thief and owner can demand amputation of the second thief.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on reclaiming the property
There are two narrations within the school: one says the first thief has no right to reclaim the property (because it is no longer his guarantee), the other says he can reclaim it to return it to the owner. This dispute does not affect the ruling on amputation of the second thief.
The ikhtilaf is about the first thief's right, not the second thief's penalty.
5
Apply the ruling to new scenarios
Test your understanding: what if the property is stolen from the first thief before his hand is cut? What if the first thief repents and the property is returned before amputation? The key variable is the timing of the second theft relative to the amputation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بطلب المالك أو السارق لو سرق من يد السارق المال المسروق أيضاً أي السارق سارق بعد القطع. (مستخلص) لا بطلب المالك إلخ [ أي لا يقطع يد السارق الثاني. (ط)] أي لو قطع سارق بسرقة وسرق المسروق من عنده لم يكن للسارق ولا للمالك أن يقطع السارق الثاني؛ لأن المال غير متقوم في حق الأول بعد القطع، حتى لا يجب عليه الضمان بإهلاكه فلا ينعقد موجبه للقطع، بخلاف ما إذا سرق منه قبل أن تقطع يده حيث يكون له ولرب المال القطع، وليس للسارق الأول ولاية الاستراد في رواية وفي رواية له ذلك ليرده على المالك رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٩/١] بعد القطع : أي بعد قطع يد السارق الأول، فلو قبل القطع يكون له وللمالك الطلب. (ط)
Not by the demand of the owner or the thief if [the property] is stolen from the hand of the thief, the stolen property also, i.e., a thief stealing from a thief after amputation. (Mukhtasar from Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:409) 'Not by the demand of the owner, etc.' [i.e., the hand of the second thief is not cut. (Tah)] Meaning: if a thief is cut for a theft and then the property is stolen from him, neither the [first] thief nor the owner can cut the hand of the second thief; because the property is not considered valuable (ghayr mutaqawwam) with respect to the first after the amputation, so that he is not liable for compensation if he destroys it, therefore its cause for amputation does not arise. Unlike if it was stolen from him before his hand was cut, in which case both he and the owner of the property can demand amputation. There is a narration that the first thief has no right to reclaim it, and another narration that he does have that right to return it to the owner. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:409) 'After amputation': i.e., after the hand of the first thief is cut; if before cutting, then he and the owner can demand [amputation]. (Tah)
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage states a specific rule: if a thief (first thief) has his hand amputated for theft, and then someone else (second thief) steals the same property from the first thief's possession after the amputation, neither the first thief nor the original owner can demand the amputation of the second thief. The reason is that after the hadd is carried out, the property is no longer considered valuable (muttaqawwam) in relation to the first thief; he has no obligation to guarantee it, so the essential cause (sabab) for amputation does not exist. However, if the theft from the first thief occurred before the amputation of his hand, both the first thief and the owner can seek amputation. There is a disagreement within the Hanafi school regarding the first thief's right to reclaim the property: one transmission says he has no such right, another says he does have it so he can return it to the owner. The rule is conditional on the amputation having occurred; before amputation, the property is still valuable.

Key Terms
غير متقوم (ghayr mutaqawwam): Not valuable; property for which the possessor has no guarantee (damān) and which cannot be the subject of a sale or compensation.
سارق بعد القطع (sāriq ba'd al-qaṭ'): A thief who steals after the first thief's hand has been amputated; the property is already subject to the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hand of a second thief not cut when he steals property from a first thief whose hand has already been amputated?
A) Because the first thief has no right to demand amputation
B) Because the property is not considered valuable (ghayr mutaqawwam) with respect to the first thief after his amputation
C) Because the property now belongs to the state
D) Because the second thief is considered an agent of the original owner

Key Takeaway
The hadd penalty for theft requires the stolen property to be valuable (mutaqawwam) in the hands of the person from whom it is taken; after amputation, the first thief loses this status, so subsequent theft from him does not trigger amputation.

When Amputation is Dropped: Return, Ownership, and Claim
Ruling on when amputation is dropped due to return, ownership, or claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where amputation is dropped in Hanafi fiqh and apply the condition that the adversarial claim (khusuma) must persist until execution.

Core Idea
Amputation for theft is dropped if the property is returned or ownership changes before the adversarial claim is established.

In Hanafi criminal law, the hadd penalty for theft is not automatically imposed upon the act of stealing; it requires a formal adversarial claim (khusuma) that continues up to the moment of execution. This lesson examines three situations where the thief's actions after the theft – returning the property, acquiring ownership, or claiming ownership – nullify the penalty. Understanding these rulings requires recognizing that procedural conditions are as critical as the substantive act itself.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the core condition
The amputation penalty for theft requires a formal adversarial claim (khusuma) by the victim, which must persist until execution. Without this condition, the theft is not legally 'manifested' for hadd purposes.
This is a procedural requirement, not a substantive one.
2
Analyze the first case: return before litigation
If the thief returns the stolen property to the owner before any formal claim, the khusuma is absent, so amputation is dropped. The text details subcases regarding return to relatives, distinguishing between those in the thief's household (effective return) and those not (ineffective).
Abu Yusuf's contrary view is based on analogy with return after litigation, but the relied-upon position uses istihsan.
3
Analyze the second case: acquisition of ownership
If after judgment (but before execution) the thief acquires ownership of the stolen item (e.g., by gift or sale), the amputation is dropped because the adversarial claim no longer exists at execution. The condition must hold at the moment of execution.
Zufar, Shafi'i, and Ahmad disagree, holding that the completed theft is sufficient.
4
Analyze the third case: claim of ownership
If the thief claims ownership of the stolen property, this creates a doubt (shubha) that blocks the hadd. The text implies the amputation is dropped.
The passage is cut off here, but the pattern is consistent.
5
Compare with post-litigation return
Returning property after litigation has begun does not drop the amputation, because the khusuma was already established. This contrast clarifies the importance of timing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And whoever steals something and returns it before the confrontation (khusuma)... or before raising it to the judge, or by the demand of the thief, the second thief. – As for the giver of usury, he cannot litigate because he has no ownership. Its form: a man bought ten dirhams for five, and it was stolen from the buyer; he claimed in the absence of the seller – it is cut according to our three imams, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. (Hashiya, Fath) 'By the demand of the owner': i.e., if the thief steals from one of these, and the owner demands the stolen property, the thief's hand is cut by his claim in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), while in the narration of Ibn Samaca, he is not cut in the absence of the depositary and his like; because the thief did not steal from the owner but from them, so it is not permissible for others to demand. The face of the apparent narration: the owner is the possessor of the right, and the claim of these is derived from him because they have a protective hand, and a mere imagined suspicion is not considered, because the original is its absence. (Mukhtasar) 'Not by the demand of the owner, etc.' [i.e., the hand of the second thief is not cut. (Tah)] Meaning: if a thief is cut for a theft and then the property is stolen from him, neither the [first] thief nor the owner can cut the hand of the second thief; because the property is not valuable for the first after the amputation, so that he is not liable for compensation if he destroys it, therefore its cause for amputation does not arise. Unlike if it was stolen from him before his hand was cut, in which case both he and the owner of the property can demand amputation. There is a narration that the first thief has no right to reclaim it, and another narration that he does have that right to return it to the owner. 'After amputation': i.e., after the hand of the first thief is cut; if before cutting, then he and the owner can demand. (Tah) 'And whoever steals, etc.' The relative clause is the subject and the predicate is 'he is not cut'. These are four issues: One: A man stole something and returned it to its owner before they litigated – he is not cut because litigation is a condition for manifestation of theft, so the theft does not appear without a claim. From Abu Yusuf: he is cut, by analogy with returning it after litigation. This is clear if he returns it after the judgment of amputation, and likewise if he returns it after the witnesses testified but before judgment, by way of istihsan. If he returns it to his child or relative, if the child is in his household, it is like returning to the owner, so he is not cut if before litigation; if not in the household of the stolen-from, he is cut due to lack of actual or legal return. If he returns it to his wife, his hired worker, or his slave, he is cut. If he returns it to his father, grandfather, mother, or grandmother who are not in his household, he is not cut because these have a semblance of ownership. If he gives it to the household of these, he is cut. The second issue: alluded to by his saying 'or he owns it' – i.e., if the thief owns the stolen object by being gifted it and delivered, or it is sold to him after judgment of amputation, he is also not cut. Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad in a narration say: the amputation is not dropped; rather, his hand is cut, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf; because the theft occurred with full realization and manifestation, and this contingency does not override the value at the time of theft. Our evidence: the execution of the judgment in hadd penalties is satisfied by the actual carrying out; before that, the condition of litigation must continue at the time of execution, so it is like if he owned it before the judgment, and like if the descriptions of the witnesses changed by blindness, muteness, apostasy, or sin in this case. The third issue: alluded to by his saying 'or he claims' – i.e., if he stole something and then the thief claimed [ownership]...
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive treatment of situations where the amputation penalty is dropped even though the act of theft occurred. The text begins with the matn: 'Whoever steals something and returns it before litigation, or owns it, or claims [ownership] – he is not cut.' The commentary then details four cases (the fourth is incomplete in the OCR). The first case: if the thief returns the stolen property to the owner before any legal proceedings, the amputation is waived because the condition of litigation (khusuma) is not fulfilled. There is a narration from Abu Yusuf that he would still be cut in analogy with return after litigation, but the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya) is that he is not. Subcases involve returning to family members: if the recipient is under the thief's care (in his household), it is like returning to the owner; otherwise, it is not considered a proper return. The second case: if the thief acquires ownership of the stolen item (e.g., by gift or purchase) after the judgment but before execution, the amputation is dropped according to the Hanafi school, because the condition for execution (the adversarial claim) must persist at the time of execution. Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and a narration from Ahmad argue that it does not drop because the theft was already complete. The third case: if the thief claims ownership of the stolen property (without returning), the amputation is also dropped, as the claim creates a doubt (shubha). The passage is cut off, but the pattern is clear: the hadd is avoided when there is any ambiguity or when the property is restored to the victim before formal litigation.

Key Terms
خصومة (khuṣūma): Legal contestation or claim; the formal proceeding that establishes the theft.
ردّ (radd): Return of the stolen property to its owner, which drops the hadd if done before litigation.
ملكه (mallakahu): The thief acquires ownership of the stolen object through a valid transfer after the theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a thief acquires ownership of the stolen item after the judge has issued the amputation order but before the execution, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Amputation is still carried out
B) Amputation is dropped
C) Amputation is postponed until ownership is transferred back
D) Thief must pay compensation instead

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the amputation penalty for theft is contingent on the continuation of the adversarial claim (khusuma) until execution; any action that removes this condition—such as return before litigation, acquisition of ownership, or claim of ownership—drops the hadd.

Cutting by Request of the Owner: Condition for Hadd Execution
Ruling on condition of victim's request for hadd cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of the owner's request for the hadd cutting and apply it to scenarios involving depositaries, invalid transactions, and secondary theft.

Core Idea
A thief's hand is cut only at the victim's request—the owner's claim is the operative condition.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes a critical condition for implementing the hadd punishment of cutting the hand: the request of the victim (owner). Without the owner's demand, even if theft is proven, the punishment is not executed. The text also addresses nuanced situations where the stolen property is in the hand of a depositary or where the transaction is invalid (riba). These rulings demonstrate the Hanafi school's careful balancing of the victim's right and the avoidance of doubt in hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its condition
The passage begins by stating that cutting is executed by the request of the owner. Note that the owner's demand is a necessary condition; without it, the hadd is not applied.
This condition is unique to the Hanafi school; other schools may allow automatic execution.
2
Analyze the proof for this condition
The 'face of the apparent narration' (wajh al-riwāya) argues that the owner is the true right-holder. The depositary's claim is derived from the owner, and any presumed doubt is disregarded because the original state is the absence of doubt. This is a rational proof based on authority and presumption of integrity.
The proof is based on the principle that the owner's right is primary and the depositary's hand is protective.
3
Compare the Hanafi position with the opposing view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree in the case of a missing seller in a riba transaction. They argue the buyer has no valid ownership, so the property is not mutaqawwim for him. The Hanafi response: the buyer has possession and is liable, so the property is mutaqawwim; cutting does not depend on the seller's presence.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi view is mufta bihi.
4
Apply the ruling to the secondary thief case
If a thief's hand is cut and then the stolen property is stolen from him, neither the first thief nor the original owner can request cutting of the second thief. After the cut, the property is no longer mutaqawwim for the first thief, so the cause for cutting does not arise.
Before the cut, both the first thief and the owner could request cutting.

The Book of Theft. 121: The manner of cutting and its proof. And cutting is [executed] by the request of the owner if he stole from them, not by the request of the owner or the thief if he stole from the hand of the thief (i.e., the stolen property from the thief after the cutting). And as for the giver of riba, he has no right to litigate; because he has no ownership. Its example: a man bought ten dirhams for five, and [the dirhams] were stolen from the buyer, and [the buyer] claimed in the absence of the seller: cutting is [applied] according to our three imams, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. (Hashiya, Fath) 'By the request of the owner': meaning if a thief stole from one of these [people], and the owner requested the cutting, the thief is cut by his claim in the apparent narration. And in the narration of Ibn Samā‘a: he is not cut in the case of the absence of the depositor and the like; because the thief did not steal from the owner, but rather stole from them, so it is not permissible for other than them to demand him. The face of the apparent narration: that the owner is the possessor of the right, and the claim of these [people] is only derived from him; because they have a protective hand, and no attention is paid to an objection of a presumed doubt; because the original is its absence. (Mukhtaṣar) 'Not by the request of the owner etc.' [i.e., the hand of the second thief is not cut. (Ṭ)] i.e., if a thief was cut for a theft and the stolen property was stolen from him, it is not for the thief nor the owner to have the second thief cut; because the property is not a valid consideration (muttaqawwim) with respect to the first [thief] after the cutting, so that he is not liable for its destruction, so its cause for cutting does not arise. Contrary to if it was stolen from him before his hand was cut, where both he and the owner of the property have the right of cutting. And the first thief does not have the authority to reclaim in one narration; and in another narration, he has that to return it to the owner. (Ramz al-Ḥaqā’iq: 1/409) 'After the cutting': i.e., after the cutting of the first thief's hand. So if before the cutting, both he and the owner have the claim. (Ṭ)
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the condition for implementing the hadd punishment of cutting the hand for theft. It states that cutting is only carried out upon the request of the victim (the owner of the stolen property). If someone steals from a non-owner (e.g., a depositary), the cutting is still done at the owner's request, not the depositary's. The text also discusses the case of a second thief who steals from the first thief after the latter's hand has been cut: neither the first thief nor the original owner can request cutting of the second thief, because the stolen property is no longer a valid consideration (muttaqawwim) for the first thief after his punishment. There is a dispute with Zufar and al-Shafi'i regarding the case of a missing seller (in a riba transaction), where the Hanafi school cuts the buyer's hand if the dirhams are stolen from him, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree. The relied-upon position is that of the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). The passage also notes that there is a narration from Ibn Samā‘a that in the absence of a depositor, the thief is not cut because the thief did not steal from the owner directly. However, the apparent narration (zāhir al-riwāya) holds that the owner's right is sufficient and the depositary's claim is derived from the owner, so the cut is applied. The key principle is that the property must be mutaqawwim at the time of theft and the victim must demand the punishment.

Key Terms
القطع (al-qaṭ‘): The hadd punishment of cutting the hand of a thief.
بطلب المالك (bi-ṭalab al-mālik): The condition that the victim (owner) demands the punishment; cutting is not automatic.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): A property that is legally valuable and protected such that its destruction entails liability.

B) Only the owner

D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The hadd cutting depends on the victim's demand; the property must be mutaqawwim for the victim at the time of theft.

Four Cases Where the Hadd of Cutting Is Dropped
Four scenarios negating cutting after theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios where the hadd of cutting is not applied and explain the Hanafi reasoning for each, including the intra-madhhab disagreement on price drop.

Core Idea
Cutting is dropped if the stolen property is returned, owned by thief, claimed as own, or drops below nisab before execution.

The hadd of cutting for theft is a severe punishment, but the Hanafi school recognizes that certain events after the theft can create doubts that suspend it. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq outlines four specific cases where the punishment is not carried out, even if the theft was initially proven. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for applying hudud correctly and avoiding unjust punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Scenarios
Read the passage and list the four scenarios where cutting is not performed: (1) return before litigation, (2) thief acquires ownership after judgment, (3) thief claims ownership, (4) value drops below nisab after judgment.
All four are introduced by 'or' (أو) in the text.
2
Analyze the Shubha in Each Scenario
For each scenario, identify the factor that creates a doubt (shubhah) sufficient to drop the hadd. For example, claim of ownership creates ambiguity about rightful possession.
Shubhah is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh for averting hudud.
3
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
Note that Imam Muhammad holds cutting should still apply in the fourth scenario (price drop) because nisab was met at the time of theft. Compare with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf who require nisab at execution.
This ikhtilaf illustrates the importance of the timing of conditions.
4
Understand the Opposing School's View
Al-Shafi'i (and others) hold that cutting is applied in the third scenario (claim of ownership) unless the thief provides proof, to prevent loopholes. The Hanafi response is that the claim itself creates shubhah.
The difference stems from how each school weighs the probability of truth.
5
Apply the Rule to a Hypothetical
Practice: A thief steals goods worth 12 dirhams. Before trial, he returns them to the owner's employee (not in the owner's household). Does cutting apply? Use the passage: returning to non-household does not waive cutting, so it still applies.
The passage specifies return to owner, his child, or household dependents.

And whoever stole something and returned it before litigation... to its owner, or he owned it after judgment, or he claimed it was his, or its value decreased from the nisab—the thief is not cut even if he did not prove. And if two confessed to theft then one of them said 'It is my property,' they are not cut... (continues) The third issue: he indicated by his statement 'or claimed': i.e., if he stole something then the thief claimed... to its owner or his child or his relatives who are in his household. Or he owned it: i.e., the thief owned the stolen object after the judgment of cutting, even if the ownership was by gift and delivery. From the nisab: due to a decrease in price in the place of litigation after the judgment and before the cutting. 'He is not cut': the answer to the four issues, i.e., in all of them.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents four scenarios in which the hadd punishment of cutting is not applied despite an initial valid theft. These are: (1) the thief returns the stolen property to the owner before any legal claim is made; (2) the thief acquires ownership of the stolen object (e.g., by gift or sale) after the judge has ruled for cutting but before it is executed; (3) the thief claims ownership of the object (even without proof) after it has been established by witnesses; and (4) the value of the stolen object falls below the nisab (minimum threshold) due to a change in market price after judgment but before cutting. In each case, the cutting is waived. The text includes disputes: Imam Muhammad holds that cutting is still applied in the fourth case (based on the value at the time of theft), but the Hanafi position is that the condition of nisab must persist at the time of execution. The passage also shows that if the thief returns the property to the owner's dependents (e.g., his wife, child under his care), it is considered as returned to the owner, so cutting is waived before litigation. However, returning to someone not in the owner's household (e.g., an employee) does not count. The general principle is that the punishment of cutting is suspended by any factor that creates a doubt (shubha) or removes the legal interest (mutaqawwim) at the time of execution.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khuṣūmah): The legal claim or litigation initiated by the victim against the thief.
النصاب (al-niṣāb): The minimum value of stolen property required for the hadd of cutting; typically 10 dirhams or more.
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt that prevents the hadd punishment; in this passage, the thief's claim of ownership creates a shubhah.
الإمضاء (al-imḍā’): Execution of the punishment; in the context of the fourth scenario, the condition of nisab must be present at execution.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of cutting for theft is suspended by any doubt (shubha) or change in conditions that arises after the theft but before execution, as illustrated by these four specific scenarios.

Joint Confession and Ownership Claim
Rulings on multiple thieves and their confessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two thieves confess jointly and one claims ownership, and explain the change in Abu Hanifa's opinion regarding the absent thief.

Core Idea
A claim of ownership after joint confession creates shubhah that drops the hadd for both thieves.

This lesson examines a scenario where two thieves confess to the same theft, but later one claims the stolen property is his. The Hanafi school rules that both are spared from the hadd due to the doubt introduced by the claim. We also explore Abu Hanifa's evolving opinion on the case where one thief is absent and witnesses testify, showing how the school refines its reasoning over time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two thieves confess jointly to stealing an item. Later, one of them says 'the item is mine.' The confession is joint, not independent.
The claim of ownership is made after the joint confession but before or after judgment.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Both thieves are not cut; the hadd of theft is dropped entirely. This is because the claim of ownership creates a shubhah (doubt) that prevents the certainty required for applying the hadd.
The ruling applies whether the claim is made before or after the qadi's judgment, as long as the hadd has not been executed.
3
Understand the legal basis
The hadd is dropped by shubhah. The Prophet (ﷺ) said: 'Avert the hudud by doubts.' The claim of ownership, even if weak, introduces a possibility that the thief had a right to the property, thus nullifying the certainty of theft.
Hanafi law gives great weight to shubhah in hudud cases.
4
Distinguish the absent-thief scenario
If two thieves steal, one is absent, and two witnesses testify against both in the presence of the present thief, the present thief is cut (Abu Hanifa's later opinion). The absent thief's absence does not create a shubhah because the possibility of a doubt from the absent thief is not concrete.
Earlier, Abu Hanifa held that the present thief should not be cut because the absent thief might later claim ownership. He retracted this view.
5
Note the change in Abu Hanifa's opinion
Initially, Abu Hanifa considered the mere possibility of a future claim as shubhah. Later, he ruled that such a possibility is not a valid shubhah because it cannot be proven. This shows the school's commitment to actual doubt over hypothetical doubt.
The final mufta bihi opinion is that the present thief is cut.

And if two confessed to theft then one of them said 'It is my property,' they are not cut. And if two stole and one of them is absent, and two witnesses testified against both, the other (the present one) is cut. And if a slave confessed to theft, he is cut. (commentary) 'One of the two thieves' if two confessed... i.e., they both had stolen. And he is the present one if two stole... a morally responsible slave, whether permitted (ma’dhūn) or prohibited (maḥjūr) in the view of Abu Hanifa: that this stolen object is owned by him after two witnesses testified against him, he is not cut for the shubhah. And al-Shafi‘i said: he is cut unless there is a clear proof, so that the door of hadd is not closed. (Mukhtaṣar) 'It is my property' whether they claimed before judgment or after it before execution. (ʿAynī) 'And one of them is absent': i.e., if two men stole then one of them is absent, then two witnesses testified against their theft, the other (present) is cut according to Abu Hanifa, which is his later opinion. He used to say initially: not cut, because if he were present perhaps he would claim a doubt (shubhah) at his presence. Then he retracted and said: cut, because absence prevents proving theft against the absent one, so it remains non-existent, and the non-existent does not create a shubhah. So no consideration is given to the possibility of a shubhah occurring. (Mukhtaṣar, Fatḥ) 'And they testified' in the active voice: i.e., two witnesses testified in the presence of the other. (Miskīn ‘Abd etc.): the issue has aspects; because either the slave is permitted or prohibited, and the property is either extant in his hand or destroyed. If he is permitted, his confession is valid regarding the right of cutting and the property, so his hand is cut and the property is returned to the victim if it is extant; if destroyed, no liability.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage deals with multiple thieves and confessions. First, if two people confess to a theft and then one of them claims the stolen property is his, both are not cut because the confession was joint and the claim creates a doubt (shubhah) about theft. Second, if two thieves steal and one is absent, and witnesses testify against both, the present thief is cut. Abu Hanifa initially held that the present thief is not cut because the absent one might have a defense (shubhah), but he later retracted to the view that absence does not create a shubhah, so the present thief is cut. Third, the case of a slave: if a slave confesses to theft, his hand is cut. There is a dispute with al-Shafi‘i: if the slave claims ownership after witnesses testify, Abu Hanifa says he is not cut due to shubhah, while al-Shafi‘i says he is cut to prevent the hadd from being blocked. The text also notes that if a slave is permitted (ma’dhūn) to trade, his confession is valid regarding both the hadd and the property; if the property is still in his hand, it is returned to the victim; if destroyed, no liability.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt in the evidence or circumstances that causes the hadd punishment to be dropped.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession of the thief, which can establish the hadd if not contradicted.
مأذون (ma’dhūn): A slave who has permission from his master to engage in trade; his legal acts are valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He is not cut until the absent thief is found

Key Takeaway
A claim of ownership after joint confession creates shubhah that drops the hadd for both thieves; absence of a thief does not create a valid shubhah.

Slave's Confession and the Shubhah Defense
Rulings on slave's confession and the shubhah defense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that a slave's claim of ownership after confession drops the hadd due to shubhah, and evaluate the Shafi‘i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A slave's hand is spared if he claims ownership after confessing, as shubhah overrides the hadd.

This lesson addresses the specific case of a slave who confesses to theft and later claims the stolen item is his. The Hanafi school gives weight to this claim as a doubt (shubhah) that prevents the cutting of the hand, while the Shafi‘i school argues for enforcement to keep the hadd effective. We also examine the conditions under which a slave's confession is valid, particularly the distinction between a permitted (ma’dhūn) and prohibited (maḥjūr) slave.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A slave confesses to theft. Two witnesses testify against him. Then the slave claims the stolen item belongs to him.
The confession occurs first; the claim of ownership comes after the witnesses testify.
2
Identify the Hanafi ruling
The slave is not cut; the hadd is dropped because of shubhah (doubt) from his claim of ownership.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa, and it is the mufta bihi.
3
Identify the opposing view (Shafi‘i)
Al-Shafi‘i holds that the slave should be cut, unless there is clear proof (bayyinah) that the property is his. Otherwise, the door to hadd would be closed entirely.
The Shafi‘i concern is that false claims would always be made to escape punishment.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school maintains that the hadd is averted by doubts. The claim of ownership, even if uncorroborated, introduces a possibility that the slave had a right to the property. Certainty is required for the hadd, and doubt overrides it.
The Hanafi position prioritizes the principle of leniency in hudud over the fear of false claims.
5
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In a Hanafi court, the slave's hand is not cut; in a Shafi‘i court, it is cut. This difference is significant for the administration of justice.
The dispute is legitimate; both schools rely on valid principles.

Source Text

And if two confessed to theft then one of them said 'It is my property,' they are not cut. And if two stole and one of them is absent, and two witnesses testified against both, the other (the present one) is cut. And if a slave confessed to theft, he is cut. (commentary) 'One of the two thieves' if two confessed... i.e., they both had stolen. And he is the present one if two stole... a morally responsible slave, whether permitted (ma’dhūn) or prohibited (maḥjūr) in the view of Abu Hanifa: that this stolen object is owned by him after two witnesses testified against him, he is not cut for the shubhah. And al-Shafi‘i said: he is cut unless there is a clear proof, so that the door of hadd is not closed. (Mukhtaṣar) 'It is my property' whether they claimed before judgment or after it before execution. (ʿAynī) 'And one of them is absent': i.e., if two men stole then one of them is absent, then two witnesses testified against their theft, the other (present) is cut according to Abu Hanifa, which is his later opinion. He used to say initially: not cut, because if he were present perhaps he would claim a doubt (shubhah) at his presence. Then he retracted and said: cut, because absence prevents proving theft against the absent one, so it remains non-existent, and the non-existent does not create a shubhah. So no consideration is given to the possibility of a shubhah occurring. (Mukhtaṣar, Fatḥ) 'And they testified' in the active voice: i.e., two witnesses testified in the presence of the other. (Miskīn ‘Abd etc.): the issue has aspects; because either the slave is permitted or prohibited, and the property is either extant in his hand or destroyed. If he is permitted, his confession is valid regarding the right of cutting and the property, so his hand is cut and the property is returned to the victim if it is extant; if destroyed, no liability.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage deals with multiple thieves and confessions. First, if two people confess to a theft and then one of them claims the stolen property is his, both are not cut because the confession was joint and the claim creates a doubt (shubhah) about theft. Second, if two thieves steal and one is absent, and witnesses testify against both, the present thief is cut. Abu Hanifa initially held that the present thief is not cut because the absent one might have a defense (shubhah), but he later retracted to the view that absence does not create a shubhah, so the present thief is cut. Third, the case of a slave: if a slave confesses to theft, his hand is cut. There is a dispute with al-Shafi‘i: if the slave claims ownership after witnesses testify, Abu Hanifa says he is not cut due to shubhah, while al-Shafi‘i says he is cut to prevent the hadd from being blocked. The text also notes that if a slave is permitted (ma’dhūn) to trade, his confession is valid regarding both the hadd and the property; if the property is still in his hand, it is returned to the victim; if destroyed, no liability.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt in the evidence or circumstances that causes the hadd punishment to be dropped.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession of the thief, which can establish the hadd if not contradicted.
مأذون (ma’dhūn): A slave who has permission from his master to engage in trade; his legal acts are valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Cut because confession is valid
B) Not cut because of shubhah
C) Cut only if the property is destroyed
D) Not cut because slaves cannot be punished

Key Takeaway
A slave's claim of ownership after confession creates a doubt that drops the hadd, a key Hanafi application of the shubhah principle.

Non-combination of Amputation and Compensation in Theft
Ruling and proof that amputation and compensation are mutually exclusive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that amputation and compensation do not combine in theft, explain the proof from the hadith 'La ghurm...', and identify the intra-Hanafi differences.

Core Idea
A thief either loses his hand or pays compensation—never both.

This lesson from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a key principle in the law of theft: the mutual exclusivity of the hadd penalty (amputation) and financial compensation for the stolen property. We will examine the ruling, the intra-Hanafi differences, and the proof from the hadith, as well as the response to objections about its chain and meaning.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the core legal principle: amputation (qat') and compensation (daman) cannot be combined. The thief either loses his hand or pays, never both.
This applies whether the property is destroyed or exists, and whether the thief is wealthy or poor.
2
Locate the proof
Find the dalil in the passage: the hadith 'لا غرم على السارق بعد قطع يمينه' reported by al-Daraqutni.
The Hanafis rely on this hadith despite chain criticisms.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the hadith proves the ruling: the word 'ghurm' (liability) is indefinite in a negative context, indicating generality—all forms of compensation are negated after amputation.
This is a Hanafi usul principle: al-nakirah fi siyaq al-nafi tufid al-umum.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note the three positions attributed to Abu Hanifa: (1) amputation is principal, property subsidiary; (2) property is principal; (3) both are independent. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad each adopted one view.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that they do not combine, following the hadith.
5
Compare with other schools
Contrast the Hanafi position with al-Shafi'i (combine always), Malik (combine if wealthy), and Ahmad/Zufar (combine always). The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the thief pays compensation in addition to amputation.
Under Shafi'i, both rights are fulfilled; under Hanafi, only amputation.

Source Text

كيفية القطع وإثباته
وتردّ السرقة إلى المسروق منه، ولا يجتمع قطع وضمان،
أي قطع يد السارق
صدقه مولاه أو كذبه، وإن كان محجوراً والمال هالك يقطع ولا يضمن كذبه مولاه أو صدقه، وإن كان قائماً وصدقه مولاه يقطع عندنا، ويرد المال على المسروق منه، وإن كذبه وقال المولى: المال مالي، قال أبو حنيفة: تقطع يده، والمال للمسروق منه؛ لأن المال تابع للقطع، وقال أبو يوسف والشافعي: تقطع يده والمال للمولى؛ لأن كلاً من القطع والمال أصل، وقال محمد: لا تقطع والمال للمولى؛ لأن المال عنده هو الأصل، وقال زفر: يصح إقراره بالمال إن كان مأذوناً، وإن كان محجوراً لا يصح إقراره بالمال، ولا يصح إقراره في حق القطع مأذوناً كان أو محجوراً؛ لأن إقراره بالقطع يتضرر به المولى، فلا يقبل إقراره عليه.
قلنا: صحة إقراره عليه من حيث أنه آدمي ثم يتعدى إلى المالية في ضمنه فيصح، وحاصل هذا الخلاف راجع إلى أن المال أصل أو القطع أو كلاهما، فعند أبي حنيفة: القطع هو الأصل والمال تابع، وعند محمد: المال هو الأصل فلا يثبت القطع بدونه وعند أبي يوسف كلاهما أصل، وحكى الطحاوي أن الأقاويل الثلاثة مروية عن أبي حنيفة، فقوله الأول أخذ به محمد والثاني أخذ به أبو يوسف. (عيني، فتح) ولا يجتمع قطع وضمان [ أي ضمان المال مطلقاً سواء هلك أو استهلك، وسواء كان السارق موسراً أو معسراً. (ع، مس] كالحد . مع العقر؛ لأن وجوب الضمان ينافي القطع؛ لأن السارق يتملك المسروق بأداء الضمان مستنداً إلى وقت الأخذ، فيتبين أنه ورد على ملكه فينبغي القطع، أي لا يجتمعان مطلقاً . يعني في الهلاك والاستهلاك واليسار والإعسار عندنا، وعند الشافعي: يجمع بينهما مطلقاً؛ لأنهما حقان مختلفان في السبب، فيستوفي كل منهما؛ لأن القطع حق الشرع وسببه ترك الانتهاء عما نهي عنه، والضمان حق العبد، وسببه أخذ المال، وبه قال أحمد وزفر، وعند مالك: يجمع بينهما إذا كان السارق موسراً وإن كان معسراً فلا ولنا: قوله عليه: "لا غرم على السارق بعد قطع يمينه"، أخرجه الدار قطني، رواه أبو حنيفة عن ابن مسعود له، و لم يرد عن غيره خلاف. فإن قلت الحديث ،معلول قال الدار قطني وفي إسناده سعيد بن إبراهيم، وهو مجهول، ويروى بطرق كلها لا تثبت، ولو سلم احتمل أنه أراد بنفي الغرم نفي العذاب في الآخرة، ويحتمل أنه أراد أجرة الحداد، ويحتمل أنه أراد نفي الضمان أبداً كما كان في بدء الإسلام، ومع هذه الاحتمالات لا يمكن الاحتجاج به لنفي الضمان؟ قلت: قول الدار قطني لا يقبل إذا انفرد، وإنما تكلموا في الحديث من حيث إسناده؛ لأنه رواه المسور عن عبد الرحمن بن عوف والمسور لم يلقه وهذا إن ثبت فهو صفة الإرسال والمرسل حجة، والنبي عليه ذكر الغرم منكراً في موضع النفي، والنكرة في موضع النفي تعم، فينتفي عنه جميع أنواع الغرم، وقال الفقيه أبو الليث: روي عن محمد ابن الحسن لله أنه قال: إنما لم يجب عليه الضمان في الحكم والقضاء، وأما ديانة فالضمان واجب، وهذا القول أحسن. [رمز الحقائق: ٤١١/١] (فتح)
Book of Theft. Section: How amputation is carried out and its proof. The stolen property is returned to the victim, and amputation and compensation do not combine. (Meaning: amputation of the hand of the thief. If his master confirms or denies his confession; and if he is under interdiction and the property is lost, he is amputated and no compensation is paid whether his master denies or confirms. If the property is still in existence and his master confirms, he is amputated in our view, and the property is returned to the victim. If his master denies and says: the property is mine, Abu Hanifa says: his hand is amputated and the property is for the victim because the property is subsidiary to the amputation. Abu Yusuf and Al-Shafi'i say: his hand is amputated and the property is for the master because each of amputation and property is an independent basis. Muhammad says: he is not amputated and the property is for the master because in his view the property is the basis. Zufar says: his confession regarding the property is valid if he is permitted, but if he is under interdiction his confession regarding the property is not valid, and his confession regarding amputation is not valid whether he is permitted or under interdiction because his confession to amputation harms the master, so it is not accepted against him. We said: the validity of his confession against him is from the perspective that he is a human, then it extends to the financial matter implicitly, so it is valid. The gist of this disagreement reverts to whether the property is the basis, or the amputation, or both. According to Abu Hanifa: amputation is the basis and property is subsidiary. According to Muhammad: property is the basis, so amputation is not established without it. According to Abu Yusuf: both are basis. Al-Tahawi reported that the three sayings are transmitted from Abu Hanifa: his first saying was adopted by Muhammad, and the second by Abu Yusuf. (Ayni, Fath) And amputation and compensation do not combine [i.e., compensation of the property absolutely whether destroyed or consumed, and whether the thief is wealthy or poor. (?, Mus) like the hadd with the dowry? Because the obligation of compensation contradicts amputation; because the thief owns the stolen property by paying compensation dating back to the time of taking, so it becomes clear that it came upon his property, so amputation is called for. i.e., they do not combine absolutely, meaning in destruction, consumption, wealth and poverty according to us. According to Al-Shafi'i: they are combined absolutely because they are two rights with different causes, so each is fulfilled; because amputation is a right of the Law and its cause is not refraining from what is prohibited, and compensation is a right of the servant and its cause is taking the property. Ahmad and Zufar agreed with this. According to Malik: they are combined if the thief is wealthy, but if he is poor then not. And our proof is his saying (peace be upon him): 'There is no liability on the thief after amputation of his right hand', reported by Al-Daraqutni, narrated by Abu Hanifa from Ibn Mas'ud, and no disagreement is known from others. If you say the hadith is defective, Al-Daraqutni said its chain includes Sa'id ibn Ibrahim, who is unknown, and it is narrated through ways all of which are not established; even if accepted, it is possible he meant by negation of liability the negation of punishment in the Hereafter, or he meant the fee of the blacksmith, or he meant the negation of compensation forever as it was in early Islam; with these possibilities it cannot be used as proof for negating compensation? I say: the saying of Al-Daraqutni is not accepted when he is alone, and they only criticized the hadith from the perspective of its chain because Al-Miswar narrated from Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and Al-Miswar did not meet him; if this is established, it is a feature of mursal, and mursal is a proof. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) mentioned 'liability' in the indefinite form in a negative context, and an indefinite in a negative context is general, so all types of liability are negated for him. And Faqih Abu al-Layth said: it is narrated from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan that he said: compensation is not obligatory upon him in legal judgment and judiciary, but as a matter of religious duty, compensation is obligatory. This saying is better. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/411] (Fath)
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage begins the Book of Theft (Kitab al-Sariqa) in Kanz al-Daqa'iq, presenting the ruling on amputation (qat') and compensation (daman). The core ruling is that amputation for theft and financial compensation cannot be combined in any circumstance: if the thief is amputated, he does not pay compensation, and vice versa. The commentary first clarifies that the stolen property is returned to the victim. It then delves into a complex case involving a slave thief and his master's confirmation or denial of his confession. The school's positions diverge: Abu Hanifa considers amputation the principal and property subsidiary, so if the master denies ownership, the hand is cut and property goes to the victim; Abu Yusuf and Al-Shafi'i treat both as independent, so property goes to the master even if hand is cut; Muhammad considers property the principal, so no amputation; Zufar distinguishes between permission and interdiction. The commentator notes that all three views are attributed to Abu Hanifa by Al-Tahawi. The main proof is the hadith 'No liability on the thief after amputation of his right hand,' with detailed defense against objections about chain weakness and possible interpretations. Finally, Abu al-Layth's opinion that compensation is waived judicially but not religiously is cited as preferred.

Key Terms
قطع (qat'): Amputation of the hand as the prescribed penalty for theft.
ضمان (daman): Financial compensation for the stolen property.
محجور (mahjur): A person under legal interdiction, such as a slave or a minor, whose legal actions are restricted.
لا يجتمع قطع وضمان (la yajtami' qat' wa daman): The principle that amputation and compensation are mutually exclusive in theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The hand is cut and the property goes to the master.

D) The hand is cut and the property is destroyed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school definitively rules that amputation and compensation do not combine for theft, based on the hadith and the principle that the hadd takes precedence over civil liability.

Return of the Stolen Item After Amputation: The 'Ayn and Compensation
Ruling on the stolen item after amputation and intra-Hanafi difference on compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning the stolen item after amputation and distinguish when compensation is required.

Core Idea
The stolen item is returned if it exists; if destroyed after amputation, no compensation combines with the hadd.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents the rule regarding the stolen property after the thief has been amputated. The central issue is whether the thief must return the actual item or pay its value if it is gone. The Hanafi school establishes a clear distinction: the item ('ayn) is returned if it remains, but if it is destroyed, amputation and compensation do not combine. However, there is an internal difference on whether a fatwa can be given for compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Locate the phrase 'وتردّ العين لو قائماً'. This tells us that the specific stolen item must be returned if it is still in existence.
2
Understand the condition
The commentary explains that 'if it still exists' means the item is in the thief's possession after amputation. It remains on the owner's ownership.
If the item is destroyed or consumed, different rules apply.
3
Analyze the compensation ikhtilaf
The text presents three views: from Abu Hanifa via Abu Yusuf (no compensation, mashhur), via al-Hasan (compensation), and from Muhammad (fatwa for value but not enforced). Trace the reasoning.
The well-known view avoids combining compensation with hadd.
4
Apply the tarjih
The mufta bihi is no compensation after amputation, though the fatwa for value is sometimes given. Also note the before/after amputation distinction: if owner demands compensation before cut, no hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة
١٤٤
كيفية القطع وإثباته
وتردّ العين لو قائماً. ولو قطع لبعض السرقات لا يضمن شيئاً. ولو شق ما سرق في الدار ثم أخرجه قطع.

ولو قائماً : أي إذا قطع السارق وكان المسروق قائم في يده يرد إلى مالكه؛ لأنه باق على ملكه، وإن كان هالكاً أو مستهلكاً لم يضمن في رواية أبي يوسف عن أبي حنيفة وهو المشهور، وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة يضمن، وعن ابن سماعة عن محمد: أنه يفتى بأداء القيمة؛ لأنه أتلف مالاً محظوراً بغير حق، ولا يحكم به؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى إيجاب ما ينافي القطع، وفي "الكافي": هذا إذا كان بعد القطع، وإن كان قبله فإن قال المالك: أنا أضمنه، لم يقطع عندنا، فإنه يتضمن رجوعه عن دعوى المال، وإن قال: أنا أختار القطع، يقطع ولا يضمن. رمز الحقائق: ٤١٢/١] (مستخلص، فتح)
Book of Theft. Section: How amputation is carried out and its proof. The specific item (al-'ayn) is returned if it still exists. And if he is amputated for some thefts, he does not compensate anything. And if he tears what he stole inside the house then takes it out, amputation applies. 'If it still exists' means: when the thief is amputated and the stolen item is still in his hand, it is returned to its owner because it remains on his ownership. If it is destroyed or consumed, he does not compensate in the narration of Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, which is the well-known view. In the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, he compensates. From Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad: that a fatwa is given for payment of value because he destroyed property that was prohibited without right, but it is not judged by it because it leads to imposing what contradicts amputation. In al-Kafi: this applies after amputation. If before amputation, if the owner says: I guarantee it, he is not amputated according to us, for it implies his withdrawal from the claim of property. If he says: I choose amputation, he is amputated and does not compensate. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/412] (Mukhtalaf, Fath)
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on the stolen item after the thief has been amputated. The main principle is that if the stolen property ('ayn) is still in existence and in the thief's possession after the amputation, it must be returned to its rightful owner because it never left his ownership. If the item is destroyed or consumed, the well-known view from Abu Hanifa (via Abu Yusuf) is that no compensation is paid, to maintain the principle that amputation and compensation do not combine. However, there is a narration from Muhammad that a fatwa may be given for paying the value, though it is not enforced judicially. The commentary also distinguishes between the situation before and after amputation: if the owner demands compensation before the cut, the thief is not amputated (since the owner's claim of property is abandoned), but if the owner insists on amputation, the cut proceeds without compensation.

Key Terms
عين ('ayn): The physical stolen property itself, as opposed to its value.
هالك (halik): Destroyed (perished) without trace.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known Hanafi view, what happens if the stolen item is destroyed after the thief's amputation?
A) The thief must pay the full value.
B) The thief must pay no compensation.
C) The thief must pay half the value.
D) The thief is imprisoned instead.

Key Takeaway
The stolen item is returned if it exists; if destroyed after amputation, no compensation is required in the well-known Hanafi view.

Amputation for One Theft Exempts Compensation for Others
Ruling that amputation for one theft exempts compensation for other simultaneous thefts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling that amputation for one theft exempts compensation for all prior thefts and distinguish it from the position of the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
One amputation wipes out liability for all prior thefts — a single divine penalty suffices.

The hadd penalty for theft is amputation of the hand. But what if a thief has committed multiple thefts before being caught and amputated for only one? Does he still owe compensation to the other victims? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses that question, revealing a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement and a cross-madhhab difference. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi view that amputation is a comprehensive divine right that settles all prior thefts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the core ruling
The text presents a thief who has committed multiple thefts (sariqat) and is then amputated for only one of them. The core ruling under Abu Hanifa is that he does not compensate anything for the remaining thefts.
The amputation is wajib as a hadd penalty.
2
State the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the thief must compensate for all thefts except the one for which he was amputated. Understand their reasoning: amputation only applies to the litigated case; the present victim does not represent absent victims.
This disagreement is based on whether amputation covers all thefts or only the adjudicated one.
3
Analyze the proofs for each position
For Abu Hanifa: amputation is a single right of Allah (haqq Allah) that, once fulfilled, suffices for all prior thefts. For the Sahibayn: amputation removes protection from property only for the litigated theft; other victims' property remains protected.
The disagreement is rooted in the nature of the hadd penalty — is it a comprehensive expiation or a specific punishment?
4
Identify the cross-madhhab disagreement
Al-Shafi'i opposes even the consensus position: when all victims are present and litigation covers all thefts, Shafi'i still requires compensation for unlitigated thefts. This highlights a fundamental difference in the theory of hadd.
The Hanafi consensus (Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn) agrees when all victims are present — no compensation. Shafi'i dissents.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
The relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's view: amputation for one theft exempts compensation for all others. This is considered more lenient and consistent with the principle that the hadd is a divine right that settles all claims.
The text presents Abu Hanifa's view as primary, with the Sahibayn's as an alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قطع لبعض السرقات لا يضمن شيئاً. ولو شق ما سرق في الدار ثم أخرجه قطع.

ولو قطع لبعض إلخ : أي من سرق سرقات فقطع في إحداها فهذا القطع لجميعها ولا يضمن شيئاً للآخرين مطلقاً عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا يضمن كلها غير التي قطع بها. ومعنى المسألة إذا حضر أحدهم فقطع بخصومته، إذا حضروا جميعاً فقطع بخصومتهم لا ضمان عليه بالاتفاق خلافاً للشافعي لهما: أن المسقط للضمان القطع، وهو حصل للحاضر والحاضر ليس بنائب عن الغائب فلم يوجد لغيره فبقيت أموالهم معصومة، وله أن الواجب بالكل قطع واحد حقاً لله تعالى، فإذا استوفى كان للكل، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا سرق من واحد نصباً مراراً، ثم قطع لأجل نصاب واحد [ رمز الحقائق: ٤١٢/١] (مستخلص)
And if he is amputated for some thefts, he does not compensate anything. And if he tears what he stole inside the house then takes it out, amputation applies. 'And if he is amputated for some' etc.: meaning one who steals multiple thefts and is amputated for one of them, this amputation serves for all of them and he does not compensate anything for the others absolutely according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: he compensates for all except that for which he was amputated. The meaning of the issue: if one (victim) is present and he is amputated due to his litigation, if all are present and he is amputated due to their litigation, there is no compensation by agreement, contrary to al-Shafi'i. For the two: that which removes compensation is amputation, and it occurred for the present one, and the present one is not a deputy for the absent one, so it did not occur for others, so their property remains protected. For him (Abu Hanifa): the obligatory is one amputation as a right of Allah Most High, so when it is fulfilled, it is for all. And upon this disagreement is the case if he steals from one person a nisab repeatedly, then is amputated for one nisab. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/412] (Mukhtalaf)
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a thief steals multiple items from different owners (or multiple times from one owner) and is then amputated for only one of the thefts. According to Abu Hanifa, the single amputation satisfies all thefts, and he pays no compensation for the others. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two) hold that he must compensate for all thefts except the one for which he was amputated. The commentary explains the reasoning: the two view amputation as a penalty that only applies to the specific theft litigated, while Abu Hanifa sees amputation as a single divine right that covers all thefts committed before the amputation. If all victims appear and litigation covers all thefts, there is consensus that no compensation is due. The same principle applies if a single victim suffers multiple thefts reaching the nisab threshold; amputation for one nisab exempts compensation for others.

Key Terms
سرقات (sariqat): Multiple thefts (plural of sariqa) committed by the same thief.
قطع (qat'): Amputation of the hand as the hadd penalty for theft.
ضمان (daman): Compensation (monetary liability) for stolen property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a thief steals from three different people and is amputated for one theft, what happens regarding compensation for the other two?
A) He must compensate both.
B) He need not compensate either.
C) He compensates one of them.
D) He is amputated again for the other two.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's ruling that a single amputation exempts the thief from compensating any other thefts committed before it is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
