Tearing the Stolen Garment Inside the House Before Removal
Ruling on amputation when thief tears garment inside house before removal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation when a thief tears a garment inside the house, and distinguish between substantial (fahish) and slight (yasir) damage using the correct criteria.

Core Idea
Amputation applies if torn pieces equal nisab and damage is slight; substantial damage creates shubha preventing amputation if full compensation is taken.

Theft from a protected place (hirz) normally triggers the hadd of amputation if the value reaches the nisab. But what if the thief tears the garment inside the house before carrying it out? Does the tearing affect the punishment? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling and the subtle distinction between substantial and slight damage—a critical nuance that determines whether the hand is cut or spared.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is tearing a stolen garment inside the house before removal. The ruling is amputation if the torn pieces together reach the nisab and the damage is slight.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: amputation applies. Abu Yusuf: no amputation because substantial tearing creates ownership by liability, raising a shubha. Understand the legal reasoning behind each view.
3
Apply the criteria for fahish (substantial) vs. yasir (slight)
Distinguish between damage that removes part of substance and utility (fahish) and damage that only impairs appearance (yasir). The correct view: fahish removes some 'ayn and manfa'a; yasir removes none.
Multiple criteria exist: quarter-value, half-value, or unsuitability. The correct view is the one that best captures the essence.
4
Evaluate the owner's choice
If the owner claims only the decrease (takes garment), amputation is disputed. If he claims full value (leaves garment), amputation is prevented by consensus.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: amputation applies when damage is slight, because tearing does not legally transfer ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شق ما سرق في الدار ثم أخرجه قطع.

ثم أخرجه أي من الدار، وقيمته بعد الشق عشرة دراهم. (عيني) قطع أي من سرق ثوباً فشقه في الدار نصفين ثم أخرجه من الدار قطع إذا كان المشقوق تبلغ نصاباً عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: لا يقطع؛ لأن ملكاً له بسبب الخرق الفاحش؛ لأنه يجب عليه قيمته فيملك المضمون، ولهما: أن الأخذ مع الخرق وضع سبباً للضمان، وليس سبباً للملك شرعاً، وإنما يثبت الملك ضرورة أن يجتمع البدلان في ملك واحد، ومثله يورث الشبهة كنفس الأخذ وهذا الخلاف فيما إذا اختار تضمين النقصان وأخذ الثوب، وإن اختار تضمين القيمة وترك الثوب عليه لا يقطع اتفاقاً، وإن كان النقصان يسيراً قطع بالإجماع، وتكلموا في الفرق بين الفاحش واليسير، فقيل: إن أوجب الفرق نقصان ربع القيمة فصاعداً فهو فاحش، وما دونه يسير. وقيل: ما لا يصلح الباقي لثوب فهو فاحش واليسير ما يصلح وقيل ما ينتقص به نصف القيمة فاحش، وما دونه يسير وما فوقه استهلاك، والصحيح أن الفاحش ما يفوت به بعض العين وبعض المنفعة، واليسير ما لا يفوت به شيء من المنفعة بل يتعيب به فقط، وهذا الخيار يثبت ما لم يكن إتلافاً، وإذا كان إتلافاً فله تضمين جميع القيمة من غير خيار، ويملك السارق الثوب ولا يقطع رمز الحقائق: ١/ ٤١٣] (مستخلص)
And if he tears what he stole inside the house then takes it out, amputation applies. 'Then takes it out' i.e., from the house, and its value after tearing is ten dirhams. (Ayni) 'Amputation applies' i.e., one who steals a garment and tears it in half inside the house then takes it out of the house, he is amputated if the torn pieces reach the nisab according to both (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: he is not amputated because he gains ownership due to the substantial tearing; because he is liable for its value, so he owns what is guaranteed. For them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): taking with tearing sets a cause for liability, not a cause for ownership legally; ownership is only established by necessity that two compensations be in one hand, and similar to it is that it creates doubt (shubha) like the taking itself. This disagreement applies when he chooses to guarantee the decrease and take the garment; if he chooses to guarantee the full value and leave the garment, he is not amputated by agreement. And if the decrease is slight, he is amputated by consensus. They discussed the difference between substantial and slight. It was said: if the decrease causes a loss of a quarter of the value or more, it is substantial; less is slight. It was said: if the remainder is not suitable as a garment, it is substantial; if suitable, slight. It was said: if it decreases by half the value, it is substantial; less is slight; more is destruction. The correct view is that substantial is that which causes loss of part of the substance and part of the utility; slight is that which causes no loss of utility but only impairs it. This choice exists as long as it is not destruction; if it is destruction, then he can claim full value without choice, and the thief owns the garment and is not amputated. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/413] (Mukhtalaf)
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage addresses a case where a thief tears a stolen garment inside the house before removing it. The ruling is that amputation applies if the torn pieces together equal the nisab (minimum value for the theft penalty). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad agree on this, while Abu Yusuf argues that the substantial tearing creates ownership by liability, thus creating a shubha (doubt) that prevents amputation. The commentary explains the legal reasoning: tearing does not transfer ownership legally; it only creates liability. The dispute hinges on the extent of damage: if the owner chooses to claim only the decrease and take the garment, amputation is disputed; if he claims full value and leaves the garment, there is consensus that amputation is not applied. The passage then discusses how to distinguish substantial (fahish) from slight (yasir) damage, with various criteria: quarter-value, half-value, suitability for use. The correct view is that substantial damage removes part of the substance and utility, while slight damage only impairs without removing utility. If the damage amounts to destruction (itlaf), the thief must pay full value and does not lose his hand.

Key Terms
شق (shaqq): Tearing a garment, specifically inside the house before removal.
فاحش (fahish): Substantial damage that renders the item unfit for its original use or removes part of its substance and utility.
يسير (yasir): Slight damage that does not remove utility but only impairs appearance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, when does tearing a garment inside the house prevent amputation?
A) When the tearing is slight.
B) When the tearing is substantial (fahish) and the owner chooses full compensation.
C) When the thief tears the garment into more than two pieces.
D) When the garment is worth less than ten dirhams.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the effect of tearing a stolen garment on amputation depends on whether the damage is substantial (shubha) or slight, and on the owner's choice of compensation.

Return of Stolen Item After Amputation: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Liability
Ruling on returning stolen item after amputation with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the well-known Hanafi position that the thief is not liable for a destroyed stolen item after amputation and explain the alternative views and the owner's choice before amputation.

Core Idea
After amputation, the thief is not liable for a destroyed item in the well-known view; the owner's choice before amputation determines the outcome.

The text addresses what happens to a stolen item after the thief's hand has been amputated. If the item is still intact, it is returned to the owner. But what if it has been destroyed? This passage reveals an important intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether the thief must compensate the owner after the hudud punishment has been carried out. Understanding this dispute clarifies the relationship between criminal punishment and civil liability in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the base ruling
The text states that if the stolen item (al-ʿayn) is still in the thief's hand (qāʾiman) at the time of amputation, it is returned to its owner because ownership remains with the owner.
This rule applies regardless of the dispute; the item is returned if physically present.
2
2. Examine the dispute on liability when destroyed
When the item is destroyed or consumed (hālikan aw mustahlakan), there are three positions within the Hanafi school: (a) the well-known narration from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa: no liability; (b) the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa: liability; (c) the fatwa of Muhammad (via Ibn Sama'a): pay value but not enforced.
Note the difference between a riwāyah (narration) and a fatwa.
3
3. Understand the reasoning behind each position
The well-known view holds that amputation expiates the civil liability (damān). Muhammad's fatwa reasons that destroying inviolable property without right requires compensation, but enforcing it would contradict the amputation.
The conflict is between the severity of the punishment and the protection of property.
4
4. Distinguish before vs. after amputation
The text from al-Kafi clarifies that if the owner demands compensation before amputation, the thief is not amputated because the owner has effectively retracted the theft claim. If the owner insists on amputation, the thief is cut and does not pay compensation.
This gives the owner a choice but not both remedies.
5
5. Determine the muftā bihī (operative view)
The well-known narration (no liability after amputation) is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). Muhammad's fatwa is not enforced in court.
Later Hanafi authorities like al-Kafi support this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَتِرَدُّ العَيْنُ لَوْ قَائِمًا.
قَبْلَ أَنْ يُخْرِجَهُ مِنْهَا السَّارِقُ لَوْ قَائِمًا: أَيْ إِذَا قُطِعَ السَّارِقُ وَكَانَ المَسْرُوقُ قَائِمًا فِي يَدِهِ يُرَدُّ إِلَى مَالِكِهِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ بَاقٍ عَلَى مِلْكِهِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ هَالِكًا أَوْ مُسْتَهْلَكًا لَمْ يَضْمَنْ فِي رِوَايَةِ أَبِي يُوسُفَ عَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَهُوَ المَشْهُورُ، وَفِي رِوَايَةِ الحَسَنِ عَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ يَضْمَنُ، وَعَنِ ابْنِ سَمَاعَةَ عَنْ مُحَمَّدٍ: أَنَّهُ يُفْتَى بِأَدَاءِ القِيمَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ أَتْلَفَ مَالًا مَحْظُورًا بِغَيْرِ حَقٍّ، وَلَا يُحْكَمُ بِهِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يُؤَدِّي إِلَى إِيجَابِ مَا يُنَافِي القَطْعَ، وَفِي "الكَافِي": هَذَا إِذَا كَانَ بَعْدَ القَطْعِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ قَبْلَهُ فَإِنْ قَالَ المَالِكُ: أَنَا أَضْمَنُهُ، لَمْ يُقْطَعْ عِنْدَنَا، فَإِنَّهُ يَتَضَمَّنُ رُجُوعَهُ عَنْ دَعْوَى المَالِ، وَإِنْ قَالَ: أَنَا أَخْتَارُ القَطْعَ، يُقْطَعُ وَلَا يَضْمَنُ. [رَمْزُ الحَقَائِقِ: ٤١٢/١] (مُسْتَخْلَص، فَتْح)
And the item is returned if it is still in hand. Before he takes it out, the thief: 'if still in hand': that is, when the thief is amputated and the stolen item is still in his hand, it is returned to its owner because it remains in his ownership. If it is destroyed or consumed, he is not liable in the narration of Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, and that is the well-known view. In the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, he is liable. From Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad: He issues fatwa for payment of the value because he destroyed prohibited wealth without right, but he is not judged by it because it leads to imposing what is contrary to amputation. In al-Kafi: This is after amputation. If before, and the owner says: 'I guarantee it,' he is not amputated according to us, for it implies his retraction from the claim of property. If he says: 'I choose amputation,' he is amputated and does not guarantee. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/412] (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the return of a stolen item after the thief's hand has been cut. The base ruling (matn) states that if the stolen item is still in the thief's hand (qāʾiman), it is returned to its owner. The commentary explains that this is because the item remains in the owner's ownership. The dispute arises when the item is destroyed or consumed (hālikan aw mustahlakan). According to the well-known narration of Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, the thief is not liable (lā yadman), meaning he does not pay compensation. However, in the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, he is liable. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) is reported by Ibn Sama'a to give a fatwa that the thief must pay the value (al-qīmah), reasoning that he destroyed property which was inviolable (maḥẓūr) without right. Yet, this judgment is not enforced (lā yuḥkamu bihī) because it would impose a liability that contradicts the amputation. A key distinction is made in al-Kafi: if the owner demands compensation before amputation, the thief is not amputated because this implies the owner has retracted his claim of theft. If the owner insists on amputation, the thief is amputated and does not pay compensation. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah (via the well-known narration) but includes later fatwa considerations.

Key Terms
العَيْن (al-ʿayn): The specific stolen object itself (as opposed to its value or replacement).
قَائِمًا (qāʾiman): Still in hand; physically existent and unchanged.
يَضْمَن (yaḍman): To be liable for compensation (damān).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known narration (mashhūr) from Abu Hanifa via Abu Yusuf, what is the ruling if the stolen item is destroyed after amputation?
A) The thief must pay the value.
B) The thief is not liable.
C) The thief is amputated again.
D) The owner's claim is void.

Key Takeaway
In the well-known Hanafi view, amputation extinguished civil liability for the stolen item; the owner cannot demand compensation after the hadd is carried out.

Amputation for One Theft Covers All: Guarantee Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee after amputation for multiple thefts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on guarantee after amputation for multiple thefts and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
One amputation for multiple thefts: Abu Hanifa says no compensation; the Sahibayn say compensation except for the theft amputated for.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of the ḥadd punishment of amputation on multiple thefts. The core question: if a thief is amputated for one theft, does he still owe compensation for other thefts he committed? Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ, and their disagreement reveals deeper principles about the nature of ḥadd penalties and the protection of property rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is multiple thefts committed by the same thief, followed by amputation for one of them. The ruling at issue is whether the thief must pay compensation (ḍamān) for the remaining thefts.
Amputation itself is the ḥadd punishment, wajib upon conviction.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that the thief does not guarantee anything for any of the other thefts. His reasoning: the amputation is a single divine right (ḥaqq Allāh) that covers all thefts, so once exacted, it suffices for all.
His dalil is the unity of the obligation: one amputation for all thefts.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the thief must guarantee all thefts except the one for which he was amputated. Their reasoning: the amputation only cancels guarantee for the victim who was present and litigated; the absent victims' property remains inviolable (maʿṣūmah), so compensation is due.
They compare amputation to a penalty that only benefits the immediate litigant.
4
Compare the proofs and note the thamarah
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilāf) is that under Abu Hanifa, all other victims receive nothing; under the Sahibayn, other victims receive compensation. Both agree that if all victims are present and litigation is combined, no guarantee is owed.
Al-Shafiʿī disagrees even with the agreed case, holding that guarantee remains.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text sides with Abu Hanifa's view (wa lahu) and notes it as the relied-upon position from the Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قُطِعَ لِبَعْضِ السَّرِقَاتِ لَا يَضْمَنُ شَيْئًا. أَيْ مَنْ سَرَقَ سَرِقَاتٍ فَقُطِعَ فِي إِحْدَاهَا فَهَذَا القَطْعُ لِجَمِيعِهَا وَلَا يَضْمَنُ شَيْئًا لِلْآخَرِينَ مُطْلَقًا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ، وَقَالَا يَضْمَنُ كُلَّهَا غَيْرَ الَّتِي قُطِعَ بِهَا. وَمَعْنَى المَسْأَلَةِ إِذَا حَضَرَ أَحَدُهُمْ فَقُطِعَ بِخُصُومَتِهِ، إِذَا حَضَرُوا جَمِيعًا فَقُطِعَ بِخُصُومَتِهِمْ لَا ضَمَانَ عَلَيْهِ بِالِاتِّفَاقِ خِلَافًا لِلشَّافِعِيِّ لَهُمَا: أَنَّ المُسْقِطَ لِلضَّمَانِ القَطْعُ، وَهُوَ حَصَلَ لِلْحَاضِرِ وَالحَاضِرُ لَيْسَ بِنَائِبٍ عَنِ الغَائِبِ فَلَمْ يُوجَدْ لِغَيْرِهِ فَبَقِيَتْ أَمْوَالُهُمْ مَعْصُومَةً، وَلَهُ أَنَّ الوَاجِبَ بِالكُلِّ قَطْعٌ وَاحِدٌ حَقًّا لِلّٰهِ تَعَالَى، فَإِذَا اسْتُوفِيَ كَانَ لِلْكُلِّ، وَعَلَى هَذَا الخِلَافُ إِذَا سَرَقَ مِنْ وَاحِدٍ نِصَابًا مِرَارًا، ثُمَّ قُطِعَ لِأَجْلِ نِصَابٍ وَاحِدٍ [ رَمْزُ الحَقَائِقِ: ٤١٢/١] (مُسْتَخْلَص)
And if he is amputated for some thefts, he does not guarantee anything. That is, whoever steals multiple thefts and is amputated for one of them, this amputation is for all of them, and he does not guarantee anything for the others at all according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He guarantees all except the one for which he was amputated. The meaning of the issue: if one of them (the victims) is present and he is amputated based on his litigation, if all are present and he is amputated based on their litigation, there is no guarantee by agreement, contrary to al-Shafi'i. For them (the two): The canceler of guarantee is amputation, and it occurred for the present one, and the present one is not a deputy for the absent one, so it did not occur for the others, so their property remains inviolable. And for him (Abu Hanifa): The obligation by all is one amputation as a right of Allah, so when it is exacted, it is for all. And on this difference is when he steals from one person a nisab multiple times, then he is amputated for one nisab. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/412] (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding multiple thefts. The matn states: if a thief commits multiple thefts but is amputated (hand cut) for only one of them, he does not guarantee (pay compensation) for the others. The commentary explains that Abu Hanifa holds this ruling absolutely: the amputation applies to all thefts, and no compensation is owed for any of them. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that the thief must guarantee all thefts except the one for which he was cut. They distinguish the scenario: If all victims are present and litigation is combined, then there is agreement (including the companions) that no guarantee is owed—this is even against al-Shafi'i. But if only one victim appears, the companions argue that the amputation only benefits that present victim, so the absent victims' property remains protected (maʿṣūmah) and compensation is due. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the amputation is a single divine right (ḥaqq Allāh) that covers all thefts, so once exacted, it suffices for all. The same dispute applies if one person steals a nisab from a single owner multiple times and is amputated for one instance. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الضَّمَان (al-ḍamān): Compensation or guarantee for stolen property.
القَطْع (al-qaṭʿ): Amputation of the hand as the ḥadd punishment for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which scenario leads to agreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn that no guarantee is owed?
A) When only one victim is present
B) When all victims are present and litigation is combined
C) When the thief steals from a single victim multiple times
D) When the amputation is performed without litigation

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi view is that a single amputation suffices for all thefts, and no compensation is owed for any of them.

Tearing Stolen Cloth in the House: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Amputation
Ruling on tearing stolen cloth in the house; intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tearing a stolen item before removal and distinguish between major (fāḥish) and minor (yasīr) damage in the context of ḥadd.

Core Idea
Major damage to the stolen item creates shubha that prevents ḥadd; minor damage does not.

A thief enters a house, tears a cloth in two, and then takes it out. Does the torn condition affect the application of the ḥadd amputation? The Hanafi school agrees that minor damage does not prevent amputation, but they differ on major damage. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute and the precise definitions of major and minor damage that determine the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and base ruling
Read the matn carefully: the thief tears the cloth in the house (not outside) and then takes it out. The base ruling says he is amputated, provided the torn part taken out reaches the nisab (value threshold).
The tearing occurs before removal; this fact triggers the dispute.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf argues that if the tearing is major (fāḥish), the thief becomes liable for the value, which gives him a kind of ownership, creating shubha (doubt) that drops the ḥadd.
His reasoning: guarantee implies ownership, and ownership conflicts with the hadd requirement of taking from another's possession.
3
Compare the Sahibayn's response
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad counter that the act of taking with tearing is merely a cause for guarantee, not a legal cause for ownership. Ownership arises only by necessity (to avoid double recovery), which still leaves shubha, so amputation is not automatically prevented.
For them, the shubha from tearing is like the shubha from the taking itself—it does not automatically drop the hadd without further conditions.
4
Define major vs. minor damage
The text discusses multiple definitions, then gives the correct (ṣaḥīḥ) view: major damage causes loss of some substance (ʿayn) and some benefit (manfaʿah); minor damage only causes a defect without loss of benefit. This definition determines whether the damage is considered fāḥish or yasīr.
The practical outcome: if the damage is minor, amputation is enforced by consensus; if major, the ruling depends on the owner's election.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ شَقَّ مَا سَرَقَ فِي الدَّارِ ثُمَّ أَخْرَجَهُ قُطِعَ.
ثُمَّ أَخْرَجَهُ أَي مِنَ الدَّارِ، وَقِيمَتُهُ بَعْدَ الشَّقِّ عَشْرَةُ دَرَاهِمَ. (عَيْنِي) قُطِعَ أَيْ مَنْ سَرَقَ ثَوْبًا فَشَقَّهُ فِي الدَّارِ نِصْفَيْنِ ثُمَّ أَخْرَجَهُ مِنَ الدَّارِ قُطِعَ إِذَا كَانَ المَشْقُوقُ تَبْلُغُ نِصَابًا عِنْدَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ: لَا يُقْطَعُ؛ لِأَنَّ مِلْكًا لَهُ بِسَبَبِ الخَرْقِ الفَاحِشِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ قِيمَتُهُ فَيَمْلِكُ المَضْمُونَ، وَلَهُمَا: أَنَّ الأَخْذَ مَعَ الخَرْقِ وَضْعُ سَبَبٍ لِلضَّمَانِ، وَلَيْسَ سَبَبًا لِلْمِلْكِ شَرْعًا، وَإِنَّمَا يَثْبُتُ المِلْكُ ضَرُورَةَ أَنْ يَجْتَمِعَ البَدَلَانِ فِي مِلْكٍ وَاحِدٍ، وَمِثْلُهُ يُورِثُ الشُّبْهَةَ كَنَفْسِ الأَخْذِ وَهَذَا الخِلَافُ فِيمَا إِذَا اخْتَارَ تَضْمِينَ النُّقْصَانِ وَأَخَذَ الثَّوْبَ، وَإِنِ اخْتَارَ تَضْمِينَ القِيمَةِ وَتَرْكَ الثَّوْبِ عَلَيْهِ لَا يُقْطَعُ اتِّفَاقًا، وَإِنْ كَانَ النُّقْصَانُ يَسِيرًا قُطِعَ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ، وَتَكَلَّمُوا فِي الفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الفَاحِشِ وَاليَسِيرِ، فَقِيلَ: إِنْ أَوْجَبَ الفَرْقَ نُقْصَانَ رُبْعِ القِيمَةِ فَصَاعِدًا فَهُوَ فَاحِشٌ، وَمَا دُونَهُ يَسِيرٌ. وَقِيلَ: مَا لَا يَصْلُحُ البَاقِي لِثَوْبٍ فَهُوَ فَاحِشٌ وَاليَسِيرُ مَا يَصْلُحُ وَقِيلَ مَا يَنْتَقِصُ بِهِ نِصْفُ القِيمَةِ فَاحِشٌ، وَمَا دُونَهُ يَسِيرٌ وَمَا فَوْقَهُ اسْتِهْلَاكٌ، وَالصَّحِيحُ أَنَّ الفَاحِشَ مَا يَفُوتُ بِهِ بَعْضُ العَيْنِ وَبَعْضُ المَنْفَعَةِ، وَاليَسِيرُ مَا لَا يَفُوتُ بِهِ شَيْءٌ مِنَ المَنْفَعَةِ بَلْ يَتَعَيَّبُ بِهِ فَقَطْ، وَهَذَا الخِيَارُ يَثْبُتُ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ إِتْلَافًا، وَإِذَا كَانَ إِتْلَافًا فَلَهُ تَضْمِينُ جَمِيعِ القِيمَةِ مِنْ غَيْرِ خِيَارٍ، وَيَمْلِكُ السَّارِقُ الثَّوْبَ وَلَا يُقْطَعُ [رَمْزُ الحَقَائِقِ: ١/ ٤١٣] (مُسْتَخْلَص)
And if he tears what he stole in the house then takes it out, he is amputated. Then he takes it out, i.e., from the house, and its value after tearing is ten dirhams. (ʿAynī) He is amputated: That is, whoever steals a cloth and tears it in the house into two halves then takes it out of the house, he is amputated if the torn cloth reaches a nisab at both ends (i.e., the value at the time of taking out). Abu Yusuf said: He is not amputated because he has ownership due to the major tearing; because he must pay its value, so he owns the guaranteed property. For them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): The taking along with the tearing places a cause for guarantee, not a cause for ownership legally; ownership is only established out of necessity for the two compensations to be in one ownership, and such a case creates doubt (shubha) like the taking itself. This dispute is when he chooses to guarantee the deficiency and takes the cloth. If he chooses to guarantee the full value and leaves the cloth, he is not amputated by agreement. If the deficiency is minor (yasīr), he is amputated by consensus. They discussed the difference between major (fāḥish) and minor. It was said: If the deficiency causes a loss of a quarter of the value or more, it is major; what is less is minor. It was said: What the remainder is not fit for a cloth is major; what is fit is minor. It was said: What decreases half the value is major; what is less is minor, and above that is destruction. The correct view is that major is what causes loss of some of the substance (ʿayn) and some of the benefit; minor is what does not cause loss of any benefit but only causes a defect. This choice (khiyār) applies as long as it is not destruction. If it is destruction, then he has the option to guarantee the entire value without choice, and the thief owns the cloth and is not amputated. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/413] (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on tearing the stolen cloth inside the house before taking it out. The matn states that if the thief tears the item and then takes it out, he is amputated. However, there is a dispute between Abu Yusuf and the other two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf argues that if the tearing is major (fāḥish), the thief acquires ownership because he becomes liable for its value, so amputation drops due to shubha (doubt). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that the taking with tearing creates a cause for guarantee, not ownership, and ownership only arises from necessity; the situation still creates shubha, so amputation applies if the torn item reaches nisab. The text outlines multiple definitions of major vs. minor damage: quarter of value, half, or functional fitness. The correct (ṣaḥīḥ) definition is that major tearing removes some of the substance and benefit, while minor only causes a defect. The ruling depends on the owner’s choice: if he takes the cloth and demands compensation for the deficiency, the thief is amputated (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad); if he leaves the cloth and demands full value, then no amputation by agreement. If the damage is minor, amputation is enforced by consensus. If the tearing amounts to destruction (istihlāk), the owner can choose to take full value, and then the thief owns the cloth and is not amputated.

Key Terms
الفَاحِش (al-fāḥish): Major damage that causes loss of part of the substance (ʿayn) and part of the benefit (manfaʿah).
اليَسِير (al-yasīr): Minor damage that only defects the item without loss of benefit.
الشُّبْهَة (al-shubha): Legal doubt that prevents the ḥadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct (ṣaḥīḥ) definition, what constitutes major (fāḥish) damage to a stolen cloth?
A) Any damage that reduces the value by more than a quarter.
B) Damage that causes loss of some of the substance and some of the benefit.
C) Damage that makes the cloth unfit for any use.
D) Any damage that requires compensation.

Key Takeaway
Major damage to a stolen item creates shubha that may prevent ḥadd, but only if the owner chooses to take the cloth and claim deficiency; minor damage never prevents amputation.

Theft of a Sheep Slaughtered Inside the Hirz: No Hadd for Meat
Ruling on theft of a sheep slaughtered inside the ḥirz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft of an animal slaughtered inside the ḥirz and apply the distinction between nuqṣān al-ʿayn and nuqṣān al-siʿr in determining liability for ḥadd.

Core Idea
Stealing meat triggers no amputation; physical decrease is guaranteed, market drop is not.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the law of ḥadd for theft: stealing a sheep and slaughtering it inside the protected area (ḥirz) before removal. The ruling clarifies when amputation applies and introduces the critical distinction between a decrease in the physical substance (nuqṣān al-ʿayn) versus a decrease in market price (nuqṣān al-siʿr). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the ḥadd penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
A thief steals a sheep, slaughters it inside the house (the ḥirz), and then takes it out. The matn states 'no', meaning no amputation (ḥadd) is imposed. The ruling is that the thief is not amputated.
This is the core ruling of the passage.
2
Locate the rationale
The commentary explains: the theft is completed on meat (laḥm), and there is no ḥadd amputation for meat. Meat is a perishable edible, and the ḥadd applies only to items of lasting value (e.g., coins, cloth, metals).
The rationale is that the object of theft was transformed into a category that does not qualify for ḥadd.
3
Understand the restriction 'slaughters it'
The passage notes that if the thief takes the sheep out alive (without slaughtering inside), then slaughters it outside, amputation does apply—even if the value drops due to slaughter. This is because the theft was completed on the live sheep, which is eligible for ḥadd. The subsequent decrease in value is physical (nuqṣān al-ʿayn) and is guaranteed by the thief, so it does not prevent the ḥadd.
The distinction hinges on whether the theft was on the original animal or on the meat.
4
Contrast with price fluctuation after verdict
The commentary contrasts this with a different scenario: after a court verdict of amputation but before execution, if the market price of the stolen item drops (nuqṣān al-siʿr), the amputation falls. This is because a price decrease is not guaranteed by the thief—the value loss is not his responsibility. In the current case, the decrease is physical (inside the ḥirz) and guaranteed, so amputation stands if the original theft conditions were met.
Understanding this contrast is key to applying the rule correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ سَرَقَ شَاةً فَذَبَحَهَا فَأَخْرَجَهَا لَا.
أَيْ لَا يُقْطَعُ السَّارِقُ. لِلسَّرِقَةِ أَيْ لَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ رَدٌّ. شَاةً: أَيْ مَنْ سَرَقَ شَاةً فَذَبَحَهَا فِي الدَّارِ ثُمَّ أَخْرَجَهَا مِنْهَا لَا يُقْطَعُ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ قِيمَتُهَا مَذْبُوحَةً عَشْرَةً؛ لِأَنَّ السَّرِقَةَ تَمَّتْ عَلَى اللَّحْمِ، وَلَا قَطْعَ فِيهِ، وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّهُ يَضْمَنُ قِيمَتَهَا، وَإِنَّمَا قَيَّدَ بِقَوْلِهِ: "فَذَبَحَهَا"؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ أَخْرَجَهَا حَيَّةً مِنَ الْحِرْزِ، وَقِيمَتُهَا عَشْرَةٌ، ثُمَّ ذَبَحَهَا يُقْطَعُ وَإِنِ انْتَقَصَتْ قِيمَتُهَا بِالذَّبْحِ؛ وَهَذَا النُّقْصَانُ نُقْصَانُ عَيْنٍ، بِخِلَافِ النُّقْصَانِ السَّابِقِ بَعْدَ الْقَضَاءِ قَبْلَ الِاسْتِيفَاءِ، فَإِنَّهُ نُقْصَانُ سِعْرٍ، وَلِهَذَا أَوْجَبَ سُقُوطَ الْقَطْعِ هُنَاكَ؛ لِأَنَّ نُقْصَانَ السِّعْرِ غَيْرُ مَضْمُونٍ عَلَى السَّارِقِ، أَمَّا هُنَا فَلَا يُوجِبُ سُقُوطَ الْقَطْعِ؛ لِكَوْنِ نُقْصَانِ الْعَيْنِ مَضْمُونًا عَلَيْهِ. (عَيْنِي، فَتْح)
And if he steals a sheep and slaughters it and takes it out, no. That is, the thief is not amputated. For the theft: i.e., he is not obliged to return (the meat). A sheep: whoever steals a sheep and slaughters it in the house then takes it out, he is not amputated even if its value slaughtered is ten; because the theft is completed on meat, and there is no amputation for it. And it is known that he guarantees its value. He only restricted it by saying 'slaughters it' because if he takes it out alive from the safe place (ḥirz) while its value is ten, then slaughters it, he is amputated even if its value decreases by slaughter; and this decrease is a decrease of substance (ʿayn), unlike the previous decrease after the verdict before execution (of amputation), which is a decrease in price (market fluctuation). For that reason, amputation fell there, because price decrease is not guaranteed upon the thief. But here it does not cause amputation to fall, because the decrease of substance is guaranteed upon him. (ʿAynī, Fatḥ)
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage gives a ruling on stealing a sheep and slaughtering it inside the house before taking it out. The matn states 'no', meaning the thief is not amputated. The commentary explains the reason: the theft is completed on the meat (laḥm), and there is no ḥadd amputation for meat (i.e., for perishable edibles). However, the thief is liable to pay the value (ḍamān). The restriction 'slaughters it' is important: if the thief takes the sheep out alive (i.e., without slaughtering inside), its value is intact, and then slaughters it outside, he would be amputated because the theft was on the whole sheep, not just meat. The decrease in value due to slaughter (which is a decrease of the substance, ʿayn) is guaranteed on the thief, so it does not drop the amputation. In contrast, a decrease in market price (after the court verdict but before amputation) is not guaranteed, so amputation could fall. This distinction is between a physical change (which the thief must compensate) and a price fluctuation (which is not his responsibility). The ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
اللَّحْم (al-laḥm): Meat as a perishable substance that does not trigger ḥadd theft.
نُقْصَانُ الْعَيْن (nuqṣān al-ʿayn): Physical diminution of the substance of the stolen item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a thief not amputated if he steals a sheep, slaughters it inside the house, then takes it out?
A) Because the sheep was dead, so it cannot be stolen.
B) Because the theft is completed on meat, and there is no amputation for meat.
C) Because the owner allowed it.
D) Because the value decreased after slaughter.

Key Takeaway
The ḥadd of amputation for theft does not apply to perishable items like meat; a physical decrease in value is guaranteed, but a market price decrease is not.

Stolen Gold/Silver Made into Coins: Amputation and Return
Ruling on transforming stolen gold/silver into coins; amputation applies, and coins are returned to owner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation when a thief transforms stolen gold/silver into coins, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ownership through manufacturing.

Core Idea
Manufacturing stolen gold into coins does not transfer ownership in Abu Hanifa's view, so amputation remains.

In this passage, we confront a fascinating intra-Hanafi dispute: when a thief steals gold dust and turns it into coins, does he become the owner of the coins? The answer determines whether amputation applies. This lesson dissects the reasoning of Abu Hanifa versus his two companions, grounding the dispute in the concept of mutaqawwim property.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa as given in the matn: the thief is amputated and the coins are returned to the victim.
This is the mufta bihi position in the text.
2
Identify the opposing view of the two companions
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the victim has no recourse to the coins; i.e., the thief owns them through transformation.
Their position is based on the raw gold/silver being mutaqawwim.
3
Trace the root of the dispute
The disagreement originates from the principle of ghasb (usurpation): does the ghāṣib own the new form by manufacturing? This depends on whether the original material is mutaqawwim (legally valuable property).
Abu Hanifa says gold/silver as raw metal is not mutaqawwim; Sahibayn say it is.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If the thief does not own the coins, amputation is obligatory and the coins must be returned. If he does own them, amputation may drop (or still apply if the substance changed).
There are two narrations from Sahibayn on the amputation ruling.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and extension
The matn follows Abu Hanifa, and the same dispute applies if the thief makes jewelry or vessels.
The source cites Ramz al-Haqaiq and Mustakhlas for this position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ صَنَعَ الْمَسْرُوقَ دَرَاهِمَ أَوْ دَنَانِيرَ قُطِعَ وَرُدَّتْ.
أَيْ مَنْ سَرَقَ ذَهَبًا أَوْ فِضَّةً فَضَرَبَهَا دَرَاهِمَ أَوْ دَنَانِيرَ قُطِعَ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ، وَيُرَدُّ الدَّرَاهِمُ وَالدَّنَانِيرُ إِلَى الْمَسْرُوقِ مِنْهُ، وَقَالَا: لَا سَبِيلَ لِلْمَسْرُوقِ مِنْهُ عَلَيْهَا، وَأَصْلُ هَذَا الْخِلَافِ فِي الْغَصْبِ فِي أَنَّ الْغَاصِبَ هَلْ يَمْلِكُ الدَّرَاهِمَ وَالدَّنَانِيرَ بِهَذِهِ الصِّنَاعَةِ أَمْ لَا؟ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّهَا مُتَقَوِّمَةٌ أَمْ لَا؟ فَعِنْدَهُ لَا يَمْلِكُ؛ لِأَنَّهَا لَا تَتَقَوَّمُ، وَعِنْدَهُمَا: يَمْلِكُ؛ لِتَقَوُّمِهَا، ثُمَّ وُجُوبُ الْقَطْعِ عِنْدَهُ لَا يَشْكُلُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَمْ يَمْلِكْهَا عَلَى قَوْلِهِ وَقِيلَ عَلَى قَوْلِهِمَا لَا يَجِبُ الْقَطْعُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مَلَكَهُ قَبْلَ الْقَطْعِ، وَقِيلَ: يَجِبُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ صَارَ بِالصِّنَاعَةِ شَيْءٌ آخَرُ، فَلَمْ يَمْلِكْ عَيْنَهُ، وَعَلَى هَذَا الْخِلَافِ لَوِ اتَّخَذَهُ حُلِيًّا أَوْ آنِيَةً [ رَمْزُ الْحَقَائِقِ: ٤١٣/١] (مُسْتَخْلَص)
And if he makes the stolen item into dirhams or dinars, he is amputated and they are returned. That is, whoever steals gold or silver and strikes it into dirhams or dinars, he is amputated according to Abu Hanifa, and the dirhams and dinars are returned to the person from whom they were stolen. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The victim has no recourse to them. The origin of this dispute is in usury (ghaṣb) regarding whether the usurper (ghāṣib) owns the dirhams and dinars by this manufacturing or not, based on whether they are considered property with value (muttaqawwimah) or not. According to him (Abu Hanifa), he does not own them because they are not considered property; according to them, he owns them because they are property. Then the obligation of amputation according to him is not problematic because he did not own them according to his view. It was said that according to their view, amputation is not obligatory because he owned it before amputation. And it was said: It is obligatory because it became another thing by the manufacturing, so he did not own its substance. And on this difference is if he made it into jewelry or vessels. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/413] (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on transforming stolen gold or silver into coins (dirhams/dinars). The matn states the ruling: the thief is amputated, and the coins are returned to the victim. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they say the victim has no right to the coins. The root of the dispute lies in the principle of usurpation (ghaṣb): does the usurper/maker acquire ownership of the new form through manufacturing? This depends on whether the raw material (gold/silver) is considered 'with value' (mutaqawwim) in its original form. Abu Hanifa holds that gold and silver as raw metal are not considered mutaqawwim (they are not consumable goods with a prescribed value), so the thief does not gain ownership by manufacturing coins; thus, the coins still belong to the victim, and amputation is obligatory. The two companions argue that the raw metal is mutaqawwim, so the thief becomes owner of the coins, and amputation would drop because of ownership. There are two opinions on the two companions' view: one says no amputation because he owned it before cutting; another says amputation still applies because the substance changed completely (to a new object), so he did not own the original substance. The same dispute applies if he makes jewelry or vessels. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
مُتَقَوِّمَة (muttaqawwimah): Property that has a legally recognized value and can be owned and sold.
الصِّنَاعَة (al-ṣināʿah): Manufacturing or transformation that changes the form of the stolen item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a thief steals gold dust and makes it into a coin, what is the ruling?
A) The thief is amputated and the coin is returned to the victim.
B) The thief is not amputated and keeps the coin.
C) The thief is amputated but the coin is forfeited.
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the application of hadd for theft depends on whether the thief acquires ownership of the stolen item through transformation; Abu Hanifa denies such ownership for precious metals, thus maintaining the hadd and the victim's right to the new form.

Red Dye: Amputation and No Return (Two Shaykhs) vs. Muhammad's View
Ruling on red-dyed stolen cloth: amputation and civil liability dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on a thief dyeing stolen cloth red and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Red dye is an increase (ziyādah) giving the thief a right; the two shaykhs prioritize his right over the owner's, Muhammad prioritizes the owner's original property.

A thief steals a white cloth and dyes it red. Is he still subject to amputation? Must he return the cloth or compensate the owner? The Hanafi school has a nuanced dispute between the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani). Understanding this difference reveals how Hanafi jurists weigh ownership rights and added value in stolen property.

Guided Path
1
State the matn ruling
The base text says: if the thief dyes it red and is amputated, the cloth is not returned and no compensation is paid. This is the default position.
The matn is concise; the commentary expands the dispute.
2
Identify the two shaykhs' position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold: the cloth is not returned (while it exists) and no compensation is paid (if destroyed). Their reasoning: the thief's dye remains 'in form and meaning' (ṣūratan wa maʿnan), while the owner's right is only 'in form' (ṣūratan lā maʿnan). Therefore the thief's right is stronger.
They prioritize the thief's interest in the added value because he faces the hadd punishment.
3
Identify Muhammad's position
Imam Muhammad says: the cloth must be returned to the owner, and the owner must pay the thief the added value of the red dye. His reasoning: the owner's property (the original cloth) remains in every respect (ʿayn mālihi qāʾimah min kulli wajh), and the dye is subordinate (tabaʿ). The original is given priority.
Muhammad considers the cloth's identity intact; the dye is merely an accessory.
4
Analyze the reasoning difference
The key is how each side views the 'right' (ḥaqq). The two shaykhs see the dye as a transformative addition that gives the thief a dominant claim; Muhammad sees the cloth as the primary substance and the dye as incidental.
This is a classic example of competing rights in proprietary law.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the two shaykhs, the thief keeps the cloth (or its value) despite amputation; under Muhammad, the owner gets the cloth back but must compensate the thief for the dye.
The difference affects whether the thief gains or loses from the enhancement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ صَبَغَهُ أَحْمَرَ فَقُطِعَ، لَا يُرَدُّ وَلَا يُضْمَنُ، وَلَوْ أَسْوَدَ يُرَدُّ.
أَيْ مَنْ سَرَقَ ثَوْبًا فَصَبَغَهُ أَحْمَرَ قُطِعَ، وَلَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ رَدُّ الثَّوْبِ حَالَ قِيَامِهِ، وَلَا يَضْمَنُ قِيمَةَ أَبْيَضَ حَالَ اسْتِهْلَاكِهِ عِنْدَ الشَّيْخَيْنِ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ: يُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ الثَّوْبُ وَيُعْطَى مَا زَادَ الصَّبْغُ فِيهِ؛ لِأَنَّ عَيْنَ مَالِهِ قَائِمَةٌ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهٍ، وَهُوَ أَصْلٌ وَالصَّبْغُ تَبَعٌ، فَكَانَ اعْتِبَارُ الْأَصْلِ أَوْلَى، وَلَهُمَا: أَنَّ صَبْغَ السَّارِقِ فِي الثَّوْبِ قَائِمٌ صُورَةً وَمَعْنًى، وَحَقُّ صَاحِبِ الثَّوْبِ قَائِمٌ صُورَةً لَا مَعْنًى حَتَّى إِذَا هَلَكَ عِنْدَهُ أَوِ اسْتَهْلَكَهُ لَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ الضَّمَانُ، فَكَانَ حَقُّ السَّارِقِ أَحَقَّ بِالتَّرْجِيحِ، وَعِنْدَ الثَّلَاثَةِ: يُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ الثَّوْبُ بِلَا ضَمَانِ شَيْءٍ. [رَمْزُ الْحَقَائِقِ: ٤١٣/١] (مُسْتَخْلَص) وَلَوْ أَسْوَدَ: أَيْ لَوْ صَبَغَ السَّارِقُ الثَّوْبَ أَسْوَدَ يُرَدُّ الثَّوْبُ إِلَى الْمَالِكِ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ مَجَّانًا؛ لِأَنَّ السَّوَادَ نُقْصَانٌ عِنْدَهُ وَبِالنُّقْصَانِ لَا يَنْقَطِعُ حَقُّ الْمَالِكِ، وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ: لَا يُرَدُّ؛ لِأَنَّ السَّوَادَ زِيَادَةٌ يَنْقَطِعُ حَقُّ الْمَالِكِ عِنْدَهُ، وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ: هُوَ كَالْأَحْمَرِ، فَيُرَدُّ إِلَى الْمَالِكِ، وَيَضْمَنُ الْمَالِكُ مَا زَادَ الصَّبْغُ فِيهِ [ رَمْزُ الْحَقَائِقِ: ٤١٤/١] وَهَذَا اخْتِلَافُ عَصْرٍ، فَإِنَّ النَّاسَ مَا كَانُوا يَلْبَسُونَ السَّوَادَ فِي زَمَنِ الْإِمَامِ. (فَتْح) يُرَدُّ: عَلَى صِيغَةِ الْمَجْهُولِ أَيْ يُرَدُّ السَّارِقُ الثَّوْبَ الْمَسْرُوقَ عَلَى مَالِكِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ السَّوَادَ نُقْصَانٌ. (عَيْنِي)
And if he dyes it red and is amputated, it is not returned and not guaranteed. And if black, it is returned. That is, whoever steals a cloth and dyes it red, he is amputated, and it is not obligatory to return the cloth while it exists, nor does he guarantee the value of white when it is destroyed according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). But earlier it says the two shaykhs differed with Muhammad. It might be a later opinion. I'll follow the text. I will translate as is: 'And according to the three: the cloth is taken from him without any guarantee.' Abu Yusuf said: It is not returned because black is an increase that cuts off the owner's right in his view. And Muhammad said: It is like red, so it is returned to the owner, and the owner guarantees what the dye added. Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on dyeing a stolen cloth red or black. The matn states: if dyed red, the thief is amputated, and the cloth is neither returned nor compensated. If dyed black, it must be returned. The commentary reveals extensive intra-Hanafi dispute. For red dye, the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that the thief is amputated and does not return the cloth or pay compensation even if destroyed. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) says the cloth must be returned to the owner, who in turn pays the thief the added value of the dye. The shaykhs' reasoning: the thief's dye remains perceptible both in form and meaning, while the owner's right remains only in form (since the cloth is changed); if the cloth perishes, the thief is not liable, so the thief's right to the dye is stronger. According to 'the three' (possibly later scholars), the cloth is taken from the thief without any payment. For black dye, Abu Hanifa says the cloth is returned for free because black is a deficiency (nuqṣān) in his view, so the owner's right persists. Abu Yusuf says it is not returned because black is an increase (ziyādah) that terminates the owner's right. Muhammad equates black with red: the cloth is returned, and the owner compensates the thief for the dye. The text notes that this difference is temporal: in Imam Abu Hanifa's time, people did not wear black, so black was considered a deficiency; later it became an increase. This shows how custom (ʿurf) can affect rulings. The ruling on amputation for red dye is consistent across all views because the theft is proven.

Key Terms
زِيَادَة (ziyādah): Increase or added value from dye that improves the cloth.
أَصْل (aṣl): Original item (the stolen cloth) as opposed to the dye (tabaʿ).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the ruling for a thief who dyes a stolen cloth red?
A) He is amputated and must return the cloth.
B) He is amputated and does not return the cloth nor pay compensation.
C) He is not amputated but must return the cloth.
D) He is amputated and must compensate the owner for the cloth.

Key Takeaway
Red dye is considered a ziyādah: the two shaykhs prioritize the thief's right (no return/compensation), while Muhammad prioritizes the owner's original property (return with compensation).

Black Dye: Deficiency or Increase? The Role of Custom
Ruling on black-dyed stolen cloth: dispute based on whether black is deficiency or increase, with temporal custom factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how custom (ʿurf) influences whether black dye is considered a deficiency (nuqṣān) or increase (ziyādah) and how this affects the Hanafi rulings on return and compensation.

Core Idea
Black dye was a deficiency in Abu Hanifa's era but later became an increase; custom can change the legal assessment of what constitutes nuqṣān or ziyādah.

The same act – dyeing a stolen cloth black – received different rulings from the Hanafi imams based on whether black was considered a deficiency (nuqṣān) or an increase (ziyādah). This lesson explores how Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad each classified black dye, and how a change in custom (ʿurf) over time can shift the legal outcome.

Guided Path
1
State the three positions on black dye
Abu Hanifa: cloth is returned to owner for free (black = nuqṣān). Abu Yusuf: cloth is not returned (black = ziyādah). Muhammad: cloth returned but owner pays added value (like red).
Each imam classifies black differently.
2
Understand the reasoning for each
Abu Hanifa: black was not worn in his time, so it made the cloth less desirable – a deficiency. Abu Yusuf: black is a dye that adds color, so it is an increase. Muhammad: black is like red; the dye is an increase that adds value.
The difference hinges on the perceived effect on the cloth's value.
3
Identify the temporal custom factor
The commentary states: 'This is a difference of era, for people did not wear black in the time of the Imam.' That is why Abu Hanifa saw it as a deficiency. Later, when black became common, it was seen as an increase.
Custom (ʿurf) can change the legal characterization of the same act.
4
Apply the principle to a modern scenario
If today black is a popular color, it would likely be considered ziyādah. The ruling would follow Abu Yusuf's view (no return) or Muhammad's (return with compensation), depending on which view is taken.
A mufti must consider contemporary custom.
5
Note the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, owner gets cloth back free. Under Abu Yusuf, thief keeps cloth. Under Muhammad, owner gets cloth but pays for dye.
The thamarah al-khilaf is significant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ صَبَغَهُ أَحْمَرَ فَقُطِعَ، لَا يُرَدُّ وَلَا يُضْمَنُ، وَلَوْ أَسْوَدَ يُرَدُّ.
أَيْ مَنْ سَرَقَ ثَوْبًا فَصَبَغَهُ أَحْمَرَ قُطِعَ، وَلَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ رَدُّ الثَّوْبِ حَالَ قِيَامِهِ، وَلَا يَضْمَنُ قِيمَةَ أَبْيَضَ حَالَ اسْتِهْلَاكِهِ عِنْدَ الشَّيْخَيْنِ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ: يُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ الثَّوْبُ وَيُعْطَى مَا زَادَ الصَّبْغُ فِيهِ؛ لِأَنَّ عَيْنَ مَالِهِ قَائِمَةٌ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهٍ، وَهُوَ أَصْلٌ وَالصَّبْغُ تَبَعٌ، فَكَانَ اعْتِبَارُ الْأَصْلِ أَوْلَى، وَلَهُمَا: أَنَّ صَبْغَ السَّارِقِ فِي الثَّوْبِ قَائِمٌ صُورَةً وَمَعْنًى، وَحَقُّ صَاحِبِ الثَّوْبِ قَائِمٌ صُورَةً لَا مَعْنًى حَتَّى إِذَا هَلَكَ عِنْدَهُ أَوِ اسْتَهْلَكَهُ لَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ الضَّمَانُ، فَكَانَ حَقُّ السَّارِقِ أَحَقَّ بِالتَّرْجِيحِ، وَعِنْدَ الثَّلَاثَةِ: يُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ الثَّوْبُ بِلَا ضَمَانِ شَيْءٍ. [رَمْزُ الْحَقَائِقِ: ٤١٣/١] (مُسْتَخْلَص) وَلَوْ أَسْوَدَ: أَيْ لَوْ صَبَغَ السَّارِقُ الثَّوْبَ أَسْوَدَ يُرَدُّ الثَّوْبُ إِلَى الْمَالِكِ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ مَجَّانًا؛ لِأَنَّ السَّوَادَ نُقْصَانٌ عِنْدَهُ وَبِالنُّقْصَانِ لَا يَنْقَطِعُ حَقُّ الْمَالِكِ، وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ: لَا يُرَدُّ؛ لِأَنَّ السَّوَادَ زِيَادَةٌ يَنْقَطِعُ حَقُّ الْمَالِكِ عِنْدَهُ، وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ: هُوَ كَالْأَحْمَرِ، فَيُرَدُّ إِلَى الْمَالِكِ، وَيَضْمَنُ الْمَالِكُ مَا زَادَ الصَّبْغُ فِيهِ [ رَمْزُ الْحَقَائِقِ: ٤١٤/١] وَهَذَا اخْتِلَافُ عَصْرٍ، فَإِنَّ النَّاسَ مَا كَانُوا يَلْبَسُونَ السَّوَادَ فِي زَمَنِ الْإِمَامِ. (فَتْح) يُرَدُّ: عَلَى صِيغَةِ الْمَجْهُولِ أَيْ يُرَدُّ السَّارِقُ الثَّوْبَ الْمَسْرُوقَ عَلَى مَالِكِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ السَّوَادَ نُقْصَانٌ. (عَيْنِي)
And if he dyes it red and is amputated, it is not returned and not guaranteed. And if black, it is returned. That is, whoever steals a cloth and dyes it red, he is amputated, and it is not obligatory to return the cloth while it exists, nor does he guarantee the value of white when it is destroyed according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). And Muhammad said: The cloth is taken from him and what the dye added is given (to the thief) because his (the victim's) property's substance remains in every respect, and it is the original and the dye is a subordinate, so consideration of the original is more appropriate. For them (the two): The dye of the thief in the cloth remains in form and meaning, and the right of the owner of the cloth remains in form but not in meaning, such that if it perishes with him or he consumes it, he is not liable for compensation. So the right of the thief is more deserving of preference. According to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad? Actually the text says 'at the three' but then says 'the cloth is taken from him without any guarantee'? The text is unclear: 'عند الثلاثة' might refer to a different group. Let's check the original: the commentary says 'وعند الثلاثة: يؤخذ منه الثوب بلا ضمان شيء' which likely means according to the three (perhaps Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad? But earlier it says the two shaykhs differed with Muhammad. It might be a later opinion. I'll follow the text. Actually, the text says: 'عند الشيخين' (the two shaykhs) and then 'وقال محمد' then 'وعند الثلاثة' might mean according to another set of three scholars. But it's ambiguous. I will translate as is: 'And according to the three: the cloth is taken from him without any guarantee.' Then: And if black: if the thief dyes the cloth black, the cloth is returned to the owner at Abu Hanifa's view for free because black is a deficiency in his view, and with deficiency the owner's right is not cut off. Abu Yusuf said: It is not returned because black is an increase that cuts off the owner's right in his view. And Muhammad said: It is like red, so it is returned to the owner, and the owner guarantees what the dye added. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/414] And this is a difference of era, for people did not wear black in the time of the Imam. (Fath) 'Is returned' in passive voice: i.e., the thief returns the stolen cloth to its owner because black is a deficiency. (ʿAynī)
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on dyeing a stolen cloth red or black. The matn states: if dyed red, the thief is amputated, and the cloth is neither returned nor compensated. If dyed black, it must be returned. The commentary reveals extensive intra-Hanafi dispute. For red dye, the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that the thief is amputated and does not return the cloth or pay compensation even if destroyed. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) says the cloth must be returned to the owner, who in turn pays the thief the added value of the dye. The shaykhs' reasoning: the thief's dye remains perceptible both in form and meaning, while the owner's right remains only in form (since the cloth is changed); if the cloth perishes, the thief is not liable, so the thief's right to the dye is stronger. According to 'the three' (possibly later scholars), the cloth is taken from the thief without any payment. For black dye, Abu Hanifa says the cloth is returned for free because black is a deficiency (nuqṣān) in his view, so the owner's right persists. Abu Yusuf says it is not returned because black is an increase (ziyādah) that terminates the owner's right. Muhammad equates black with red: the cloth is returned, and the owner compensates the thief for the dye. The text notes that this difference is temporal: in Imam Abu Hanifa's time, people did not wear black, so black was considered a deficiency; later it became an increase. This shows how custom (ʿurf) can affect rulings. The ruling on amputation for red dye is consistent across all views because the theft is proven.

Key Terms
نُقْصَان (nuqṣān): Deficiency or depreciation; here, black dye making the cloth less valuable in the Imam's era.
اخْتِلَافُ عَصْرٍ (ikhtilāf ʿaṣr): Difference of era; a type of ikhtilaf that arises from changing customs over time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on a thief who dyes a stolen cloth black?
A) The cloth is returned to the owner for free.
B) The cloth is not returned.
C) The cloth is returned, and the owner pays the added value.
D) The thief is not amputated.

Key Takeaway
The classification of black dye as deficiency or increase depends on custom; rulings change with time, illustrating the importance of ʿurf in Hanafi fiqh.

Definition and Conditions for Highway Robbery Hadd
Defines highway robbery and states three conditions for applying the hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) and identify the three conditions for applying the hadd according to Zahir al-Riwayah, including the intra-Hanafi difference from Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Highway robbery is major theft; its hadd requires group strength, remote location, and travel distance, with Abu Yusuf's exception.

Highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) is treated as a severe crime in Islamic law, distinct from ordinary theft due to the greater harm it causes to society. This lesson examines its definition and the conditions for applying the hadd punishment, drawing from the Hanafi tradition as outlined in Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Guided Path
1
Define highway robbery
Read the definition: 'cutting off passers-by from the road' and why it is called major theft (al-sariqa al-kubra).
Focus on the element of greater harm leading to a severer hadd.
2
Identify the three conditions from Zahir al-Riwayah
List: (1) group strength and force (man'a wa shakwa), (2) occurrence outside cities/villages, (3) distance of a travel (masirat safar) from a city.
These conditions ensure the crime is truly highway robbery, not ordinary theft.
3
Note Abu Yusuf's exception
Abu Yusuf allows applying highway robbery rulings even in the city at night or less than travel distance, for public interest.
The text states 'wa alayhi al-fatwa', so this is the mufta bihi position.
4
List additional requirements for applying the hadd
The robbers must be owners of the wealth, legally liable (sane, adult), and caught before repentance.
If caught after repentance and returning wealth, hadd drops by consensus.
5
Understand the effect of repentance
Repentance before capture leads to dropping the hadd (with return of wealth), but after capture the hadd remains.
This is without disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب قطع الطريق أي قطع المارة عن الطريق وهو السرقة الكبرى وسميت كبرى؛ لأن الضرر فيها أعظم ولهذا غلظ الحد فيها، وشروطه في ظاهر الرواية ثلاثة الأول: أن تكون الجماعة ذات منعة وشكوة. والثاني: أن لا يكون في مصر ولا فيما بين القرى ولا بين مصرين والثالث: أن يكون بينهم وبين مصر مسيرة سفر، وعن أبي يوسف أنهم لو كانوا في المصر ليلاً، أو فيما بينهم وبين المصر أقل من مسيرة سفر يجري عليهم أحكام قطاع الطريق، وعليه الفتوى لمصلحة الناس. ويشترط لإجراء الحد على قطاع الطريق أن يكونوا أصحاب الأموال، وأن يكونوا من أهل وجوب القطع، وأن يؤخذوا قبل التوبة، فإن أخذوا بعدها وبعد رد المال، سقط الحد بلا خلاف.
Chapter of cutting the road: meaning cutting off passers-by from the road, and it is the major theft. It is called major because the harm in it is greater, and therefore the hadd is made severe in it. Its conditions in Zahir al-Riwayah are three: first, that the group possesses strength and force; second, that it is not in a city nor between villages nor between two cities; third, that between them and the city is the distance of a journey. From Abu Yusuf: if they were in the city at night, or between them and the city is less than a journey's distance, the rulings of highway robbers apply to them, and the fatwa is upon this for the benefit of the people. It is also required for applying the hadd to highway robbers that they be owners of wealth, and that they be among those liable for cutting, and that they be caught before repentance. If they are caught after repentance and after returning the wealth, the hadd drops without disagreement.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage defines highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) as the major theft (al-sariqa al-kubra) because the harm to society is greater, hence the punishment is more severe. It then lists three conditions from Zahir al-Riwayah for applying the hadd: (1) the group must have strength (man'a) and force (shakwa), meaning they are formidable; (2) the act must occur outside inhabited areas—not in a city, between villages, or between two cities; (3) they must be at a travel distance (masirat safar) from a city. Abu Yusuf differed, saying that if they are in a city at night or less than a travel distance away, the rulings still apply, and this is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position for public interest. Additionally, the highway robbers must be owners of the wealth they take (to justify cutting), must be legally liable for hadd (sane, adult), and must be caught before repentance; if they repent and return the wealth after being caught, the hadd is dropped by consensus.

Key Terms
قطع الطريق (qat' al-tariq): Highway robbery or banditry, i.e., cutting off the road and robbing travelers.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad's six books.
منعة وشكوة (man'a wa shakwa): Strength and force, meaning the group is capable of resisting capture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The robbery must occur inside a city
B) The group must have strength and force
C) The victim must be a Muslim
D) The robber must be a traveler himself

Key Takeaway
The hadd for highway robbery in Hanafi fiqh requires specific conditions; the mufta bihi position follows Abu Yusuf's broader application for public interest.

Punishment for Intending Highway Robbery Without Act
Ruling with proof: Hanafi position on mere intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that mere intention of highway robbery is punished by imprisonment until repentance, and explain the interpretation of Quran 5:33 that supports this classification.

Core Idea
Mere intent to rob is punished by imprisonment until repentance; the Quranic 'aw' distributes penalties, not chooses.

This passage addresses a specific case in the chapter of hudud: a person who intends to commit highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) but is caught before taking any property or killing anyone. The Hanafi school rules that such a person is imprisoned until repentance, as a ta'zir punishment. The proof is derived from Quran 5:33, where the word 'aw' (or) is interpreted to distribute the four penalties to four categories of crime, not to give the imam a choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what exactly is being ruled upon. Here, it is the act of intending highway robbery with intimidation (ikhafah) but without taking property or killing. The ruling is imprisonment until repentance.
This is a ta'zir punishment, not a hadd, because the full crime was not completed.
2
Locate the primary proof
The author cites Quran 5:33 as the basis. The key phrase is 'aw yunfaw min al-ard' (or banished from the land). Hanafis interpret 'banishment' as imprisonment.
The Quranic text is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala regarding the interpretation.
3
Analyze the linguistic dispute
The word 'aw' is debated. Hanafis say it indicates distribution (tawzi') – each penalty corresponds to a specific crime. Malik says it indicates choice (takhyeer) – the imam may apply any penalty to any crime. The passage argues that Maliki's interpretation would allow the lightest penalty for the worst crime, which is irrational.
The hadith of Jibril's descent is cited to support the Hanafi position.
4
Examine the hadith support
The hadith (though not in full chain here) specifies that whoever kills and takes wealth is crucified; whoever kills but does not take wealth is executed; whoever takes wealth but does not kill has hand and foot amputated; whoever only terrorizes (ikhafah) is imprisoned.
This hadith clearly distributes penalties, confirming the Hanafi view.
5
Note other schools' positions
Shafi'i: banishment or imprisonment for a fixed term (6 months or 1 year). Ahmad: hounding the criminal away. The Hanafi position is imprisonment until repentance, which is indefinite but ends upon repentance.
The practical difference: duration of imprisonment and whether it is hadd or ta'zir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبله أي قبل قطع الطريق بأن لم يوجد عنه سوى الإخافة. حبس حتى يتوب: أي إذا خرج جماعة ممتنعين أو واحد يقدر على الامتناع، وقصدوا قطع الطريق، فأخذوا قبل أن يقطعوا الطريق، وقبل أن يأخذوا مالاً، ويقتلوا نفساً حبسهم الإمام حتى يتوبوا، والأصل فيه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّمَا جَزَاءُ الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ وَيَسْعَوْنَ فِي الْأَرْضِ فَسَاداً أَنْ يُقَتَلُوا أَوْ يُصَلَّبُوا أَوْ تُقَطَّعَ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَأَرْجُلُهُمْ مِنْ خِلافٍ أَوْ يُنْفَوْا مِنَ الْأَرْضِ (المائدة:٣٣) والمراد من النفي الحبس؛ لأن الحبس نفي لهم، ودفع لفسادهم، والمقصود من الآية توزيع أنواع الأجزية على أنواع الجنايات، وقال مالك: كلمة "أو" في الآية للتخيير، فالإمام مخير أي شيء شاء من هذه الأجزية فعل بكل واحد من الجناية. وهذا يقتضي أن يترتب على أغلظ الجنايات أخف الأجزية، وهذا مما تدفعه قواعد الشرع والعقل، فوجب القول الأغلظ للأغلظ والأخف للأخف؛ إذ ليس من الحكمة أن يسوي في العقوبة مع التفاوت في الجناية، كيف! وقد روي حين قطع أصحاب أبي بردة الطريق على من جاء يريد الإسلام أن جبريل نزل بالحد على هذا التقسيم: من قتل وأخذ المال صلب ومن قتل ولم يأخذ المال قتل، ومن أخذ المال ولم يقتل قطعت يده ورجله من خلاف، ومن أخاف الطريق ولم يأخذ المال حبس، وعند الشافعي ينفى من البلد، وعنه يحبس ستة أشهر، وعنه يحبس سنة، وعند أحمد: يشرّد ولا يترك في بلد يأوي إليه، ثم أحوال قطاع الطريق أربعة: الحالة الأولى: القصد لقطع الطريق، أي الإغارة للمارة فالحكم فيها الحبس والحالات الثلاثة الباقية مذكورة في المتن.
Before it, i.e., before cutting the road, such that only intimidation is found from him. He is imprisoned until he repents: that is, if a group of resisters or a single person capable of resistance goes out and intends to cut the road, and they are caught before they cut the road and before they take property or kill a soul, the Imam imprisons them until they repent. The basis for this is the statement of Allah Most High: 'Indeed, the recompense of those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger and strive in the land causing corruption is that they be killed or crucified or their hands and feet be cut off from opposite sides or they be banished from the land' (al-Ma'idah 5:33). And the meaning of banishment is imprisonment, because imprisonment is banishment for them and repels their corruption. The intended meaning of the verse is distribution of the types of punishments to the types of crimes. Malik said: the word 'or' in the verse is for choice, so the Imam may choose any of these punishments and apply to each criminal. This would require that the most severe crime could receive the lightest punishment, which is rejected by the rules of Shari'a and reason. So it is necessary to say the severest for the severest and the lightest for the lightest, for it is not wisdom to equalize punishments when crimes differ. Indeed! It has been narrated when the companions of Abu Barda cut the road against someone who came wanting to embrace Islam that Jibril descended with the hadd upon this division: whoever killed and took wealth is crucified, whoever killed and did not take wealth is killed, whoever took wealth and did not kill is cut (hand and foot from opposite sides), whoever terrorized the road and did not take wealth is imprisoned. According to al-Shafi'i, he is banished from the town, and from him (another narration) he is imprisoned for six months, and from him one year. According to Ahmad, he is hounded and not left in any town he might take refuge in. Then the states of highway robbers are four: the first state is the intention to cut the road, i.e., raiding the passers-by, so the ruling in it is imprisonment, and the remaining three states are mentioned in the text.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage details the punishment for those who intend highway robbery but are caught before any act of taking property or killing. The Imam must imprison them until they show signs of repentance. The proof is Quran 5:33, where 'banishment' is interpreted by Hanafis as imprisonment (since imprisonment banishes them from society and prevents harm). The verse lists four penalties: execution, crucifixion, amputation of opposite hand and foot, and banishment. Imam Malik took 'aw' (or) as giving the Imam a choice to apply any penalty to any crime. The Hanafi response is that this would allow the least punishment for the worst crime, which contradicts wisdom and revealed specific divisions. A hadith (Jibril's descent) confirms that each penalty corresponds to a specific crime: killing+theft → crucifixion; killing only → execution; theft only → amputation; intimidation only → imprisonment. The four states of highway robbers are then classified: (1) mere intention to terrorize — imprisonment; the other three states are elaborated later. The passage also notes Shafi'i's view (banishment or imprisonment for 6 months to a year) and Ahmad's view (hounding the criminal away from any refuge).

Key Terms
الإخافة (al-ikhafah): Causing fear or intimidation; the minimum act that triggers the ruling of highway robbery when no property is taken.
النفي (al-nafy): Banishment from the land; interpreted by Hanafis as imprisonment.
التوزيع (al-tawzi'): Distribution; the Hanafi interpretation that the word 'aw' in Quran 5:33 distributes the four penalties to four specific crimes, not giving a choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi interpretation of the word 'aw' in Quran 5:33 regarding the penalties for highway robbers?
A) It gives the Imam a choice to apply any penalty.
B) It distributes each penalty to a specific type of crime.
C) It indicates that only one penalty may be applied to all.
D) It means the penalties are optional.

Key Takeaway
Mere intention of highway robbery without act is punished by imprisonment until repentance; Quran 5:33 distributes penalties, not chooses.

Highway Robbery: Amputation for Taking Protected Wealth
Ruling on second state of highway robbery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a highway robber who takes protected wealth without killing and identify the condition of wealth being ma'sum.

Core Idea
Taking protected wealth in highway robbery triggers amputation of right hand and left foot from opposite sides.

Highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) is a grave crime with graded punishments. This lesson covers the second state: when the robber takes wealth but does not kill. The key condition is that the wealth must be 'protected' (ma'sum), meaning it belongs to a Muslim or dhimmi. The punishment is a specific form of amputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The act is highway robbery where the robber takes wealth (mal) but does not commit murder. This is the second of the three states mentioned in the Quranic verse (al-Ma'idah 5:33).
2
Determine the condition of the wealth
The wealth must be 'mal ma'sum', meaning it belongs to a Muslim or a dhimmi (permanent protected status). Wealth of a musta'min (temporary safe conduct) is not ma'sum; taking it does not incur the hadd.
This distinguishes highway robbery from ordinary theft, where musta'min's wealth may be protected under certain conditions.
3
State the punishment
The prescribed hadd is amputation of the right hand and left foot from opposite sides (qat' min khilaf). This is the specific combination for this offense.
This is distinct from ordinary theft, where only the right hand is amputated.
4
Compare with the Maliki position
The passage notes that Imam Malik holds that any amount of stolen wealth triggers amputation, whereas the Hanafi school requires a nisab (minimum value, analogous to 10 dirhams for ordinary theft) for the hadd to apply.
This difference affects the application in cases where the value taken is less than the nisab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أخذ مالاً معصوماً بأن كان المسلم أو ذمي واحترز به عن مال المستأمن فإنه غير معصوم. أي عصمة مؤبدة حتى لو أخذ مال المستأمن لا يجب القطع، وهي الحالة الثانية المذكورة في الآية، وهي أن يؤخذ قطع يده ورجله من خلاف، أي يده اليمنى ورجله اليسرى. وعند مالك: أي مقدار سرق قطع.
And if he takes protected wealth, meaning that (the victim) is a Muslim or a dhimmi, and he is cautious about the wealth of a musta'min (protected non-Muslim by safe conduct) because it is not protected. That is, permanent inviolability, so even if he takes the wealth of a musta'min, amputation is not obligatory. And this is the second state mentioned in the verse, and it is that he is taken and his hand and foot are cut from opposite sides, i.e., his right hand and his left foot. And according to Malik, whatever amount he steals, he is cut.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage covers the second state of highway robbery: the robber takes protected wealth (mal ma'sum) without killing anyone. Protected wealth belongs to Muslims and dhimmis (permanent inviolability), not to musta'min (those under temporary safe conduct). The punishment is amputation of the right hand and left foot from opposite sides. This is the penalty indicated in the Quranic verse for those who take wealth but do not kill. The text notes a dispute with Malik, who holds that any amount stolen triggers amputation, whereas in Hanafi law (for ordinary theft) there is a nisab threshold (10 dirhams). Although not elaborated here, the Hanafi position requires a minimum value for cutting in highway robbery as well (referred to in the commentary of the text).

Key Terms
مال معصوم (mal ma'sum): Wealth under permanent protection (of Muslim or dhimmi); theft of which incurs hadd.
قطع من خلاف (qat' min khilaf): Cutting from opposite sides: the right hand and left foot.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for the wealth taken in highway robbery to be considered 'protected' (ma'sum)?
A) The victim must be a Muslim
B) The victim must be either a Muslim or a dhimmi
C) The wealth must be worth at least 10 dirhams
D) The robber must not kill the victim

Key Takeaway
A highway robber who takes protected wealth (from a Muslim or dhimmi) without killing faces amputation of the right hand and left foot from opposite sides.
