Execution as Hadd for Killing Without Taking Wealth
Ruling that a highway robber who kills without taking wealth is executed as a hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a highway robber who kills without taking wealth, explain why it is hadd rather than qisas, and apply the rule to scenarios involving pardon.

Core Idea
When a highway robber kills but takes no wealth, execution is a hadd — no pardon by heirs or Imam.

Highway robbery (qat' al-tariq) is a severe crime that can involve killing, taking wealth, or both. The Hanafi school classifies the punishment based on the act's nature. In this passage, we examine the third scenario: the robber kills a protected person but does not take any property. The ruling is that he is executed as a hadd (fixed right of Allah), not as qisas (private right). This distinction has major practical implications: the victim's family cannot pardon the execution, and even the Imam cannot forgive the punishment once it reaches him. Understanding this ruling requires seeing the reasoning behind the Hanafi position and how it differs from the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third state of highway robbery
The passage describes a highway robber who kills a protected soul but does not take wealth. Recognize this as the third state (after taking wealth without killing, and killing with wealth).
The first two states are mentioned earlier in the text: taking wealth alone leads to amputation of the hand and foot; killing and taking wealth leads to execution and crucifixion.
2
Note the ruling: execution as hadd, not qisas
The text states: 'he is killed as a hadd even if the guardian pardons.' Understand that the execution is a fixed penalty of Allah, overriding the private right of qisas.
Hadd means the punishment is obligatory and cannot be waived by individuals.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafi argument uses qiyas (analogy): since the penalty for taking wealth (amputation) is a hadd, the penalty for killing (execution) should also be a hadd because both are parts of the same crime. Additionally, the verse calls the punishment 'jaza'' (recompense), which is a right of Allah.
The hadith against pardoning hudud after they reach the Imam is also cited to show no pardon.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the killing should be qisas because it is killing in return for killing. Identify the practical difference: Hanafi - execution mandatory even if heirs forgive; Shafi'i - heirs may pardon.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the concrete consequence of the dispute.
5
Apply the condition for the ruling
The passage adds a condition: the killing must be such that it would obligate qisas if considered separately, i.e., direct perpetration by the killer. Not all participants may be executed unless they directly caused death.
This condition ensures that only those directly responsible face the hadd penalty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قتل قتل حداً وإن عفا الولي. أي إن قتل قاطع الطريق نفساً محرمة ولم يأخذ المال، وهي الحالة الثالثة فحكمها أن القاتل يقتل حداً أي من حيث كونه حقاً الله تعالى وإن عفا الولي، أي ولي المقتول وهو واصل بما قبله أي ولو عفا الأولياء لم يلتفت إلى عفوهم، وشرطه: أن يكون القتل موجباً للقصاص من مباشرة الكل، وقال الشافعي: الواجب قصاص؛ لأنه قتل بإزاء قتل، قلنا: القطع حق الله تعالى فكذا القتل؛ لأنه قسيمه وتسميته جزاء يشعر بذلك؛ لأنه اسم لما يجب الله تعالى، ولا أثر لعفو العبد في الله تعالى. وليس للإمام أيضاً أن يعفو؛ لقوله عليه: "تعافوا الحدود فيما بينكم فإذا رفعت إلى الإمام فلا عفا الله عنه إن عفا."
And if he kills, he is killed as a hadd even if the guardian (wali) pardons. That is, if a highway robber kills a protected soul and did not take wealth, this is the third state, and its ruling is that the killer is killed as a hadd, meaning as a right of Allah the Exalted, even if the guardian (the guardian of the slain, and it connects to what preceded, i.e., even if the guardians pardon, their pardon is not heeded). Its condition is that the killing be such as to obligate qisas by direct perpetration by all. Al-Shafi'i said: the obligation is qisas because it is killing in return for killing. We said: amputation is a right of Allah, and similarly killing (as hadd) because it is its counterpart, and its being called 'recompense' (jaza') indicates that, because it is a name for what is obligatory by Allah. And there is no effect of the slave's pardon in the rights of Allah. Also, the Imam does not have the right to pardon, because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'Forgive the hudud among yourselves, but if they reach the Imam, then may Allah not forgive him if he forgives.'
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage addresses the third state: the robber kills a protected person but does not take wealth. The punishment is execution as a hadd (fixed right of Allah), not as qisas (private right of the victim's family). Therefore, even if the victim's heirs forgive, the execution is not waived. The Hanafi reasoning is that since the penalty for taking wealth (amputation) is a hadd, its counterpart for taking life (execution) should also be a hadd. The verse calls it 'jaza' (recompense), which is something due to Allah. Al-Shafi'i argued that it should be qisas because it is killing for killing. The Hanafi response distinguishes: in highway robbery, the killing is part of a larger crime against public order, so it becomes a hadd. The condition is that the killing must be direct (mubashara) by all participants. The Imam also cannot pardon because the hadd, once reported to the Imam, must be carried out; the hadith warns against pardoning hudud after they reach the Imam.

Key Terms
حداً (haddan): A fixed punishment prescribed by Allah as a right of Allah, not subject to pardon.
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation, a private right of the victim's family that can be forgiven.
جزاء (jaza'): Recompense, referring to the penalty for highway robbery which is a right of Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a highway robber who kills without taking wealth executed as a hadd rather than qisas?
A) Because the killer is not the direct perpetrator
B) Because the crime is against public order and the penalty is paired with amputation
C) Because the victim's family always refuses to forgive
D) Because the Quran mandates execution for any murder

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a highway robber who kills without taking wealth is executed as a hadd, and neither the victim's heirs nor the Imam can pardon the execution.

The Imam's Discretion in Punishing Highway Robbery: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Options
Details the Imam's choice of punishments when a highway robber both kills and takes wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi differences on the Imam's punishment choices when a highway robber kills and takes wealth, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Imam has broad discretion in punishing the gravest highway robbery, but Sahibayn restrict it to execution or crucifixion.

The most severe form of hirabah combines murder and theft. The Quran (5:33) gives multiple penalty options, but the Hanafi school internally debates whether the Imam may also amputate the thief's hand. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents Abu Hanifa's expansive view alongside the restrictions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, showing how a single hadd crime can yield multiple valid interpretations. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi fiqh with nuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criminal state
Recognize that the fourth and worst state of highway robbery is when the robber both kills a person and takes protected wealth. This triggers the most severe penalties.
State 4: killing + taking wealth.
2
Understand the Hanafi consensus on Imam's choice
All Hanafi imams agree the Imam has a choice among prescribed punishments, but they disagree on the set of options. Abu Hanifa allows up to six permutations (cutting, killing, crucifixion in various combinations).
Abu Hanifa's view is the most expansive.
3
Contrast Abu Hanifa with Sahibayn
Imam Muhammad and the famous view of Abu Yusuf restrict the options to execution or crucifixion, excluding amputation entirely. This is a significant intra-madhhab difference.
Sahibayn: only execution or crucifixion.
4
Note Abu Yusuf's variant narration
Another narration from Abu Yusuf holds that crucifixion is the sole penalty, regardless of prior cutting. This shows even within one imam's views there can be different transmissions.
Abu Yusuf (variant): unconditional crucifixion.
5
Relate to broader principles
The dispute illustrates the principle of takhyeer al-imam (Imam's discretion) and how textual interpretation (Quran 5:33 vs. hadith) leads to different rulings.
Central to Hanafi hudud jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قتل وأخذ أي إن قتل قاطع الطريق نفساً وأخذ مالاً معصوماً، وهي الحالة الرابعة، وحكمها ما ذكره بقوله: قطع وقتل وصلب إلخ، أي إن الإمام مخير بين ثلاثة أحوال الأولى أن يجمع بين القطع والقتل والصلب، والثانية: القتل فقط والثالثة: الصلب من غير قطع وقال الزيلعي: الإمام مخير بين ستة أحوال: إن شاء قطع من خلاف ثم قتل، أو قطع ثم صلب، أو فعل الثلاثة، أو قتل وصلب، أو قتل فقط، أو صلب فقط، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وبه قال زفر، وعند محمد: يقتل أو يصلب ولا يقطع، وأبو يوسف معه في المشهور، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يصلب مطلقاً يعني قبل القطع وبعده، وعند الشافعي وأحمد: يصلب فقط ولا يقطع، وعند مالك: إن كان جلداً يصلب، وإلا فلا.
For if he kills and takes (wealth): i.e., if a highway robber kills a soul and takes protected wealth, this is the fourth state, and its ruling is what he mentioned in his statement: 'cutting, killing, and crucifixion, etc.' That is, the Imam has a choice between three states: first, to combine cutting, killing, and crucifixion; second, killing only; third, crucifixion without cutting. And al-Zayla'i said: the Imam has a choice between six states: if he wishes, he cuts from opposite sides then kills; or cuts then crucifies; or does all three; or kills and crucifies; or kills only; or crucifies only. This is according to Abu Hanifa, and Zufar said the same. According to Muhammad: he kills or crucifies, but does not cut. Abu Yusuf agrees with him in the famous (narration), and from Abu Yusuf that he crucifies absolutely, meaning before cutting and after it. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: he crucifies only and does not cut. According to Malik: if he is a (free) adult, he is crucified; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage covers the fourth and worst state: the highway robber both kills and takes property. The Qur'anic penalty is execution, crucifixion, or amputation (implied). The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa, gives the Imam broad discretion: he may combine amputation, execution, and crucifixion; or choose only execution; or only crucifixion. Al-Zayla'i expands this to six permutations. Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) and Abu Yusuf (in the famous view) limit it to execution or crucifixion, without amputation. Another narration from Abu Yusuf allows crucifixion unconditionally. The non-Hanafi schools differ: Shafi'i and Ahmad prescribe only crucifixion (without prior amputation), while Malik ties crucifixion to the robber being a free adult (jald). Thus, within the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi opinion is likely the one that gives the Imam maximum flexibility, but there is significant internal ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
تخيير الإمام (takhyeer al-imam): The discretion given to the Imam in choosing among multiple prescribed punishments for the most severe highway robbery.
صلب (salb): Crucifixion; the display of the executed body, or living crucifixion depending on opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how many basic options does the Imam have for punishing a highway robber who both killed and took wealth?
A) One: crucifixion only
B) Three: combining cutting, killing, and crucifixion or subsets
C) Two: execution or crucifixion
D) Six: all combinations of cutting, killing, and crucifixion

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives the Imam significant discretion in punishing the worst highway robbery, but the Sahibayn restrict it to execution or crucifixion, reflecting a tension between textual literalism and juridical flexibility.

Manner of Crucifixion for Highway Robbers in Hanafi Fiqh
Describes Hanafi method of crucifixion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the manner of crucifixion, including the duration and the dispute with al-Tahawi and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The relied-upon Hanafi view: crucify alive for three days to maximize deterrence.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq details the practical implementation of crucifixion (salb) as a hadd punishment. It explains the physical method, the duration (three days), and addresses an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute regarding whether the robber should be crucified alive or after execution. Understanding this ruling shows how the Hanafi school balances the purposes of punishment—deterrence and restraint—with the prohibition of mutilation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Crucifixion (salb) is part of the hadd for hirabah. The passage states the most correct view (al-ah) is to crucify the robber alive.
2
Understand the physical method
A wooden post is fixed in the ground, a crossbeam is tied, the convict's feet are placed on it, and his hands are tied to another beam above. The belly is stabbed with a spear (in the left side or jugular) to hasten death.
3
Note the duration
The body is left on the cross for three days after death, then given to the family for burial.
Abu Yusuf differed, saying leave until the body disintegrates.
4
Analyze the dispute on live crucifixion
Al-Tahawi and al-Shafi'i argue for execution first to avoid muthla (mutilation). The Hanafi response is that crucifixion is a prescribed punishment, not mutilation.
The text declares live crucifixion as 'al-ah' (most correct).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصلب حياً: أي حال كونه حيا هو الأصح. وكيفية الصلب أن يغرز خشبة في الأرض ثم يربط عليها خشبة أخرى عرضاً فيضع قدميه عليها، ويربط من أعلاها خشبة أخرى ويربط عليها يديه، أي يصلب حال كونه حياً؛ لأن هذا أبلغ في الروع والاعتبار وهو المقصود، وعن الطحاوي أنه يقتل ثم يصلب، ولا يصلب حياً توقياً عن المثلة؛ لأنه عليه السلام نهى عن المثلة، ولو بالكلب العقور، وهو قول الشافعي. ثلاثة أيام أي يترك ثلاثة أيام من موته ثم خلي بينه وبين أهله ليدفنوه. ويبعج بطنه من البعج بمعنى الشق، يقال: بعج الأرض شقها والمراد منه يطعن مجازاً بقرينة قوله: برمح أي يطعن بالرمح في ثديه الأيسر، ويخضخض أي يحرك إلى أن يموت، وقيل: يطعن في لبته حتى يموت ولا يترك أكثر من ثلاثة أيام؛ لأنه يتغير، ويؤذي به الناس، وهو الأصح عند الشافعي، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يترك حتى ينقطع ويسقط؛ لأنه أبلغ في الأرداع.
And he is crucified alive: i.e., the most correct (view) is that he be (crucified) while alive. And the manner of crucifixion is that a wooden post is fixed in the ground, then another wooden beam is tied across it, and he places his feet on it, and from above another beam is tied and his hands are tied to it, i.e., he is crucified while alive, because this is more effective in producing dread and reflection, which is the purpose. From al-Tahawi: he is killed then crucified, and not crucified alive, out of caution against mutilation (muthla), because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade mutilation, even with a mad dog. This is also the view of al-Shafi'i. (He is left) three days: i.e., he is left for three days after his death, then he is given to his family to bury him. And his belly is slit (yubba'ju) from al-ba'j meaning splitting, it is said: ba'aja al-ard (he split the ground), and by metaphor it means to stab, by context of his saying: 'with a spear' i.e., he is stabbed with a spear in his left side, and it is stirred until he dies. And it is said: he is stabbed in his jugular vein until he dies, and he is not left for more than three days because he changes and causes harm to people. This is the most correct (view) according to al-Shafi'i. And from Abu Yusuf: he is left until (the body) breaks apart and falls, because that is more effective in deterrence.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage details the practical implementation of crucifixion (salb) as a punishment. The relied-upon Hanafi view (al-ah) is that the robber is crucified alive: this means he is tied to a cross-like structure while alive, then left for three days. After three days, he is given to his family for burial. The belly is also stabbed with a spear (some say in the left side, others in the jugular) to ensure death. This is considered more frightening and deterrent. Al-Tahawi and al-Shafi'i differ, arguing that crucifixion should be after execution to avoid the mutilation of a living person, citing the Prophet's prohibition of muthla. The text also notes an alternative from Abu Yusuf that the body should be left until it disintegrates naturally for maximum deterrence. The Hanafi school thus balances deterrence and mercy, with the majority favoring living crucifixion for three days.

Key Terms
الصلب (al-salb): Crucifixion: tying the convict to a cross-like structure for display.
المثلة (al-muthla): Mutilation of a corpse or living being after death; forbidden in general.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, how long is a highway robber left on the cross after crucifixion?
A) Until the body disintegrates naturally
B) Three days
C) One day only
D) It is not left after death

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the robber is crucified alive for three days for maximum deterrence, despite the dispute.
